The Red Page
A Sonnet-Sequence of the
Year —in Australese
“Sr.” : May I chip in about the “Red Page” sonnets
—the finest parcel of poetry I’ve ever read-—and
“John Drayman’s” remarks thereon (“Red Page,”
5/3/’l4) as to actual Winter Dread that exists in
Tassy. “John” must have got his information on a
fortnightly tourist trip from an old cabman. I live
here, have done so for the last five years after living
in Victoria. I am no poet, so perhaps don’t possess
dreads, but if “John” looks up any meteorological
authorities he will find that Hobart’s winter weather
is no more rigorous than Melbourne’s. For the last
two years there has not been one football match post-
poned on account of bad or wet weather in the
southern capital. Hobart never sees snow in the
streets. Launceston has fogs; but for the winter
there being more dreaded than in Sydney or Mel-
bourne, well, it is not. I repeatedly have to visit
Melbourne in the winter, and I always want to get
home again to dodge the winter there.
The Expansion of Sydney, comparative
photographs and drawings indicating the
congestion policy of Sydney since 1840. with
an introduction by C. H. Bertie, is published
by J. li. Tyrell, Sydney.
JANUARY.
If I cud set me peepers on the coot
Thet wants a sonn'it on this weather writ
I’d put it to ’im: “ ’Ow’d he fancy it
Fer yakkerin’ on a thresher?” ’Twould be
beaut
Ter ’ave the cow we’re he mus’ foller suit
En ’eave the straw —he’d ’ave ter .do ’is bit
Er else git swaller’d in a smotherin’ pit
As hot as ’ell is at the flamin’ root.
Thet’s me this month; I’m racin’ in the heat,
In dust en rust en’ sweat near choked en’
blind,
W’ile bags er gettin’ bulged wi’ golden wheat,
En’ tossin’ empty straw in tons behind.
If there was January sonnits neat
This ’ell’s machine ’ud thresh ’em frum the
mind.
MICK MUCH.
FEBRUARY.
The pick en’ shovel ain’t so soft a toil!
I done me dash en’ ’ere I’m wiv a camp
Thet lays the plates through spinifex an’
swamp,
W’ere blokes ’ave guyver’d ’bout the lovely
soil,
W’ile all the breathin’ sky’s jist on the boil.
It ain’t alright, en’, ’struth! some’s dead to
tramp
The payday follerin’ w’en' there’s bin some
damp,
So’s coots kin tell all liquid isn’t oil.
The seaside caper en’ the surf en’ sand,
W’ere tarts chuck off their duds to git the
sun,
Is right per’aps, but thet game wuzn’t
plann’d
Fer eny man escept a bloomin’ gun.
This job’s the start thet’s got to ring the
land —-
It’s solid, but, my oath, it’s somethun’ done.
MICK MUCH.
MARCH.
’Ere goes ter give a wallop iv me time,.
Ter sling Miss March er little bit er kid.
En’ ’strath! I’m glad ter lif’ me bloomin’
lid
To ’er, en’ praise ’er up in sorter rime.
Fer square ’n’ all, she’s jist the little tart
As knows wot’s wanted fer me w’eat ’n’
wool.
Up goes me tile! Me bloomin’ tanks is full,
En’ lambin’ grass ’s got er bonza start.
I’m full er joy when ole Feb. does a get.
Fer strike me ’e’s a dry ole wowser bloke.
A bummer too, ’e mops up all me wet,
En’ leaves me cartin’ jooce fer me ole moke.
So I’m some glad to dook Miss March, you
bet!
Fer don’t she bring a million tons er soak?
COCKY JIM.
APRIL.
It’s blokes like me as gives th’ real glad eye,
Ter smilin’ little April w’en she ’ops
Along ter see ’ow goes it with me crops,
En’ turns th’ tap on if they’re lookin’ dry.
She’s sweet, too, at th’ tap-trick on th’ sly.
Ter leave me coat she kids me, then she flops
Th’ fluid down in great big shiny drops,
En’ coolly smiles en’ winks th’ other eye.
Th’ missis, too, is glad as glad kin be,
Fer sunny April ’s coaxed ’er ’ens ter lay,
En’ all ’er plants is up ’n’ lookin’ well.
She sez the’ll soon be greens fer ’er ’n’ me,
En’ if th’ bleedin’ grabs ’ll keep away,
She ’opes ter raise er root er two ter sell.
COCKY JIM.
MAY.
I ain’t a nark, but “flowers o’ May’s” a gag
Thet makes me do me block or chuck me
feed.
Them blokes wot writes the stuff fer mugs to
read
Mus’ alluz live upon the flamin’ jag.
'‘The merry month” ’s another bit o’ mag!
W’y, if yer follored to the written lead,
Yer’d kid that paddicks shone wif flowers
en’ seed—
Until yer’d git yer head shoved in a bag.
There’s grass now ’bove the groun’ en’ inch
er so,
But soft en’ pappy fer the grazin’ stock;
Ez fer the flowers, there’s on’y green to
show
W’ere every nag’s ez bony az a crock.
Erbout the" merry month thet gits the blow,
There’s frost en’ rain thet fairy tale to
knock.
MICK MUCH.
JUNE.
Fair dinkum go, ole June’s th’ plowman’s
nark!
Ter quit yer warm ole bunk afore it’s light,
’En ’op inter a world thet’s frozen w’ite,
Don’t make a feller apt ter kum th’ lark.
’En w’en yer ’unt for neddies in th’ dark,
’En flop inter a puddle, don’t yer bite !
’En wish old Nick ’u’d kum erlong ’n’ smite
Th’ job ’n’ plow ’n’ ’orses stiff: ’n’ stark!
But still at times, ter giv’ ole June ’is due,
’E ain’t all frost ’n’ slush ’n’ rain ’n’ sleet.
The’s days w’en Solly slides ’is smilin’ way,
Across er sky thet’s double boshter blue,
’En all th’ birds is tunin’ up er treet,
Ez though it wuz er glad September day.
W. E.
JULY.
Jooly’s orlright, the days are jist the thing
Fur solid yakker till yer day is done;
Uz fur the nights, there’s allez whips o’ fun
In ’ops wif righto heifers ut yer wing.
Then sittin’ tight w’en darncin’s ud its flmg,
En’ kiddin’ to yerself yer jist the one—
Until some screwin’, crook son uv a gun
The cliner grabs, en’ show yer’ve ’ad a string.
The ’Urdles now, en’ Steeple’s all the go
Ter give the blokes a charnce ter do their
dough,
Then fetch a skite their forchin jist got
pipped!
It ain’t too dusty livenin’ aroun’
These early morns wif Jack Frost on the
groun’,
Uz if the moonlight froze w’ere it wuz
tipped.
MICK MUCH.
AUGUST.
I’m glad I didn’t giv’ ther struggle best
W’en ole July fair socked it onter me,
Fer, ’strath, it’s good ter be erlive en’ see
Young August dance on sour ole Winter’s
chest.
Th’ pairin’ birds er busy wiv their nest,
’En ropes er flowers erwait th’ busy bee.
Th’ ole gohanper’s crawlin’ frum ’is tree,
’E’s fine ez wire frum ’is long winter rest.
On sunny Sundays ain’t it jes’ th’ thing
Ter lay out on yer back in long green grass,
’En listen ter yer nippers larkin’ gay.
W’ile all eround yer matin’ birdies sing,
’En fer up in ther blue tli’ cloudlets pass.
Oh! the glory uv ’n Austral August day.
P. PHROZEOUT.
SEPTEMBER.
It’s now the snide strawdecker leaves the
box,
The flynet comes to light upon the track,
En’ tarts wear lids wif veils thet flip en’
crack,
More lacey, too, they show erbout the socks;
The bullicks git noo speargrass in their
’ocks, ,
The foal’s canoodlin’ roun’ the nudgin’ ’ack,
Too flamin’ soon we’re roused be Kooky
Jack,
En’ Mag does overtime in warblin’ flocks.
Like cows’ breath in the lucerne blows the
Spring
Frum rises jest like stage scenes set in gold,
Uz through the bush the chorus songbirds
sing.
Fair dink! There’s somethun’ ’ints fur
more than’s told!
Ter git yer goin’ till the ’at yer’d fling,
En’ do yer block uz in the grass yer rolled.
MICK MUCH.
OCTOBER.
October’s sun jis’ makes me wish ter lay
Flat on me back beneath sum shady tree,
Wif sum nice tart ter shoo th’ flies
En ’unt ther bleedin’ meat-ants well erway.
Thet’s wot I’d like inste’d er forkin’ ’ay,
Er sweatin’ over wool fer £ s. d.
It’s crook, I say, thet enny bloke sh’u’d be
Kumpelled ter spoil er bonza mid-spring day.
The poit. blokes now git ther rimin’ rat,
’En rave erbout ther wavin’ fields er w’eat,
En’ tricklin’ streams, en’ s’rubs, en’ flow’rin’
trees.
Ez fer meself, I ain’t got time fer that,
I’d rutlier do me spare time on me beat,
A-screwin’ fer er tart thet wants er, squeeze.
W. E.
NOVEMBER.
Take it frum me, the flamin’ time’s not. up
Ter wot it’s cracked ter be; I feel dead crook
With summer blazin’ on me since I took
Me trip among the spielers at the Cup.
The flies ’a’ got me bunged, blind as a pup
One side, yet I must pllus ’ave a look
Fer fear the mutton ’s movin’ on the hook,
An’ screw the drink before I take a sup.
Them' blokes wot skite o’ summertime en’
spring
They oughter do some yakker—ain’t it dirt
Thet dust en’ hotwind blaze should make ’em
sing?
The way the days ’ang out! —me bit o’ skirt
Won’t smooge till dark, no, not fer any-
thing ! —•
Me socks ’ave burrs—there’s speargrass in
me shirt!
MICK MUCH.
DECEMVER.
I’ll bet ’e was a ’oary-’eaded jay
With gospel w’iskers fuzzed frum ear to ear
Wot made December end the flamin’ year,
Jest w’en the year’s awake ’n’ feelin’ gay,
’N’ raisin’ dust, ’n’ shooin’ grass away,
’N’ suckin’ rivers dry, ’n’ knockin’ fear
In cockies’ hearts; fer ’im, becripes, a bier
Wot ’as ’is jag, nor don’t arst ef ’e may!
December’s like a bloke wot’s jes’ grew up,
’N’ feelin’ strong ’n’ rorty, wild fer fun;
’E makes the pace too ’ot ’n’ does ’is block,
Then takes a pull; the wowser says, “ ’ls
cup
Er joy is spilt, ’is wickid course is run”—
But ’im, ’e knows there’s wild days still in
stock.
LAST HOPE.
More War
A U.S. publicist, J. William White pre-
sents America's Arraignment of Germany
(Unwin; Is.). Of course, there is no United
States—the war has at least done the world
the service of making it clear that the melting
pot has hot managed to melt anything except
itself. The Disunited States consists of a
majority of Yankees and various minorities
of Germans, negroes, prize-fighters and sun-
dries. That is why Wilson remains polite
but nervous. A declaration of alliance with
the Allies would mean civil war; and the
Disunited States are not yet sufficiently Ger-
manised to plump for the Entente. Obviously
the Ununited States favors the Allies; the
author states that with the exception of the
“German-Americans,” on the publication of
England’s White Book, the country ranged
itself spontaneously and with practical unani-
mity on the side of the Allies. He proceeds
to the categorical arraignment of the Hun
on the question and answer method, utilising
fully documentary evidence from both sides.
He quotes, for instance, the conviction of a
retired German Major-General, that “The
ugliest stone placed to mark the burial of a
German grenadier is a more glorious monu-
ment than all the cathedrals Europe put to-
gether.” And he aptly recalls the fact that
Belgian neutrality was solemnly affirmed by
the leaders of Germany in 1913, and even on
July 31, 1914, by the German Minister at
Brussels. And with reference to Germany’s
arrogant claim for her Ivultur, he reminds us
that: “The Germans contributed little or
nothing to the development of the railroad,
the steamboat, the automobile, the aeroplane,
the telegraph, the telephone, the phono-
graph, the photograph, the moving pic-
ture, the electric light, the sewing
machine, and the reaper and binder.
Even those dread instruments of war,
the revolver and the machine gun, the
turreted ship, the torpedo, and the submarine,
are not due to the military ardor of the
Germans. It would seem as though the Ger-
mans had been lacking in the inventiveness
which is so marked a feature of our modern
civilisation.”
As to the duties of the Disunited States,
the author points out that that country was
a joint signer with the Kaiser of the Hague
Conferences of 1899 and 1907, among' the sec-
tions of which was a recitation of practices
which the signers solemnly undertook to ab-
stain from in war. Expressly prohibited were
pillage, the levying of illegal contributions',
the seizure of funds belonging to private per-
sons or local authorities, the destruction of
buildings dedicated to public worship, art,
science or charitable purposes, the killing of
wounded, etc. And he asks whether a nation
can afford to put its name to a document
and then stand by in icy neutrality while
that document is being torn to~ shreds by
another of the high contracting parties? The
answer is, obviously, that the Neutrited States
is not a nation. The melting pot is merely a
garbage tin. J. William White, in the name
of the majority opinion of the best elements
in the garbage tin, calls strenuously for some
form of intervention on behalf of the Allies,
and adds: “If our intervention brought vic-
tory to thb cause of the Allies a month
earlier than it would otherwise come, it would
be justified.” He has reason for believing
that “before long” it will be felt to be the
duty of the President to show the Kaiser that
the sympathies, and, if necessary, the support
of the United Hesitates are pledged to the
cause of the Allies.
G. K. Chesterton decorates the war with
apt alliteration and pretty paradox. The
Barbarism of Berlin (Cassell; 6d.) is Ches-
tertonically characteristic. Pale paradoxical
pills for pink patriots.

An English governess employed prior to
the war in the household of one of the Super-
Huns has several interesting facts to relate
in her story, What I Found Out in the House
of a German Prince (Chapman and Hall, per
Dymock’s Library). Her charges, the prince-
lings, boys of five and six, used to play a
war game invented by Zeppelin, in which toy
zeppelins were manoeuvred over large models
of London and Petrograd and flour bombs
were dropped on the principal civil buildings.
Delightful pen-pictures are given of the
Super-Bosches, the Kaiser, the Crown
Prince, the Krupps, Bernhardi, and the other
“dome-heads” supposed to be the mark of
all the greatest men in Germany. Bern-
hardi, we learn, regards women not much
higher than animals, he contradicts his wife
and always walks ahead of her in the street.
He was, to the governess, “the most ruth-
less, brutal-looking man I had ever met, the
very type of militarism in flesh and blood-; —
especially blood.” She states that her mis-
tress, “very much ‘in’ with the highest court
set in Potsdam and Belin,” often let slip
official secrets to her. One was that the
Crown Prince’s instructions on his trip to
India were to ascertain the state of feeling
among the native rulers towards Great
Britain —in other words, he deliberately
accepted the hospitality of Britain in India
as a spy. And while this goggle-eyed youth
was a guest of a high native dignitary he
disgraced himself by an attempt on a lady
of the harem. One or more British officers
sacrificed themselves to save the culprit.
•‘Something horrid had happened, and every-
one in the secret was sick with disgust and
rage.” And the Crown Prince “funked”
badly when he inspected the two spirited
mounts provided for him to go pig-sticking
with the Inniskilling Dragoons at Muttra.
To save his face he had to pretend sudden
illness. Both Kaiser and Crown Prince,
though brave, are liable to these sudden
nervous attacks. “By the fear of what cruel
people might call ‘funk’ the Kaiser has been
debarred from flying with Count Zeppelin
or Orville Wright, or any other of the air-
craft inventors who would gladly have given
him an opportunity. Occasionally the same
over-powering qualm of nervousness seizes
the Crown Prince.”. Unfortunately one of
these occurred in the presence of a British
regiment.
The governess found herself mixed up in
the German secret service. Her services,
without her knowledge, were utilised to
assist German spies to gain information in
England. We learn, too, that the Kaiser
w'ent occasionally incognito to Paris, and
that General von Ivluck actually paid a visit
to some quarries near Soissons—the famous
quarries which turned out to be German
trenches, prepared a year before the war.
It seems, from this story of the Kaiser, told
to the author by her mistress, as if the
Crown Prince’s famous looting exploits are
hereditary. “Everybody knows that the
Kaiser only borrowed the wonderful Pales-
tine Bible in the Berlin Museum. He will
never give it back. He saw it in Syria,
when he was there, and begged to be allowed
to take it home to show to the savants.
The guardians. .. .had no authority to lend
such a book, and dared not do so The
Kaiser tried to bully the Berlin museum
authorities into buying the Bible, but as it
was worth nearly five million marks, they
couldn’t afford it. At last the Kaiser
induced the Sultan to use his influence” —
and Berlin got the book, the Kaiser pledging
himself to return it. The Countess thought
the world ought to be grateful to the Kaiser
for bringing such a treasure to Berlin.
Gossipy and readable, these iniimate
descriptions of the Super-Hogs have a defi-
nite topical value.
&
The German Delusion forms the chief topic
of J. W. Allen’s Germany and Europe
(Bell, per W. Dymock, Sydney, 2s. 6d.).
Without that delusion Hogmany would
never have gone to war. The Bruteons
appear to have convinced themselves of
their real, definite, absolute superiority
to all other peoples. At least, they
have tried hard for the last 40 years
to convince each other. The Bruteon, in
this delusion, is simply behind the times.
Not so very long ago every Englishman
believed that he, individually, was worth
three Frenchmen. According to Hogman
historians, all progress since the c (lapse of
the Roman Empire, all achievement in art,
science and philosophy has been the work
of the Bruteonic Tace, either directly or as
a result of Bruteonic fusion with the con-
quered and worn-out peoples of the Empire.
“Now she stands forth, armed and ready,
conscious of her Divine mission, God’s
chosen people, first in arms as in arts, to
redeem the world. And she stands alone in a
world of degeneration and of barbarism.”
And yet even the word “Prussia” is hot
German!
Many Hogmen appear really to believe
that the salvation of the world lies in the
spread, by force if necessary, of something
they call “German culture.” The nearest
equivalent to Kultur would be the word
Civilisation; but it is impossible to give any
precise meaning to this large-sounding Hog-
man word. Still more difficult is it to
define the phrase “German Kultur.” What
culture has Hogmany that other Europeans
have not? “What is the specific element in
German civilisation which the rest of the
world needs so sorely that Reims Cathedral
must be battered and smashed in order that
we may get it? Personally, lam not aware
that up to the present Germany has given
us anything to equal in value a single one
of the great statues on the west front of
Reims.” The only possible answer to this
question is that Hogmany has for the world
a new religion, the religion of Woden or of
Napoleon or of Zarathustra—or of Callan
Park.
Our task, and maybe our hardest task, will
be to destroy this Hogman illusion of
superiority, “A people that has actually
built Berlin, and yet believes itself capable
of building something finer than Reims
Cathedral, would seem to be hopekss.”
_ Allen writes with restraint and without
bias; his attempt to state a for Hog-
many is obviously honest; amid the host of
ephemeral war publications his fittle book
is worth consideration.

Recent military productions include:
Hints to Non-Coms in the Australian Mili-
tary Forces (Is.), Outposts (is. 3d.), Hints
on Health for Soldiers (6d.), published in
handy form by Angus and Robertson, Syd-
ney, the first two written by Lieut. R.
Stupart; a Dictionary of Naval and Mili-
tary Terms, by C. F. Tweney (Unwin, per
Angus and Robertson; 2s. 6d.), which,
though it gives the names of many warships,
does not mention the Australia; and a hand-
book of Musketry (Murray, Is.).

VARIOUS VERSE
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The Turn of the Tide.
A long time waiting with little to show,
We sat in our boat, at either side,
While the listless weeds washed to and fro.
And here and there, at the will of the tide.
The bay was still, and the trees and flowers
That nodded at noon w'ere all asleep;
Yet never a silver-bream was ours.
And never a crimson lord of the deep.
With little to say and much to think.
We watched, while the velvet hours went by ;
The ripples arise and the ripples sink.
Alone on the waters —Rose and I.
I said to her then: “The good time flies.
Let us get hence, for the bay is wide.”
Rose lifted her dark blue, laughing eyes.
And “Wait,” she said, “till the turn of the
tide.”
The bay was azure from east to west —
All still and azure from north to south;
The rose that reddened on Rose’s breast
Was red as the rose of Rose’s mouth.
“ ’Tis weary waiting,” I said to Rose.
(Was ever a rose as fair as she?)
“The love-hour comes, and the love-hour goes.
And when will the bright ‘Yes’ spoken be?”
Then Rose grew red —do you wonder why?—
And, somehow or other, I said or sighed :
“The line is set, but the prey is shy—
I’ll wait, dear Rose, till the turn of the tide.
N.S.W.
RODERIC QUINN.
Pale Sunday Lovers.
They are as pale as dough, and she wears
specs.
He has a boxer and a long-tailed coat.
They “walk out” speechlessly —two fragile
wrecks
With large heads wobbling on thin, halt-
starved necks.
Where’er they’re bound, they’ve “just got
off the boat.”
If they have thoughts, they don't exchange
them now,
But. overawed by Marriage in advance
And the sweet weight of, their engagment
vow%
They wander aimlessly and anyhow,
A foot apart. This is the true romance.
She gazes longingly with glassed-in eyes
At well-kept villas as they loiter by.
She has her dreams; but she at heart is
wise
And knows right well, however hard he tries.
lii some small, humble shed they’ll live and
die.
Are they to wed next year and so defy
Eugenics, Death. Insolvency and Care?
Or have they solemnly agreed to tie
Their future's up till he attains some high
And dizzy height such as a chief clerk s
chair?
They are ashamed. Their awkward, writh-
ing w'alk
Proclaims the fact. His foolish coat-tails
flap
About his thighs. Her vacant face of chalk
Books like a slice of old and speckled pork.
But she is “nice” and he’s a decent chap.
They arc “respectable”! ITow r could A ice
haunt
Such tails as his and her gold Sunday specs!
To Satan they would jointly cry “Avaunt!”
Did he accost them on this speechless jaunt.
But would he worry once he’d seen their
necks?
N.S.W.
KODAK.
Clarinda.
Clarinda. is as fair in face
As when I first became her slave.
Her figure still preserves its grace;
And every charm that made me crave
Her heart survives. Yet she to me
Seems now less fair; for 1 am wise,
And future dangers I can see —
There's something wrong about her eyes!
And yet her eyes are deeply brown
And lit at times with subtle fire.
They have a beauty of their own
Enough to stir the heart’s desire.
One time I verses wrote to them.
And in their glances took delight;
Their beauty I do not condemn —
There’s something wrong about their sight.
\Ye walked together on a day
When all the earth was glorious.
I spoke my heart in careless way
About life’s golden springtime. Thus
She answered me, with sombre air
In rather melancholy vein :
“There is a dark cloud over there,
And I’m convinced that it will rain !”
“Here with the earth-stars, many-lined,
About our feet, awhile we’ll camp,”
I cried in joy, still unsubdued.
She answered: “No ; the ground is damp ”
And when, alternative to that,
A skiff I on the river found,
The tempting trip she murmured at:
“I am afraid we might be drowned!”
And so it was throughout the day
That to me seemed so fair and bright,
“The sun’s too hot,” I heard her say
(’Twas “chilly” coming home at night).
And while on joyance I was bent
She saw all clouds that marred the day,
And picked some cause for discontent
From everything that round us lay.
I strive to see the best on earth ;
And Sorrow, with its lifted head
To search for causes marring mirth,
Is not for such as I to wed.
Clarinda’s faults I cannot blink :
Though still I love her otherwise.
My heart turns cold whene’er I think.
There's something wrong about her eyes!
N.S.W.

THURSDAY, APRIL 22, 1915.
THOSE WHO WAIT FOR HELP.
Ik the story of the big war there is nothing
that grips quite as hard as the knowledge
that broken Belgium is waiting with pathetic
trust for the day when victorious British
troops will march into Brussels whistling
“Tipperary.” Belgium’s children starve;
her fields are full of the graves of men ; her
women have faced the last thing in horror.
But the big hope remains, and British boots
may yet stir the dust of Quatre Bras and
Waterloo while there is a Belgian nation
left to rejoice. At present, however,
progress is being made only by inches,
and unless the German runs out of
nitre to make his powder, or gets so
short of sausage and potatoes that he can
no longer stand up to his gun, or is con-
verted by the Peace Society, the faith of
Belgium will have a still harder strain put
upon it before the conquering cheers that
mean a square meal and a sound sleep are
heard in the streets of her capital. Let the
help from outside come as fast as it may, the
waiting is tedious; and history is full of
stories of those - who hung on in fearful
expectation of the aid that never arrived.
From the day that llezekiah waited
vainly in Jerusalem for news that Tiehakaii
had crossed the desert and smashed the
Assyrians to the hour when an Irish rebel-
lion passed out in smoking thatch and grim
military executions because of the late and
inadequate assistance of the French, the
weak and ill-prepared have suffered un-
speakable things through putting their trust
in outside help. The fault has not always
been with those who offered their aid, nor
has it been humanly possible to prevent
delay and misfortune. The French did try
to assist the Irish rebellion; Tieiiakah did
try to come to Jerusalem’s rescue. A thou-
sand expeditions have been fitted out by
the strong to help a weak ally to shake off
the oppressor. But take the story of such
undertakings first and last, it is astonishing
to note the smallness of their success and
the diminutive part in history that they
really play. On the other hand, some of
history’s brightest pages are those which
record the triumphs of small peoples left to
their own resources who by preparation,
forethought aud determined loyalty to
themselves have hurled back invaders of the
largest and most terrifying sort. An exami-
ration of actual facts must modify the
most sanguine person’s view of the extent
to which even the biggest military country
can protect a small ally from the things that
really count in warfare.
The case of Belgium is not exceptional
. when examined in the light of the past; but
Time has made this change—what was
always difficult in the old days has become
practically impossible now that a blow can
be delivered with such speed and a mistake
can be rectified only at such appalling cost.
Hence the talk of protection of smaller
nations by larger is now in a general way
worth nothing. It is easy to say that if
big Mexico suddenly and without cause
poured troops into small Guatemala, the
still larger United States would interfere
and see fair play. As a matter of fact, the
United States might fight Mexico to a stand-
still .and still not save Guatemala from any
of the real horrors of the invasion. The
final result could make no difference to the
people whose homes had been broken, whose
women had been ravished, and whose whole
plan of life and outlook upon the world had
been torn in the sack and carnage.
The chief handicap from which Austra-
lian Defence suffers is the deeply-rooted
belief that this country is protected from
without. It is this which colors the remarks
of the politicians and of the man lighting
his pipe at the corner. Yet in reality, even
with such protection as we nominally pos-
sess, Australia is about the most exposed
and unsheltered country on the face of the
planet. Had Germany, when preparing her
great assault upon England, pursued a differ-
ent policy, sent a fleet on a world voyage
that would have made her mistress of these
seas at the date of the prearranged declara-
tion of war. and concentrated an attack upon
the Australian capitals, what would have
been the exact value of the protection in
which we have trusted so greatly? It is
perfectly true that, after months spent in
sweeping the North Sea of mines, England
might have delivered a tremendous assault
upon the German naval ports in her home
waters; but it is equally certain that all the
horrors known to the Belgians would have
been felt in Australia, and that we should
still have been starving and hoping and wait-
ing for the relief that might come, but must
come too late. Judged by the present aspect
of the war, such a line of procedure on the
part of Germany would have been about the
best that she could have pursued in her
attempt to make her navy effective; and
whatever the final results of the general con-
flict might have been, it is difficult to see
how it could have failed of a measure of
success. At _ any rate, it is quite certain
that no British protection could then have
saved our homes from shame and our people
from bitter suffering.
* * * *
Australia, from her peculiar position, and
beset by dangers of a peculiar nature, has
need of all the protection she can get from
Britain and from her association with other
British countries; but the safeguard offered
by a promise of assistance from a distance
is of no more real value than the scrap of
paper on which the Kaiser set his boot when
it threatened the march of his army. The
blow that strikes Australia may fall without
an hour’s official warning, and the only
defence possible is that which is prepared
and waiting at the moment of the first chal-
lenge. Relief, vengeance, what you will,
may follow; but Australia’s protection lies
in the weight with which she herself can
strike the invader. In the light of this know-
ledge all future preparations must be judged.
A. Defence system which trusts to outside
aid to better its own inadequacy can be of no
use to us.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
A Party of Incurable Jibbers.
During the last few months Joe Cook and
his friends have now and again explained
m ysteiies of Libeial policy; but though
no one could understand what they were
then talking about , Liberal idea s were
crys tallised into simple form last week when
the Federal Parliament met. “Let us
eschew party politics, pass necessary war
measures and close up,” cried Joseph. This
means, of course, that the truce Joe
would^t Ugten t ’ o during the elections
should now come into operation—that a
Parliament, elected to discharge some very
definite obligations, should take morphia
into its veins and go off to sleep until a mess
ten thousand miles away is tidied up, which
may be to-morrow or the next day or on
some date so far in the future that Joseph
will find even fewer hairs on his head when
peace arrives than he can discover now. For
rest and quiet—for the undisturbed peace
of the valetudinarian bottle-oh with a pipe
and a newspaper-—the Australian Liberal
yearns with pathetic intensity. It is noth-
ing to Cook that to the people of the Corn-
monwealth a revision of the tariff is a mat-
ter of vital importance, or that the amend-
ment of the Constitution is more pressing
than ever before, or that the Northern Ter-
ritory shrieks for attention. To him these
are merely matters of trifling moment, to be
put on the shelf and taken down for leisurely
inspection later on, or—preferably, no doubt
—forgotten in the mock fury of a sham-
fight concerning preference to unionists or
about the electoral rolls. Nor is this frantic
desire to bolt for the bush peculiar only to
Cook. “There is nothing urgent except
matters relating to war,” babbled MiLlen
in the Senate. Even the tariff and the
cost-of-living question are insignificant. To
Australia these questions are not insignifi-
cant. They have a place of importance
almost equal to that of the war ; for though
national existence hinges to some extent on
the struggle in Europe, the problem of indus-
trial development goes to the very root of
the nation’s social life. To argue that
because the people are engaged in fighting
an enemy in the field they should condemn
themselves to continue, thus weakened, an
unequal struggle with commercial oppo-
nents may be Liberalism, but is just plain
idiocy all the same. The fact is that Cook
and Millen don’t want to do the work they
are paid for. And because they don’t want
to handle such a question as the tariff, they
imagine', or profess to imagine, that the
electors also want the matter hidden away
behind a bogus truop arranged in the inter-
ests of a few bewildered individuals whose
normal attitude is one of opposition to every-
thing, including work.
Is the High Court to Leg-rope Justice?
Tiie knot into which the High Court of Aus-
tralia tied itself by the judgment in the
Father case last December seems to be even
more confused than The Bulletin sus-
pected ; for it now transpires that, in with-
drawing from Eatiieb that leave to appeal
which they had previously granted him, at
least two of the Judges did not know what
they were doing, and another one appears not
to have understood what the effect of his
finding would be. Eatheb, it may be re-
called, was convicted of an offence at Syd-
ney after the presiding Judge (Hamilton)
had ruled that certain evidence tendered by
the prosecution came within the scope of
“the corroborative evidence which implicates
the accused” demanded before conviction is
allowable under the Crimes Act. Eatiieb
appealed to the Full Court against this rul-
ing, and the appeal was heard by Chief
Justice Cullen and Justices Being and Sly.
Cullen and Sly held that Hamilton was
right; Peing that he was absolutely wrong.
There was grave difference of opinion on a
point going to the very roots of the law of
corroboration —of what “implicates” a per-
son accused of a particular offence —-and the
appeal was by leave carried to the High
Court, the point at issue being determination
of the construction to be placed in future by
the higher and lower Courts in N. S. Wales
upon an integral part of the criminal code.
There was long argument before four Judges.
It is known that they differed. They
ordered re-argument before the Full Bench,
and the matter was thrashed out some more
at great length. Then by a majority it was
decided that the Court would have nothing
to do with the thing—that Eatheb and his
appeal could go to Hades, because the Court
had decided to set up a rule for itself by
adopting the practice followed in criminal
appeals by the Privy Council. That prac-
tice is to entertain criminal appeals only in
cases where justice in its very foundations
has been subverted. This decision, by
which the Court evaded its obligations to the
nation, had the immediate result of estab-
lishing as a precedent the more than dubious
ruling of Judge Hamilton which settled
Eatiieb, and the wider consequence of bang-
ing and bolting the doors of the High Court
as a place of appeal against the erroneous
construction of the criminal law. And it
has the additional and even more amazing
feature that among the majority of Judges
there was one who now admits he agreed
with Isaacs (the only dissentient/ that the
ruling of the Courts below was fatuous, and
another who “thought he was following the
practice of the Court.” The matter has
since December been referred to occasionally
by the Court, but last week it bobbed up in
a new form; and finding they could not
dodge it, their Honors fell over the obstacle in
a large, muddlesome heap. The question this
time was whether Chidley, the citizen of
abbreviated attire who pervades Sydney,
could appeal against a conviction sustained
by a divided judgment of the State Supreme
Court. A majority of the High Court
thought he couldn’t, because of the rule laid
down in re Eatiieb ; the remainder of the
Court thought that, if he couldn’t, there was
something seriously wrong with the legal
machine. The upshot of a tangled discus-
sion was that their Honors went into hiding
at the beginning of the week, and, removing
their wigs, settled down to a good hard
think. The position they find themselves in
is a fairly serious one, and for the people
of the Commonwealth perilous to the last
degree. Under the Constitution which gave
it birth, the High Court of Australia was
established as a Court of criminal appeal.
For it to go behind this and decide that,
instead of being the medium through which
criminal jurisprudence may be shaped, it will
merely make interposition in circumstances
almost inconceivable —and by copying the
Privy Council rule it has done this, or. at
any rate most of the Judges think so—4s a
something the Legislature should not tol-
erate. This naper is not much concerned
about either Father or Chidley individu-
ally : but it has a strong desire to see the
criminal law so laid down that no doubts
can exist about the course of Justice.

The Greater Criminals.
There are two classes that deserve to be
hanged or boiled. One is composed of a
sort of working man —the working man
who gives practical approval to the pro-
paganda of the I.W.W. Some leaflets
recently issued by the body which calls it-
self the “Fighting Union” contained the
following counsel: —
Slow work means more jobs. More jobs means
less unemployed, and less competition means higher
wages. Less work. More pay.
SLOW DOWN, SLOW DOWN.
HOW TO MAKE YOUR JOB EASIER.
GET WISE TO I.W.W. TACTICS.
Don’t be a Pacemaker, someone has to be the
slowest, let it be you.
Australia needs just now men who, if they
won’t go away and fight, will at least stay
behind and work. The call for workers who
will butt in with the utmost vigor and help
to produce khaki and small arms and
canned goods and boots and a thousand
other requisites of warfare was never so
acute. The alternative for the member of
the “Fighting Union” who scorns to be a
pacemaker, and incidentally for his family,
may be such experiences as are now being
endured by Belgium and Servia. What
shall it profit an I.W.W. enthusiast that
he got wise to the “Fighting Union’s” tac-
tics, and slowed down, and refused proudly
to be a pacemaker in April 1915 if in April
1916 he finds the country where he is pursu-
ing his leisurely existence faced with inva-
sion? The fact that he did less work, and
encouraged others to follow suit, will not
mitigate for him the pain of seeing his
womenfolk outraged and such property as
he may have destroyed. The rotten worker
has his prototype at the other end of the
scale in the rotten capitalist. It was estab-
lished in the Senate last week that one
firm of contractors had supplied the Aus-
tralian Government with military boots the
sole-leather of which would not have stood
more than two days’ wear. Another con-
cern engaged to supply candle-boxes of
weight, and provided goods which
were 4oz. under weight in each case.
Napoleon hanged the swindling contractors
of France, and his methods with workers
who either shirked or malingered in the
time of their country’s great need were
equally sudden. Strenuous measures of the
sort are being taken in Europe, even in
these humanitarian times, with proved
traitors. The worker who deliberately
“slows down” when his defenders on the
other side of the globe are perishing for
lack of clothing is as blameworthy as the
patrol who leaves his post to sleep behind
a hedge; and the manufacturer who sup-
plies his country’s army with shoddy is in
the same category as the outcast who sells
it for a spy’s wage. But, because the war
is still far away and because Australians
are congenitally casual, the nation’s most
dangerous malefactors remain at large
while comparatively respectable burglars
and forgers continue to go to goal in large
numbers.

Holman’s Eloquent Tears.
That extraordinary person, W. A. Holman,
took advantage of the discussion on the
totalisator at the P.L.L. Conference in Syd-
ney to exhibit himself as a man who stood
foursquare to the limelight of purity and
high devotion to principle. He proclaimed
himself as one who was sternly opposed to
the shifts and expedients of tinkering reform
—as one who would cut his right hand off
before he would bring the Labor platform
down to a level on which gambling should
be regarded as worthy of the consideration
of the great movement. It was a line pose
and calculated to win blind Support from
those whose inmost convictions are to be
swayed by a finely-modulated voice and the
use of fluent phrases. Holman came to the
Conference under a cloud of suspicion. He
knew it was charged against him that he
had betrayed Labor, failed to do the work
he had been appointed to do, neglected the
Labor platform, and associated himself with
its enemies ; and he knew also that a bold front
and a puritanical pretence of political right-
eousness might prevent those charges being
pressed home. In this knowledge he rushed
on the Conference, took the elephant by its
tusks, and orated delicately in its ear a
statement that looked like a schoolboy’s des-
perately planned excuse for neglect of home
lessons. Lie explained, in effect, that the
war had been very fierce, and, owing to the
disturbance which it had created, it had been
found quite impossible to carry through
those things which, if he had kept his
promise, should have been done long before
the war started. He assured his hearers,
also in effect, that black was very often
a covering for white, and that if they would
watch until their eyes got the right focus,
they would see that black in this instance
really was white. As for the things left
undone, there was no reason why they
shouldn’t be attended to right away, always
provided that the war behaved itself. But
in any case they should wait and see. The
effect of the oration upon the delegates was
mixed, but Holman knew that this was
only the first round. The second arrived
when the totalisator discussion gave the
opening. Then the elocutionist rose to the
full height of a Mark Antony. He de-
nounced the totalisator machine on the
ground that Labor should oppose the iniquity
of gambling in all its forms, and he wept
rhetorical tears that a great movement, for
whose principles he had in the remote past
gone to gaol—when was this, by the way?—
should descend to the awful depth of taking
the racecourse punter under its protection
and enabling him to get a fair deal. Of
course, the common-sense of the Conference
carried the totalisator proposal, but Hol-
man had scored to the height of. his desire.
He stood forth the scourged and persecuted
champion of Labor’s ideals, who even now
would stand in the pillory for an ethical
principle, and who would oppose to the last
tear drop the degradation of his party. For
a man who had for long had it in his power
to carry out the best portions of the Labor
platform, and who had not only failed to do
so, but had run directly counter bath* to their
spirit and letter, the pose was a remarkable
one. Yet the political contortionist con-
trived to get himself accepted at face value,
and by his admit tears won to himself sup-
port in the very direction in which he most
needed it, Nohady can doubt Holman’s
histrionic ability.. He contrives to be the
villain of the Labor drama for SO weeks of
the year, and then turns round and becomes
its hero for the two weeks that really count
with the organisation that nominally' controls
him. All this is a tremendous danger to
the Labor party. At present there is but
one Holman, but his success makes it cer-
tain that he will soon have; plenty of imita-
tors. With 20 skilled elorntdimists in the
field the P.LX. Conference would become a
mere mutual admiration society, and the
Labor platform would be a toy trotted out
annually as the central object, in a game of
rhetorical hide-and-seek, interspersed with
stage heroics and theatrical tears. Labor
as a political organisation has failure written
large across it, and for the earnest men in
the movement there are two Broad courses
open. The party can retain the form of life
and palter down to real death by remaining
an electoral machine for the sim-mit of
HoLMAN-ism and all that it means, oy it can
decide upon its own death bv the abolition of
party, and so rise to a newer and stronger life
without the old name hr the old catch-words
that have proved so empty.. In a system of
non-party government Holman might be
quite a useful member of any' legislature- The
very ability that he has used to warp and
twist the intent of his party could be har-
nessed to solid and enduring work when there
was no further place for trick or finesse;
but for HoLMAN-ism there could be no room
at all in the new order. The mandate from
the people could neither be brushed v aside nor
explained away. The business of the Gov-
ernment would be simply to give it the effect
of law, and even a Holman in office could
not get round that simple position. Elective
Ministries and the Referendum with Initia-
tive had the seal of the Labor movement set
upon them long ago. It is about time the
old planks were brought to the front again
and put into practical use. As the political
game is being played at present, Labor —the
real party, with large holies and big enthusi-
asms —cannot score a try because its own
backs are defending its opponents’ goal. The •
position is ridiculous, and the sooner a new
set of rules is formulated the better for
everybody—not even excepting W. A. .
Holman.


Naticual Efficiency.
Victoria’s Minister of Public Works
llagelthorn, who is by way of being a
Itich Landlord, proposes to import a “lead-
ing professor of a well-known American
university” to teach the State points on
“national efficiency.” llageltiiorn’s idea
is excellent, but its details are simply dis-
astrous. In the first place his expert will
have to spend at least two years obliterat-
ing his views as a Yank and building a
fresh set in accord with our industrial sys-
tem, with its eight-hour day, arbitration
courts, wages boards, etc.; and long before
he has crystallised the new ideas it will
have been borne in on the Minister that an
Australian, not necessarily a professor,
might have done the job better and in
shorter time. “National efficiency” is
merely a synonym for organisation, and the
avoidance of that waste which this country
is simply revelling in. The waste of effort
involved, in our happy-go-lucky ways of
distributing our daily food supplies is monu-
mental. It would take the swiftest expert
on earth a good many years to put right that
mess. Then he could put in another similar
term grappling with the waste of effort
caused by the breaks of gauge. After that
he might apply his “national efficiency”
policy to the absurd practice of dragging
wool from Riverina to Sydney and wheat
from Horsham to Melbourne, and similar
wasteful habits all over the Commonwealth.
When that job was finished the expert could
lay his vast intellect on to the waste of
effort involved in sending greasy wool to
England—or Germany—to be scoured,
twisted, woven into cloth and sometimes
made into garments, and then dragged
back. If the expert had any , time on his
hands after he had abolished 12 unneces-
sary Houses of Parliament and six super-
fluous Governors he might get busy on the
waste ,of effort indulged; in by the thousands
of unnecessary “commercial men” in the
big cities, with their clerks, typewriters,
travellers, canvassers and other adjuncts.
After that —well, _ the expert may want a
rest. That there is room for a million tons
more “national efficiency” in Australia is
undoubted, but so far as he has indicated,
Hagelthorn’s idea of the substance
begins and ends with the policy of speeding-
up workers in factories, which is a mere
mouse of a conception to make a fuss about.
A Proposal to Destroy Farm Cove.
The proposal to take the stuff excavated for
the construction of the Sydney city railway
to form a road around Farm Cove should
rpuse general protest. Two or three years ago
roads were outlined in connection with the
scheme for turning Government House into
something democratic though indefinite, but
the idea seemed to have been mislaid or for-
gotten. Now it is revived in a new form
which threatens to be even worse. The pub-
lic gardens around Farm Cove are about the
choicest possession of the city, and their
best point is the clean water-frontage to the
little bay. All smells stop outside the
railings, and the women and children
have a playground of the most beautiful sort
that is kept really free from pollution. This
seems to annoy Ministers to such an extent
that they pine for the honk of the motor and
the reek of petrol in the pure and quiet
place. The proposal to make public roads
among the lawns and flower beds having met
with general disapproval, they come again
tq the attack with a scheme to throw rock
and spoil into the sea in front of the gardens,
in order that by hook or crook noise and
stench may be introduced between the popu-
lar resort and the water that gives it charac-
ter and charm. The water frontages about
the city which have been reserved for the
people are few enough, and decency would
suggest that they should be increased rather
than diminished ; but this is evidently not
the State Government’s view. The remark
of a city alderman that the proposed road
would not interfere with the gardens is
evidently. an echo of Ministerial opinion.
But it will convince nobodv. The proposal
to make a road round the Cove is simply a
proposal to steal the people’s most delightful
water-frontage in a tricky fashion. Were
the Government to fill up Mosman Bay with
rpeks and rubble, could any sane person be
found to _ declare that the gardens of resi-
dents which reach to the water’s edge had
not been interfered with? The public should
see to it that the spoil from the city railway
is not thrown into Farm Cove, even if the
State Premier and his colleagues have to eat
it a clod at a time.
The Breakdown of the Constitution.
Abe the Australian States federated, or is
the whole thing a dream? Having seized all
the wheat in N. S. Wales, and put police-
men on duty to see that not a bag went to
a foreign country like Victoria, the Govern-
ment has now placed a heavy foot down on
trade in butter. The wheat seizure was
justified by the argument that no N. S.
Wales surplus existed, and that if any
wheat was permitted to go out of the State
the deficiency would have to be met by im-
portation from abroad. In the case of but-
ter, however, no shortage of supply is alleged
to be likely. There has been copious rain-
fall all summer in the coastal dairying dis-
tricts, and the cow cockies’ output shows no
sign of shrinkage. But in Victoria, where
the drought has been particularly severe, the
inhabitants have had to rely to some extent
on N. S. Wales and Queensland. So the
N. S. Wales Government has decided that
Victorians, being mere aliens, are not en-
titled to get more than 10 per cent, of the
butter made in the Ma State. All of which
is merely a second instalment of State Spite,
and another reminder of the limitations in
the instrument by which it was fondly sup-
posed that trade and commerce within Aus-
tralia had been handed over to the control of
the Commonwealth. It looks as if before very
long trade with neighboring States in Aus-
tralian products will be as difficult to carry
on as trade with the enemy; for the
Bumbles who now rule the roost obviously
look upon local boundaries as frontiers.
When the other States begin to take a hand
in the game the position will be magnificent.
When Peace Comes.
Up to the present, Germany is inviolate.
So, to all industrial intents and purposes, is
Austria. Their manufactories are intact.
In many cases these concerns are not only
capable of beginning to turn out huge quan-
tities of finished goods the moment peace
arrives, but no doubt they also hold large
stocks. In the happy past the pur-
chaser had a wide area of choice
among manufacturing nations. The buyer
of motor-cars, for example, who didn’t
fancy German-made articles, could go
to Belgium or France and get as good, or
better. Pianos, toys, glass and countless
other products were made as well and as
cheaply by one nation as another. To-
day, Belgium, one of the busiest of manu-
facturing countries, is a waste, and so are
some of the richest parts of France. As in-
dustrial rivals to Germany they are likely
to be out of the hunt for years to come. If
the Hun caves in within a limited time—in
other words, before his own territory has
been invaded —he will thus be in a favorable
position to rehabilitate his national for-
Also, he will have the spur of Neces-
sity .digging into his flanks. In order to pay
the gigantic indemnity that will be demanded
of him he will need to work harder than
ever, and he will require to produce more
and to sell it cheap. No one who knows the
German character can doubt his ability to
do these things. To whom will he sell?
Plainly to those nations whose tariff walls
are inadequate to keep his sweated goods
out. Foremost amongst them is Australia,
the country which is already suffering bit-
terly' from its inability to supply itself with
munitions, small-arms and military requi-
sites in general—a legacy of the foreign-
trade habits of generations of politicians. If
Germany fights to the last—if it battles
desperately on while its territories are over-
run aud the woollen manufactories of Saxony
and Bavaria are destroyed, and the glass
and porcelain works of Thuringia and Silesia
are dynamited, and the silk concerns of
Rhenish Prussia and Baden go under, and
the great iron-works, and paper-mills and
machinery shops and breweries ascend in
smoke—countries such as Australia will get
a fortuitous Protection which, incidentally,
they do not merit. Their industries will have
a chance to forge ahead, not through any
merit on their part, but because so much of
the civilised world is in ashes. But in the
likelier event of Germany deciding that, if
it can’t hold on to Belgium and the North-
East of France, it' can’t put up an effective
resistance at home, there will be but one
thing to save the Commonwealth’s existence
as a manufacturing country, and that is a
tariff. And when one remembers that by
the time Peace arrives Germany will be so
broke and so desperate as to be willing to
sell almost anything it has for the price of
a blutwurst sandwich, the necessity of mak-
ing Australia’s tariff wall one of the loftiest
in history becomes very apparent.
Get in the Ruck, Young Man!
If the N. S. Wales Labor Government offers
B. R. Wise the job of Agent-General pro
tem. it will be a small reward for the work
which he has done for the Australian demo-
cratic cause. Wise’s public career is a
complete sermon on the unwisdom of walk-
ing in the van—just as G. H. Reid exem-
plifies the advantage of dodging cautiously
along in rear of the main body. Wise
was a Federalist when Federation was un-
popular among self-styled Right-thinkers,
more especially in N. S. Wales; and at a
time when Reid, Higgins. Piddington and
other high officer-holders of to-day were op-
posed either to the thing itself or to the
scheme proposed. For some years after
Federation the issue in the Australian Par-
liament was almost exclusively the tariff.
Wise, the Cobden Medallist, visioned the
urgency of high Protection for Australia and
was sufficiently courageous—and, from the
point of view of his own material prosperity,
foolish —to say so in the most bigotedly
foreign-trade part of the Commonwealth.
Nowadays the necessity of a high tariff, more
especially against cheap German, Austrian
and Yankee goods, is a gospel even in
Woollahra and Rose Bay.- At the time Wise
first talked high tariffism thq doctrine was
heresy even in Woolloomooloo. When Wis'E
first introduced arbitration the employing
class carried on as though he had imported
the Devil. Arbitration has not entirely
stopped strikes, of course, any more than the
criminal law has entirely abolished theft.
But with all its faults the community clings
to it still and declines on any account to
revert to the old. anarchic state of affairs of
the" ’9o’s and earlier. Ten years ago the advo-
cate of compulsory service and an Australian
fleet was regarded in well-to-do circles (and
in many others) as a Socialist, and a vision-
ary,. a fool, and one who wished to
wantonly insult the Crown and the
Flag. Wise was one of the earliest
Defence enthusiasts to be heard in pub-
lic. He advocated the Referenda, too, when
an affirmative answer seemed an impossibility.
As a reward for his foresight, or progres-
siveness, or congenital lunacy, or whatever
it is, he has now the chance of a 12 months’
job as State Agent-General. How much
subtler was the political part played by
G. H. Reid! He opposed Federation
bitterly—till he sensed the fact that a
majority of the electors wanted it. He
talked Freetrade for as long as N. S. Wales
votes attached to it, which was for years
after it had been dead as regards the rest
of the Commonwealth. He tried Anti-
Socialism on the constituencies, and jet-
tisoned it when he found that it wasn’t
going down. He fiercely fought local
Defence till the country declared for it,
and then he became its warm upholder.
Always he was the adopter of his oppo-
nents’ best ideas ; the reaper where others
had sown; the tory cuckoo in the demo-
cratic nest; and his reward for consistently
lumbering along a mile or so in rear of the
legislative ’bus has been several of the best
jobs in the nation’s gift. The spectacle of
B. R. Wise thankfully taking the Agent-
Generalship—it may be for long and it may
be for only 12 months —while G. H. Reid
relinquishes the High Commissionership in
the reasonably well-founded expectation of
a seat on the High Court bench, is one that
should be considered diligently by all ambi-
tious young politicians so long as the abomi-
nable party system endures. The lesson
of it is that the successful politician must
on no account seek to propel the vehicle of
progress, but rather dig his heels into the
earth and suffer himself to be dragged pro-
testingly in its wake.
«.
The trained Australian officer who is kept in
the Commonwealth while the foreign service
commands he hungers for are bestowed on
militia officers of far less experience, is not
the only fretting regular we have. The rank-
and-file are chafing just as much, and dis-
appointed permanent artillerymen are writ-
ing poignant poems to this paper about the
ignominy of being detained to sweep out
peaceful seaside forts and polish taps, while
raw amateurs are pouring into the camp-
schools to hurriedly train for the real thing.
British Territorials have relieved the regu-
lars at such accessible places as Malta.
Can’t remote Australia also stop putting the
military cart before the horse, and give the
deserving local specialists a chance to practise
their life-job? Complete efficiency should
not disqualify a soldier from soldiering, and
keep him carefully in the background sweep-
ing barracks and writing embittered verse
about his unheroic lot.
Elwood Mead, the expert engaged from the
U.S.A. to put Victoria’s irrigation schemes
on a workable basis, goes back home next
month. Mead is a genial Yankee who knows
just about all there is to know concerning
water when it is available; but the recent
drought caused him to wear a worried look
when the vaunted Waranga Basin supplies
petered out, and the settlers in the “smiling”
irrigation areas got a taste of what the dry
farmer across the road had been having all
the time. Not that Mead didn’t do his best
to avert the calamity ; but when Labor mem-
bers began to nose round in search of ammuni-
tion to use against the Water Commission
and Peacock’s Government, he got sick of
it all and suddenly remembered a fat offer
from over the water. It must be said to Mr.
Mead’s credit that he was always sympa-
thetic and courteous in his dealings with the
wild and woolly settler from Overseas, who
is apt to be a bulldog when up against adver-
sity. One such bulldog went to strike the
one-armed chief during a peppery interview.
But Kennedy, chairman of the Closer Settle-
ment Board, happened to be there, and he
just took that tyke by the scruff of his neck
and threw him out into the street. Still, in
spite of his good points, it can’t be truthfully
said that the settlers are sorry the Yankee
is going. They like him as a man. but official-
dom of any kind is out of favor in the chan-
nel country since the Waranga trickles gave
out.

Judge Backhouse, Solomon of N.S.W.
Marine' Court, dealt the other day with the
case of the harbor lighter Hornet, which
sank outside Sydney Heads a few weeks
back and drowned a member of its crew.
Briefly Backhouse’s conclusions were :
(1) Plainly the Hornet was quite uns.eaworthy and
unfit to proceed to sea.
(2) The vessel was not provided with a certifi-
cated master.
(3) There was no lifeboat on board to save the
crew.
(4) The Court mourned with the deceased sailors'
relatives.
(5) The Court was blessed if it could see any-
thing eke to do.
But what about the maritime statutes which
provide for the punishment of owners and
masters who send or take manslaughtering
craft to sea? And what is wrong with the
penalties to be inflicted when ships go voyag-
ing without being properly officered or
equipped? If the Marine Court can’t en-
force them, what is it on deck for? If only
for weeping crocodile tears over waterlogged
seamen, then it is an expensive luxury that
we can’t afford in these strenuous times.
The High Court decided last week that the
notice warning passengers on N. S. Wales
railways against pushing themselves outside
carriage windows is no part of the contract
they make with the carrier. A man had the
usual elbow beyond the alignment, when the
usual swinging door of the usual passing
train led to the usual doctor’s bill and the
usual banquet of Costs. The small Court
gave the man £lOO damages. Enlightened bv
more expensive argument the Full Court said
the-small Court didn’t know, its job and re-
versed the judgment. Now, either enlight-
ened or obfuscated by still more argument,
the High Court decides that the Full Court
was the one which sruessed wrong; and if
there is any of the £loo'left th.e man who was
long ago awarded the amount can collect it.
Still, even if a man-has a surnlus elbow, it
will be hardly worth while hanging it out of a
railway window on the strength of the High
Court judgment; for the passenger will have
to show, seemingly, that he wasn’t “unreas-
onably” negligent; and that stimulation gives
large scope for the consumption of Costs,
with grave doubt as to the ultimate result.

Customs Minister Tudor was waited on the
other day by a deputation from the Victorian
Sawmillers’ Association asking for increased
duties on timber. The deputation made out
a strong case, but not stronger than the facts
warrant. Canada, the U.S.A. and Russia
are the only countries with a larger forest
area than Australia. Yet from its
100,000,000 acres of forests, 27,000,000 acres
of which are reserved for timber, this coun-
try derives a revenue of only £254,000; while
it buys from the foreigner—including, until a
few months ago, Germany and Austria —tim-
ber and wooden goods to the value of
£3,920,000 annually. On the other hand our
exports are worth only £903,000. From
Amurka we buy yearly over £1,200,000 worth
of tree, chiefly Oregon and hickorv, produced
under conditions which allow it to be shipped
to this country and sold at a cheaper price
than local hardwoods, which experts have
a hundred times affirmed are superior to most
of the imported stuff. Great Britain im-
ported in 1913 £42,725,000 worth of timber,
including £5,427,000 worth of hardwoods, but
this country supplied only £243,000 worth.
In the same year five countries —Russia, Ger-
many, Austria, France and Belgium—which
are now drenching Europe with blood, sold
to the Ma country timber and manufactures
of timber valued at £15,522,000. That sup-
ply being now mostly cut off, Australia might
be doing a service not only to itself, but also
to Ma, by interesting itself in this matter.
But even if we could only keep our own
market it would be a great thing. A5O per
cent, reduction in our timber and furniture
imports would mean an increase of £2,000,000
in the value of our factory products, and the
spending of well over £1,000.000 extra in
wages. Customs Minister Tudor said the
business would "receive attention”; but if
this means waiting for the Inter-State Com-
mission to report, the Australian timber in-
dustry might almost as well set about order-
ing from Uncle SiiAM the wood for its
coffin.
—
Victoria’s Fruit Commission lately un-
earthed an “honorable understanding” which
calls for attention. The “arrangement” is
the property of the Australian Dried Fruits
Association, which practically controls the
output of Mildura, Renmark and Irymple.
At the beginning of each season this body
fixes the wholesale quotations for raisins,
currants, dried apricots, etc. The grocers
who mentioned the thing made no complaint
about the prices fixed, though they thought
some of them might be lower without any-
body being hurt; what they did object to was
the Association’s stipulation that anybody
who bought “outside” should get no more
goods from inside. Two Melbourne grocers
swore that they were offered Goulburn Val-
ley fruit this season at Id. a lb. less than
the Association figures; but as the growers
couldn’t guarantee a regular supply, the
tradesmen dared not buy. A legal member
of the Commission remarked that there was
law directed against restraint of trade. Also
there is a State Attorney-General sworn to
administer the" law. Appearances, however,
suggest that both are fictions.
The party system spread itself over the
P.L.L. Conference at Sydney, and mocked at
the delegates. True, there are traces of the
system within the Conference itself; but
none of the groups would think it necessary
to sack the secretary because he was sus-
pected of leanings towards some other
group, and when elections are held the offices
are shared pretty equally amongst the repre-
sentatives of the various factions according
to the “form” they display, the men and
women being chosen for such merit as may
exhibit itself on , appeal to the delegates. In
spite of this method of dealing with its own
affairs, the Conference allowed the foolish
party system in Parliament to turn it into an
impotent gathering of wind-smiters. The
bulk of the delegates gathered with a fixed
idea that Holman deserved censure. Hol-
man met them with gaseous explanations
that explained nothing. Though it is pos-
sible his word-spinning convinced some
of the delegates that he was right, the
majority of delegates felt that he was
hopelessly wrong. Had there been
no party system in Parliament hold-
ing members to blind allegiance to
a leader who says what must be
done and what left undone, the Conference
might have asserted its will —would, un-
doubtedly, have done so. As it is, the big
organisation, with every reason to be angry,
dared not even be indignant, and bowed its
foolish head to the threat of a man who has
violated practically all its principles, and
turned Labor int> a mendicant at the door
of Cohen. The Worker pictures the posi-
tion by showing a small Holman shaking
a fist in the countenance of a giant. If the
giant had been shown tied ir the bonds of
the party idea, and helnless though wrath-
ful, the situation would have been illus-
trated more completely.

weary burden by a swarm of “bums,” who offered to
sell me a motor-car, or a gold-mine, or a chicken
farm, or an invalid's chair, or the latest edition of
the Scriptures. Apparently this crowd mistook me

The Master Mechanic.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
If we have the munitions. —General French.
In the loud place of turning wheels
The steel glows white, the hammers fall;
His hand across the levers steals
That make the vast machine his thrall.
Its throbbing purpose is his own,
His mind through all the spindles flies,
And crank and pinion shout or groan
Obedient to his master eyes.
By day, by night he may not rest
From this stupendous battle toil;
Here at the furnace flames the test,
These hammer blows the foes despoil.
In valiant metal deftly wrought
A nation’s deeds the world may see.
And death by lathe and anvil taught
Brings the proud foeman to his knee.
Afar the day is dark with smoke,
( An iron tempest shakes the world ;
Grim ranks of war with stroke on stroke
From vantage points of doom are hurled.
Advanced, forced back, in grim retreat,
Spent, but unconquered, still they strive;
And day and night the hammers beat
To keep their battle strength alive.
Not on the hillside scourged with woe,
Not where the wasted cities burn,
But where the furnace forges glow
The chariot wheels of triumph turn.
The foeman breaks, the day is won.
In clamorous rout his squadrons reel,
And victory crowns for labor done
The stout Field-Marshal of the Wheel.
N.S.W.

It looks as if the all-in stage of the European
disturbance is going to be reached by so
short a cut that before many weeks have
passed the question will not be who are
taking part, but who are out of it. In
that event Uncle Sam will find it harder to
discover a neutral than to send the Crown
Prince a barrel of pickled pork. Italy,
hitherto torn between a desire for the
present profits of peace and the future
profits of plunder, seems likely now to make
up her mind, and, by plunging into the aigu-
ment with a bayonet, to make things even
more lively than they are for the old gentle-
man who lives at Vienna —if he lives any-
where. There has already been a mild col-
lision between Italian and Austrian troops,
and the seizure, of large shipments of goods
consigned from over-sea to Austria and Crei-
many. Also rumors which may be reliable, or,
on the other hand, may be entitled to great
disrespect, are floating about of the Rou-
manians and Italians being ready to fall
simultaneously across their respective fron-
tiers. Should this take place, then refer-
ences to “the Front” will be to a mighty
circle of steel corralling up the whole family
of variegated Huns, who, if what is printed
as news be only approximately true, are
beginning to regard one another with the
eye of dark suspicion. Meanwhile the
battle rages with fury and persistence on
the Franco-Belgian lines, where the Allies
are more than holding their own ; and from
the Carpathians the Austrians are said to
be again falling back before the Russian
hosts' and dropping into Hungary. All this
was stated long ago and may again turn
out to be mere moonshine; but as no news
comes to hand of any marked German suc-
cess, the probability is that the Allies are
at last successfully sitting on the enemy s
chest. In the Dardanelles the lurk has
managed to hit back again with a torpedo,
but, generally speaking, the campaign there,
as elsewhere, is uainfully obscure.
H. G. Wells on the moral of it:—
The Battle of Mons —when the British Army, in
the wrong place and the wrong quantity, and with-
out sufficient machine guns and aeroplanes, covered
itself with glory in a losing fight—was lost upon
the playing grounds of Eton.
One of the few cheerful incidents of the
collision which was suffered the other morn-
ing by the Melbourne-Sydney express was the
characteristic comment of a more or less dis-
tinguished American passenger. “A very
small thing,” he told the reporters, disparag-
ingly, “compared with what we have in the
States.” It sounded almost as though, at
home, they had an officer to arrange such
things in a fitting manner. A Chief Colli-
sioner, probably.
George Black, N.S.W. Chief Secretary,
writes with reference to recent criticism of
a beef purchase on behalf of Britain :
One reason for the price seeming higher than that
paid for local supplies is that from all meat taken
over by the State Government for the Imperial
Government the brisket, flanks and shins are cut off.
ln this direction there is a loss of about 101 b.
per carcase. These trimmings are practically value-
less.
Yet the Govt, lately went to the expense of
having literature printed showing how valu-
able these valueless shins and briskets are
for “workers’ dinners.” Moreover, as the
Chief Sec. says he has “not the time to cal-
culate the difference between the cost of car-
cases bought in the rough and carcases
placed in refrigerating chambers ready for
shipment,” it looks as if the business ivas
carried through in the dark.
The baker’s dozen of Federal Parliamen-
tarians who were aboard the Melbourne
express which rushed into the stationary
Temora mail at Binalong (N.S.W.), early
on Saturday morning, haven’t been heard
complaining about their luck since. Just for
once Postmaster-General Spence and Joe
Cook were on the point of going into the
same lobby, and this time it was one where
privileges don’t matter. One passenger
was fatally injured and about a score
gathered themselves together with _ minor
damages. Senator McDougall had his nose
cut, but a piece of sticking-plaster put that
right, and he was able to wander into Syd-
ney Stadium in the evening to see if a couple
of pugs could more successfully keep what
they smell with out of the firing-line. The
mishap at Binalong appears to have been
robbed of its potential tragedy through the
north-bound express having had to slow down
while crossing a bridge just before reaching
the point of collision. Of course there is
the usual demand to know whether the infre-
quent accidents can’t be prevented altogether.
To which the only reply possible is that they
can be —as soon as ever the railways can
find men who are always infallible, and can
hire them for eight or nine shillings a day.

Allen Doone’s experiment of trying a sam-
ple of Uncle Sam’s cheap comedy on his
faithful Irish-Australian followers was com-
menced on Saturday at the Palace (Sydney),
and “Ochone!” and “Hell to its sowl! was
all the reward he got—and about all 100
Much Johnson” deserves. He be the
foine, upstanding dispenser uv untroots
to announce it as “the greatest farce-
comedy of the age” ! If it has any
merits (which is doubtful) they are
not brought to light by the players.
Doone and co. present the piece without any
evident acumen, and the audience is left to
get its money’s worth out of the dialogue.
And, if you plaze, here’s a sample of the dia-
logue : “My dear sir, you don’t suppose”
“Oh, yes, I often do —more often than fre-
quently” “You don’t know what to do if
a lady faints.” “No ; I don’t.” “There you
are. Well, don’t do it.” This to constant
feeders from Doone’s hand of the ould sim-
plicities and melodies of such plays as “Barry
of Ballymoore” and “The Wearin’ of the
Green.” Ochone! Of course, there is no
reason why Doone shouldn’t try himself out
in another line of work occasionally, but the
more occasionally he does it the better. And
ragtime comedy work ought to be scored out
entirely.

“The Lifeguardsman” is expiring nobly
at Sydney Royal in the arms of Julius
Knight and Irene Browne. He will breathe
his last on Saturday night, after which the
bereaved playhouse will close its doors for
a week. On Saturday, May 1, the barriers
will be thrown aside to expose “The Man
Who Stayed at Home.” The despicable
cur is said to be very entertaining, con-
found him.

The pantomime army continues to
mobilise at about eight o’clock every evening
in Her Majesty’s, Sydney. Field-Marshals
Barry Lupino and Arthur Stigant report
that the situation remains unchanged and
wholly favorable to the cause of allied
Mirth and Melody.— Latest bulletin from
the Front of the House.

The revival of “The Man on the Box,” at
the Little Theatre, Sydney, emphasises the
loss the Violet Paget company has suffered
in the departure of Hugh Buckler. Gerald
K. Souper, excellent character-actor as he
is, is not the irrepressible U.S. comedian-hero
required for the part of the young lieutenant
who pretends to be a coachman. His work is,
as always, conscientious; but he lacks the
Niblo touch, though in his serious intervals
he gives the character a genuine dignity.
Victor Fitzherbert, as the journalist friend,
attempts with some success to give to the
opening scene the bustle and briskness that
are needed, but his rapidity of dialogue is
slightly overdone. Kenneth Brampton’s for-
“ CINDERELLA ” PANTO., AT SYDNEY
HER MAJESTY'S.
The old hot-poker joke is successfully revived.
eign Count is a delightful and restrained
study of the stock character of the titled
spy. jtrthur Cornell is satisfactory as a
TJ.S. Judge. In the part of the heroine,
Violet Paget has a character that quite suits
her skilled methods; and the support by the
rest of the company is fairly efficient. The
innocuous comedy amuses.
Those who had seen Robert Parker only
in Wagnerian parts were as surprised at
his first Sydney concert on Saturday night
as some others had been 18 months ago
when he came out in flannels and a smooth
young face to sing in “Madame Butterfly.
But if he is no longer a robustious bearded
warrior he is still the owner of a great
baritone voice which even a Wagnerian
orchestra can’t drown. One cannot say
anything more greatly to the credit of the
singer than that the wonderfully powerful
“The Time is Past” lost nothing by trans-
ference to the concert platform. Entirely
satisfactory support is given by Mrs. Car-
men Harris, formerly one of the Pinschof-
Wiedemann girls of Melbourne, and the
child Scapini, whose violin-playing does
more than give promise. As accompanist
Harold Whittle was in complete sympathy
with his soloist —as he always is when he
has the right kind of soloist. The third
concert of the series will be given this
(Thursday) night.

The return of Muriel Starr to the part of
the “Bought and Paid For” wife is an-
nounced. The main question now agitating
Criterion audiences is whether a certain
Sydney Judge considers this play naked or
nude. This paper classifies it as a neck-to-
knee melodrama.
* *
“Sinbad the Sailor,” with Maggie Moore
as Head Stewardess, Chief Engineer and
First Mate combined, reports all well at
the Adelphi, with satisfactory passenger
lists and no damage thus far from German
submarines. The port of Final Perform-
ance is now only eight nights’ steam
ahead, and by the time the popular old ark
has made fast there its collection of Aus-
tralian femininity in tights should turn out
to be as profitable a cargo as ever dodged
the Customs.
** * *
James J. Corbett continues to deliver the
goods at the Tivoli in much the same profit-
able and forcible manner as he was wont to
hand out his other brand of amusement in
days gone by. Klimo and Klifton, a pair
of Roman ring experts, and a tuneful tenor
with a name that has the appearance of
having escaped from Shakspeare one dark
night, are the most recent importations.
Sterzelly, his dog Lucky, and the shadow-
graphist Stanleys, still clamor for recogni-
tion, and make good for honorable mention.
