Wirths’ elephants’ At Homes are being
well attended at Prince Alfred Park, and
Lizzie, the blood-sweating hippo., confiden-
tially asks fair visitors through the bars if
they know of any beauty doctor who might
be able to cure her of her distressful but
profitable complaint. Lizzie would rather
be insolvent than go on for ever making
money out of a ruined complexion.
*
Compared with the blood-and-bedroom
dramas of Andersonian tradition, the ancient
farcical comedy “Confusion” is an insipid
dish to set before the King’s (Melb.). It
is as though an audience, hungering for a
pound of steak and onions, was confronted
with a poached egg. Yet quite a good speci-
men of its own obsolete sort of entertain-
ment is Derrick’s “Confusion,” wherein the
parlormaid and the butler are the owners of
a lawful baby, and the newly-married mis-
sus smuggles a bull-terrier pup into the
household with the connivance of Uncle
Blizzard, and the pup and the baby become
twin causes of those cheap and artless mis-
understandings which account for the old,
old foolishness of everybody thinking every-
body else must be mad. The false assump-
tion of lunacy was still a novelty in farci-
cal complication when “Confusion” was born
to the stage; but much repetition has worn
the nap off it. The comedy throughout is
rather flabby, and measured by the ordinary
standard its brevity exceeds the limit. In
fact, although the decent old thing is in-
teresting as a curio, the multitude, for the
most part, calls it “rather pussy,” and the
multitude happens to be right. Whereas poor
“Bill Adams” was positively puerile, the
well-constructed “Confusion” is merely
“pussy.” All the same, a capable co. can
get some wholesome laughs out of it. Maggie
Knight and Harry Paulton, jun., as a
pining spinster and a stodgy stage uncle,
score most. Carleton Stuart, who has to work
hard in the part of a reasonable husband
suffering from ridiculous suspicions, is
abetted in the third act by Charles Brown.
Ethel Buckley is coupled with A. Albert in
the baby episodes. Miss Macpherson is an
easy, unaffected, unimpressive wife who can’t
understand that marriage is not all hugs
and honeymoon; and Herbert Ford is coupled
with a young lady who cuts no ice to speak
of.

“High Jinks,” the ragtime concoction
which is frankly naughty and obviously bor-
rowed from the French, is happening as
before at Her Majesty’s (Melb.). The advt.
says that “High Jinks” is enough to make
the Sphinx laugh. But nobody knows how
the Egyptian identity feels on such matters.
It might make the image weep.

** * *
“Lady Godiva” is now rattling the bones
of the old legend to make a Melbourne holi-
day. In the Princess’s story, however, the
fair female does not exhibit her contours
merely for the purpose of saving Coventry
from the tax-gatherer. Rather her ride is
tendered as proof of her chastity, previously
impugned by the evil fibs of. the bogus earl
of Northumbria. This nefarious idler wants
to add Godiva to his other belongings, and
tries to annex her in her nightie by drug-
ging the supper-beer of her serving man
and pinching the keys of her sleeping apart-
ment. Also he attempts to incinerate the
lady’s supposed lover, in reality her sister’s
secret canoodler; and later he endeavors to
finish oil her famous undress parade with a
crudely-devised bridge sensation.. But all
his schemes are ruined by a witless park
dosser called Tom o’ Coventry, who plays
a hero’s part by outing Northumbria in
favor of the rightful heir and restoring
Godiva to the confidence of her fat-headed
husband. Tom, who is eventually blinded
in belated respect to the legend, is pre-
sented by Fred Kehoe as a melancholy fugi-
tive from Bedlam watching over Godiva
with the dumb devotion of a dorg. Miss
Oriel Hotson is Lady Godiva, and sumptu-
ous fleshings hidden under a Niagara of pale
hair make her renowned ride a picture
which any church journal could publish.
John Kirby alternates between a victorious
chuckle and a defeated snarl as the fraudu-
lent Northumbria. George Cross is a tor-
mented, red-legged noble condemned to soli-
tary confinement in gorgeous, golden boots
and tight-fitting clothes. Ward Lyons plays

At the Tivoli (Melb.) the Tales of Mozart
have caught the public ear. The public’s
other ear is outstretched for news of the
turns that will be presented when the faded
Follies cease. Friday will see their finish.
Meanwhile, the gaiety of Melbourne is
enhanced by the departure of Monte Wolf
for the Land of Stage Stars and Tripes.
No more does vehement Monte glare ex-
citedly at his victims whilst making noises
which, as it now appears, were intended to
pass for singing—of the ragtime sort. A
more tuneful man figures in Monte’s stead,
and invites the chorus to witness that beauti-
ful picture which used to be entitled “Sep-
tember Malm.”

The Strollers—they have risen superior to
their original appellation of “Royal”—are
now putting in their fifth week at the Melb.
Auditorium, and Melbourne treats each
change of programme as a Saturday-night
event. The potted play that closes the pre-
sent proceedings is “A Spanish Bull Fight.”

For another week or two, before going on
tour, the Naval War will be carried on at
the Glaciarium. Due warning of the ap-
proaching hostilities is always provided by
a brass band seated on the Glassy Harem
roof, whilst a couple of briny sons of the
season (who are suspected of being ushers
in disguise) hoist their slacks and shiver
their timbers on the footpath outside.

Melbourne Repertory Theatre has two
very interesting productions in view for
May. The first will be Arnold Bennett’s
“The Great Adventure,” which has had a
two-years’ run in London; and the next “The
Trojan Women,” by our old friend Euri-
pides. Each play is to go for three nights,
the season starting at the Athenaeum Hall
on May 1.
* * * *
Shaw perversely classed “The Philan-
derer” among his Unpleasant Plays, so he
alone is to blame if it hasn’t been a com-
mercial success, as popular as (say) “Can-
dida.” Thqse who read him know it for
light, amusing comedy, and as played by
Adelaide Repertory Theatre it went with a
ripple throughout. Its chief drawback now-
adays is that it “dates” itself so emphatic-
ally as of the ’9o’s, when for men and
women to. meet in a club was a subject: for
joke, and Ibsen still stood as the dreaded
symbol for Advanced Thought. “The new
humor,” too, was a phrase of that day,
coined by such as Gilbert, Burnand and
Byron for the younger school. The before-
mentioned club was “a (ow place ; the women
members smoke, and earn their own living.”
It is impossible to take seriously a play in
which it is uncertain till curtain-fall which
of three women—all on the stage together
—the hero will marry. Of the three, Beryl
Kook scored best, as the girl who is pas-
sionate, in both senses. Zoe Reid, though
intelligent as ever, has been seen to much
greater advantage; and Kitty Sullivan (a
recruit), though she spoke the lines of the
“manly” voung woman well enough, had the
odd drawback of looking too girlishly pretty
for the part. Among the men, James
Anderson and Arthur Smith did good char-
acter-work ns the two shocked and bewil-
dered fathers. Douglas Walsh had all the
“fat” as the philanderer. It is half sheer
lying, half appalling candor—what London
calls a Hawtrey part—and he made the
most of it.
** * *
From Adelaide Royal, manager Stewart
has taken the Gilbert and Sullivan Co. west,
with still more west (South Africa) in
prospect. Patronage in Adelaide was
astonishingly big. Gladys Moncrieff and
Pearl Ladd alternated leading parts amic-
ably ; . Walenn showed himself a sound
humorist, though he shouldn’t clown when
other people are singing; Villiers Arnold
made some small narts stand out by ouaint
posturing; and Ethel Morrison resolutely
neglected the difficult task of making her-
self look old and ugly enough for the parts
Gilbert unsportingly makes fun out of. At
the Tivoli, the temporary season has been
so successful that manager Crawford .is
continuing it. But the popular pink Dan-
dies are in their last outdoor evenings.
These party politicians with their press
agents are worse than the stage favorite who
howls for limelight. A week or two ago
the air was filled with talk about the prodi-
gious labors of Carmichael in making the
system of public instruction in N. S. Wales
the most up-to-date on the face of the earth.
The new Minister (Griffith) now comes
along to sav that it is a good thing the De-
partment has secured such a vigorous admin-
istrator as himself, because it is at least a
quarter of a century behind the times.
AT POVERTY POINT.
F. G. Samson writes: Henri Herault,
late manager for Path# Frbres in Aus-
tralia, has been killed in action in
France. As soon as war broke out,
Herault, though married but a short
time previously to the daughter of one
of the most respected members of the French
colony in Sydney, packed up and left for the
Front. On arrival in France he got an
attack of pneumonia, and was laid up in a
military hospital until the middle of Febru-
ary. So it must have been in his first
battle that he met his fate. In the moving-
picture world he will be deeply regretted;
most of the camera men now operating in
Australia learnt their work from him. Pre-
vious to coming to Australia as member of
an expedition fitted out by Pathfis to illus-
trate life and customs in the Northern
Pacific he had visited well-nigh every capital
in Europe with his camera.
“Pen”: Received the “call” at Ballarat,
Anna Gould, the last of the Golden City’s
veteran pros., aged 80. A handsome woman
in her day, clever and decidedly popular—so
old Ballarat speaks of her. At the
Charlie Napier Theatre in the ’6o’s she was
playing “lead” in “Time Tries All,” and
drifted too near the footlights. Her clothing
was suddenly ablaze. Tom King sprang
from his place as leader of the orchestra;
Dr. Roseman followed, and between them
they beat out the fire. The player was little
damaged, and within a few weeks she was
married to Roseman. So tragedy became
romance. But soon it was tragedy again—
Dr. Roseman suicided, for a reason not avail-
able at the time. His widow married T.
Coombe, Town Clerk of Ballarat. Soon he
died, and Anna Gould had once more to
return to the “boards,” where she played
small parts till the early ’Bo’s.
Every theatrical gossiper knows that the
veteran Walter Bentley is the son of a
Scotchbyterian parson (Begg), but it was
never generally known that L. J. Lohr, the
popular showman of old, is another link
between the Church and the Stage. The
register of a Brighton (Melb.) church re-
cords the fact that in August, 1885. Lewis
John, youngest son of the Rev. Charles
Lohr, rector of Alborg-Harleston, Norfolk,
England, married Kate Alice, youngest
daughter of the late Tom Bishop, Philadel-
phia (U.S.A.). The success of Marie Lohr
in “sweet simplicity” parts is accounted
for. Her mother, Kate Bishop, was an
actress who excelled in the artless, angelic
line of character; and “Daddy” Lohr was
the son of a peaceful country rector.
The most remarkable theatrical show that
ever happened in Victoria was given in
1857 by “Bill” Iloskins at Hepburn (near
Daylesford), where the Jim Crow diggings
were then booming. Hoskins had a small
co. at Castlemaine, when a pub proprietor
from Hepburn came along and engaged him
to give one consecutive performance on satis-
factory terms. Hoskins was to get all the
receipts without any deductions whatever,
on a guarantee of a £75 minimum. The
only stipulation was that the advt. posters
should be printed in English, French and
Italian. Conveyances were provided gratis,
and on a Sunday night Mr. and Mrs. Hos-
kins and the co. arrived at Jim Crow —or
Hepburn. The only building in the place
was a store and pub combined, with a
blacksmith’s forge and butcher’s shop at-
tached, so Hoskins wondered where the
audience was to come from. But next
night the one building was besieged with
miners, each bearing a candle stuck in a
bottle. The forge was the stage end of the
theatre and the store the auditorium. The
miners packed the floor and perched on the
cross beams. After the performance they
had a wild spree, and next day Hoskins de-
parted with a vote of thanks, a few nuggets
and over £lOO in cash. That was 58 years
ago. The village of Hepburn to-day con-
sists of a State school and about 20 habita-
tions all told. One might sweep the Jim
Crow. ranges thereabouts with shrapnel and
not kill anything more human than a goat.
“Fax”: Mention of Marcus Clarke’s
widow (Marion Dunn) having played in
“Confusion” when it was first produced in
Melbourne, reminds me that she had then but
recently gone back to the stage. She retired
on her marriage, and did not get many
engagements after she returned. As a mat-
ter of fact she was never the popular actress
that vague reminiscencers have said she was.
Father John Dunn (born O’Donoghue)
ranked as a good comedian of the old-
fashioned sort, and sister Rosa Dunn, who
married L. L. Lewis, was a prime favorite ;
but Marion amounted only to “a worthy re-
presentative of the family name.” A fairly
pretty girl, with an early stage training
which made her efficient in burlesque and
comedy, she lacked the natural gift of mum-
mery. The same want of dramatic instinct
is apparent in her daughter, Marion Marcus
Clarke, now acting in the Niblo company.
“Midchannel”: The musing of “Masetto”
(B. 4/3/’l5) concerning Verdi and Wagner
discloses a distinct vacuum in his musical
learning. Verdi is as much indebted to
Wagner as “Bellerive” is to Byron. Any
Bulletin contributor who attends a series
of ops. by Wagner and Verdi, and is able
to detect sufficient similarity between them
to establish an indebtedness, is something
of a genius, or worse. With Wagner the
great task of the orchestra is to hold itself
sufficiently in check to give the singers a
chance of being heard. With Verdi one has
to look with a microscope for the instrumen-
talists, who just make enough sound to save
themselves being overlooked. Verdi .was a
slave to melody, and hadn’t quite grasped
the meaning of orchestration, or had very
little time or use for it, except in “Aida,”
“Otello” and “Ernani;” With Wagner, ex-
cept for an occasional slice of melody, the
orchestra is boss of the show and a very
heavy and wearisome boss at that. To be
able to pick out a spice of melody in a
thunderclap is a rare accomplishment, but to
detect a vein of resemblance between the
formidable Wagner and the light and airy
Verdi is a rarer one. The most brilliant ex-
ponent of orchestration plus melody is
Puccini. Possibly “Masetto” will file a bill
against this composer on behalf of Vogner.
N.S.W. Attorney-General Hall is a proud
man. He announces with a scream of joy
that he made £17,000 out of the Wheat Grab.
Asked who had the money he chuckled that
he knew who hadn’t got it—the middlemen.
Tiie Bulletin knows somebody else who
hasn’t got it —the farmers.
All the Dukes and Earls and Rothschilds
who were supposed to be quite small frag-
ments of the mighty Norton Griffiths Co.
■ fell out of the business in a heap the other
day. When the negotiations were still in
suspense nothing less than an Earl or sim-
ilar potentate was represented as chairman
of directors. Now the gentleman who
is representative of the concern in Aus-
tralia says the firm consists of one man,
Norton Griffiths, known as “Empire .Tack.”
The local representative’s name is Mayoh;
and the last time that name was before the
public it was in connection with a scheme
—which failed—to get the public to put up
enough money to build a pier and dancing-
hall at Manly. The dancing-hall may have
been part of the great movement towards
Empire solidarity, for which Mr. Norton
Griffiths has done so much.
A Sydney man has reached newspaper
notice through having been 50 years a bread-
carter, the last 21 years spent on the same
run. He doesn’t appear to have any ideas
of value about the war; but on the subject
of arbitration he has a word or two to say
to Those who often denounce it. Fourteen
years ago bread-carters received 365. a week
for covering a full run and doing odd jobs
in the bakehouse as well. Their hours of
toil were unlimited, except when some gen-
erous employer insisted that his hands
shouldn’t work more than 24 hours a day.
Then a union was formed, and arbitration
assisted it to get better conditions—in fact,
arbitration was the inducement to form the
union. The men got a wage of £2 ss. and
working hours were limited to 10. Now the
lowest wage is £2 12s. 6d. and the hours are
nine ; and the bread-carters seem to be ever-
lastingly explaining that there will be no
delivery to-morrow on account of some holi-
day or other.
Last week-end the Federal House of Reps,
closed with a riot about certain proposed
increases of salary to Parliamentary officials
and civil servants. The disturbance started
over a proposed rise for one man from £9OO
to £lOOO a year, but the total amount in-
volved is over £6OOO. It transpired that
the estimates now before the House were
compiled by Cook and adopted bv Fisher,
who waved aside as trifling the objections
raised to these advances. In the affairs of
a nation £6OOO a year is not much as an
ordinary thing, but in the face of a falling
revenue and the adoption of rather desperate
devices to keep going on even the old scale,
it becomes a matter of importance. If this
sum is officially regarded as of no consequence
in one direction it is likely to be similarly
regarded in others, and the multiplication of
6000 by a hundred or two may be disas-
trous. The Commonwealth is not in such a
position that it can regard thousands as tray-
bits. If it is, it ought to be ashamed of
itself for sponging on harassed John Bull
to the extent of £28,000,000 in one year.

Of Hamlets they had seen and heard,

Anent the mime he loved the best.

One Tree’s embonpoint Dane;
But Hughes, an old scene-shifter, spoke
In favor of an unknown bloke,
Hugh Montmorency Vane—
“ For when ’e starred as ’Amlet, then
We’d always done by ’arf-past ten !”

THE MICROBE OF OBESITY
CONQUERED AT LAST.
(EXTRACT FROM THE “DOCTOR
AND HIS FAMILY.”)
“Reductions of from one to two stone of
excessive fat in a few weeks’ time, and final
reduction to normal weight of extremely
fleshy persons, were reported at a recent
medical conference, when the subject of
obesity was discussed. The remarkable
effects obtained are attributed chiefly, to the
use of salith leaves, taken from sea pi an Is.
Their action increases the supply of oxygen
in the blood, and''thus consumes the excessive
fat. Physicians present . attached special
significance to the fact that by this sii ole
vegetable treatment, strength and yigor r.ve
greatly increased instead of being diminish :1.
Furthermore, the skin and tissues undergo
a natural contraction while the weight is
being reduced, so that no flabbiness ensues.
The use bf toxic drugs or weakening diets
was condemned as dangerous and undesir-
able. Any person suffering 1 from obesity
can easily get rid of excessive fat by obtain-
ing from a chemist about an ounce of
salith' leaves, and taking two before- each
meal. A noticeable , reduction >in iveight
takes place within a week cr 10 days.]’
P.S.—The final report evidently means the
final and complete, destruction of thej much
discussed microbe of obesity,*
SOCIETY
“De Yid” swears it’s a true bill: —
What a thinking animal is the female of the
species! One of her walked into the Adelaide Post
Office the other day to despatch a parcel to the
usual boy in Egypt. The official took it, weighed
it and asked the usual question regarding the con-
tents. The lady dimpled delightfully. “Oh, if
you must know, it’s just half a dozen fresh eggs.”
“But. we can’t forward perishables all that way,”
he gasped. She assured him that they wouldn’t
smash, as she’d packed them in wadding. “You
see,” she added, “Charlie’s been ill and in hospital,
and mother and I thought it would be so nice for
him to have a few fresh eggs.”
The commercial travellers who have been
holding an inter-State conference in Brisbane
gasped when one of their number proposed co-
operative buying of spirits, tobacco, cigars
and other creature comforts for the C.T.
clubs in all six States. To men who thrive
by the multiplicity of commissions the sug-
gestion was like a punch from a snowball.
The assurance that club expenses would be
brought down with a run by this purchasing
in bulk was only like heaping coals on the
fire. As a principle of political economy
co-operation was well enough, but applied to
working-day practice there was too little
money in it from the commission-grubber’s
point of view. And Victoria, which had the
temerity to fling the bombshell on to the
conference table, bowed its diminished head
before the remonstrances of four sister
States. “This threatens our very existence,”
tearfully pleaded president Bowcher, and
the “Hqar, hears” were like the sound of a
grand !
A note from a man who had just finished
the washing-up: —
II the overworked idea of the street collection
perishes of insufficient nourishment after this war,
the few sane people left will heave the biggest sigh
of relief. First there .was Hospital : Saturday ; then
Wattle Day came with another crowd of “helpers”
and social advertisers. Then, lately, in quick succes-
sion, the city, which represents a. State.cracked by
drought, was afflicted with Belgian Flag Day and
Rose Day. The motive behind it all is good; but
the total cash result is wretchedly poor for the
boots, motor tyres and cheek expended. What annoys
the thinking citizen is that, while real work goes
begging, there never seems to be any lack of
“assistants” when the job consists of running about
the streets from early morn to late afternoon rattling
collection-boxes in the faces of strange men. But
let a weary house-mother advertise that she is pre-
pared to pay good wages and supply first-class hoard
and lodging to anyone who will help with the home
and kids, and she is lucky if she gets an application
from one she-autocrat, If the lady-beggars really
want to boost the patriotic funds, let them hire
out their affability and boundless energy in the little
homes where there is real women’s work, and pour
their wages into the charitable coffers. Certainly it
might cause complications at the Trades Hall; but
before Mary Ann could make an effective protest,
she would have to prove that she really existed and
was ready to sign on.
Tlie University of California lias been
raking together statistics of the weight? of
infants coming to light in Britain, Australia
and the U.S. The average at birth of 247
males born in the Commonwealth was
127.30 z., and that of 234 females 121.20 z.
One thousand males born in the Lambeth
Hospital (London) averaged 113.80 z., and
1000 females 113.20 z. The usual weight of
little Stars and Stripes is 120.50 z. and of
sister 115.70 z. All of which adds to the
proof of the deterioration of the Bullpup
breed in the climate of Australia.
“Bloggs” : Where is Truth? In the lead-
ing columns of the dailies you read that
Germany is on the verge of insolvency.
Meat is short, likewise bread. ■ The wealthy
classes are leaving for Denmark, Norway
and other neutral countries in order to
lighten the strain on the local supply of
necessaries. The most fantastic devices are
in use to maintain the importation of
petrol and rubber. These articles come in,
in small quantities, via Barcelona and
Switzerland, on whose German borders they
are collusively stolen by the Kaiser’s emis-
saries. The picture of want and despera-
tion is impressive, and it is presented about
six times weekly. Yet last Saturday,
tucked away in a corner of one of the
same dailies, was this:—
A motor-car exhibition has just been opened in
the largest hall in the world, erected on the Kaiser-
damm in Berlin. According to the Tagliche Runds-
chau, 8000 electric lamps of 300,000-candle power
. are required to illuminate this great building.
If it is true that Berlin is staging such a
motor-car exhibition after nine months of
war —well, it’s a long, long way to Potsdam.

“Mariner”: As a precaution during war
time, the Admiralty inaugurated an “exami-
nation service” at all “defended ports” in
the Empire. For the third time this year
the examining steamer at Sydney has col-
lided violently with a vessel which was
being bailed up. All the bumps have
occurred between the Heads, and the one
last week nearly sent both vessels to the
bottom. Devout seamen bound to Sydney
nowadays chant the following litany before
grog: “From lightning and tempest, from
shipwreck and sudden death, from Germans
and the examination steamer, good Lord
deliver us.”
George Black, the new Chief Secretary of
N. S. Wales, has succeeded in his advocacy
of the claims of the Sydney street speaker,
and Holman announces that nobody will be
prosecuted who keeps his oratory in reason-
able check and doesn’t disorganise the tram
service. This seems only decent in the light
of the fact that citizens who can’t speak in
public, and haven’t any ideas to express, if
they could, are allowed to wander as slowly
as ruminating cows along the city pavements
and hold up anybody who really has busi-
ness to do. This aimless street-mooching on
the part of well-dressed loafers might very
well be regulated. Any person under 40
who walked in the street at less than three
miles an hour should do so only between the
tram rails. There might be a mess in the
middle of the streets for a day or two, but
the new rule would smarten up wonderfully
any loafers who were left.
Up Inverell (N.S.W.) way lately a fettler
was on night duty at a bridge undergoing
repairs, his job being to signal trains
to slow down while crossing. Trains were
scarce during that shift, and it was a long,
dreary wait for the first —the mail. The
monotonous cacophony of the frogs in the
creek made the man drowsy ; so he impro-
vised a bunk from bags and sundries close
up to the line and arranged an alarm to
awaken him in time to signal the train. In
due course the- alarm sprang—m—m—m—
rattle—bang. . . .But the sleeper didn’t hear.
The medical man said death was quite in-
stantaneous. The watcher had gone to sleep
with his head on the rail, confident that the
vibration of the approaching train would
alarm him in time. Eternity held the road
that trip.
Adelaide has woke to the fact that its
- chief hospital is altogether out of date, and
a deputation last week asked the new
Government to rebuild. Tinkering with the
present buildings, it is urged, would be false
economy. The statement was freely made
that many private hospitals in the city are
better equipped. Chief Secretary Styles
agreed offhand that something big must be
done, but to “scrap” all the big pile stand-
ing is a heavy order. The hospital has
the benefit of a Martin bequest. Half of
this money is in leased property, which
should be of much larger value shortly; but
about £32,000 cash is in hand, and this is
offered to the Government for a start. A
new and thoroughly up-to-date place might,-
however, cost anything from ' : £200,000 up-
wards ; and S. Australia hasn’t yet con-
tracted Holman’s habit of waving a lordly
hand and saying: “Do the thing properly!”
trusting to luck to find the money.
THE OLD STOCKMAN’S LAMENT.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
AVrop me up in me stockwhip and blanket,
And bury me deep down below,
AVhere this piffle and sham won’t disgust me,
In the land where the coolibahs grow ;
For I’ve stayed with some well-to-do people,
And I’ve dined with some middle-class folk;
And I’ve sorrowed by clock-tower and steeple
Till my heart for the Commonwealth’s broke.
They have flown in another direction,
AVho ured to clack-clack by the hour
Of “this awful, un-British Protection,”
Of “our dear friend the member in power,”
And the Higher Religion for Dossers, .
And the Need of an Object for Drunks.
Now they’re all of them Red or Blue Crossers,
AA’ith their tails sticking out of their trunks.
There are citified Martins in dozens—
The Darling' Point Martins the pick—•
Who used to be horrified cousins
Of a Martin we knew as “Mad Mick.”
He is hanging out somewhere where French is;
And they heard he’d enlisted —somehow.
It would paralyse Mick in the trenches
To know how he’s glorified now.
You remember the George Henry Crosses?
They’ve packed up twelve trunks in despair.
He’s the boss of the back-station bosses,
-And Ernie’s the son and the heir.
He .has never put hands on a wether,
Nor heard a pithed store-bullock grunt;
So they’re taking the mailboat to England
To guard Ernie safe to the Front.
And each of the war-going, parsons
, Costs many a heart-breaking tear —-
Dike that caddish young cub of old Carson’s,
All found and four hundred a year.
He feels not a word that he preaches,
But he won’t be criticised there;
As he drones, where the flying shell screeches,
Poor Tommy will shiver and swear.
“Our relatives, too” (plague the Censor!)
Each girl has a tear on her cheek.
Cousin Roger has gone as dispenser
(Expenses and three pounds, a week.
More risky than listening to sermons,
. As some of our fellows will find,
In a fierce fortnight’s fight with the Germans
In front —and with Roger behind!)
And the girls, they are writing like blazes,
And Auntie is moaning like H—•;
And -I wish I was under the daisies—
Or the cowdung would do just as well.
So I want to be wropped in me blanket,
- And buried down- —deep down —below ;
AVhere this cant and this cackle won’t reach me—
In the land where the coolibahs grow.
rbs.w.
HENRY LAWSON.

The French ships Chateaubriand and
General de Sonis have been faithful com-
panions in misfortune. Two years ago both
went ashore on the South Aus. coast within
three months of each other. Both were
refloated, towed to Melbourne, and docked
there./ And now both have been finally dis-
posed of within a few days of each other
by German pirate submarines.

“Letusee”: Re “Jermyn Street” and his
remarks on French wines being denied the
unlucky German from now henceforth and
for ever more, I sco ff. For the past 50
years the great weinhandlers of Austro-
S U nffLV V V" p P lied France with about
50 pei cent, of those velvet clarets,” deli-
cious hocks and dry champagnes, the Rheis-
ivbf\ a “ d T ° ther nes of delicate flavors, of
which _ Jermyn Street” has such loving
memories. The consumption of wine in
Euiope long ago outran the possible output
ot h rencli vignerons, who, being wise, would
not spoil their markets by putting out im-
mature stock; so they had recourse to im-
portation from the communal weinhandlers
or Austro-Hungary, who have too much
respect for the grape to send poor stock out
or the country If Australia would only
adopt their methods we might hope for wines
or the same quality. The peasant grower
who owns from a half to an acre of land,
has his own little press. He takes his must
to the communal cellars, or to private cel-
lars more rarely, and has it tested by three
experts, sometimes four. On their reports
the grower is paid—according to quality. A
prize is given annually to the grower who
brings in the best must. No matter how
gieat and how influential the weinhandler
may be, his vats, cellars and distilleries are
liable to surprise visits, and the Law keeps
its eye skinned to detect any adulteration,
any lack of cleanliness, or any marketing
of immature juice. In several of the great
cellars doors still bear unbroken the Govern-
ment seal set upon them at the time of the
I lanco-Prussian war. If the Common-
wealth had the interests of this industry
at heart it would follow suit and seal up
cellars at the conclusion of each harvest,
and keep, them sealed for periods varying
from four to 10 years.
‘Coot”: Why keep the Australian bantams
back? Sir S. Pembrey, lecturer on physi-
ology at Guy’s Hospital, London, recently
shattered the big-man idol into many frag-
ments. He declares that small men make
the more efficient soldiers, for the following
reasons:—-
The essential organs are in the head and the
trunk, and these are often better developed in the
short than in the tall man, the weight of the brain
being relatively greater in the short man, and the
reaction .time not so long. Tall men of full • pro-
portions are heavy and often slow, and there are
strong physiological reasons for the greater agility
and activity of the small man, who does not Suffer
from the mechanical disadvantages of Height and
weight. The small man has a greater capacity for
work and endurance, and has stronger resistance to
disease.
Anyhow, nothing is surer than that the
attempt to measure up the qualities of people
of different countries by the same physical
standard is idiotic. The wire and-whipcord
man of Queensland may have no “chest”
worth mentioning; but lie could >put most
tubbier Englishmen to bed after a tough
day’s work.
“Y.Z.”: In Collins-street, Melbourne,
on an afternoon of last week a man
chased another about 20yds. along the
block, and smote him violently on the
hat. The man whose chapeau was
assaulted turned suddenly, whereupon the
assailant bashed him on the nose. Consterna-
tion and curiosity in the face of the man
who had been attacked gave way to plain
annoyance, and lie drew off, and butted his
left against the aggressor’s jaw, knocking
out a parcel of teeth. The teeth slid across
the ..pavement, and remained with No. I’s
hat in the gutter, grinning vacantly. No. 1
tried to- cross No. 2; No. 2 blocked, and
coming in with his knee up fouled No. 1
awfully.., The. gasp No. 1 gave when that
knee shifted his wind was like the cough of
a dying horse. No. 2 was still busy. He
tyas 'a brisk in-fighter, and a neat hdok put
No'.- I- down. No. 2 went down with him,
and proceeded to press his advantage home.
He punched No. I with all his hands ; then,
gathering in the ears of the man :| under,
rapped his head smartly on the pavement.
Every moment I expected to hear something
give. . Among the spectators during most of
this time was a tallish fair lady, the most
composed of all. She waited till" No. 2 had
rapped No. I’s head nine times. Then she
placed a gloved hand on No. I’s shoulder. “Ex-
cuse me,” she said sweetly, “but you are the
wrong man.” No. 1 looked at her in some
confusion for a moment. “Oh, am I?” he
said with some diffidence. “I am sure I beg
your pardon.” “Not at all,” said the lady,
“my mistake.” Then No. 1 gathered up his
hat and went on, and several kindly people
took up No. 2 and his teeth, and bore him
into a chemist’s shop.
INEXPENSIVE.
MRS. JONES: “Oh, I think your frock is
lovely. Did it cost you viuch? ”
MRS. SMITH: “Only one good cry."
The Clerks’ Union of Australia has been
talking about it for a long time, and now
seriously threatens to haul the employers
to the Federal Arbitration Court. At one
time the N. S. Wales Union , was a large
and energetic body, with a prospect of doing
many things; but there was a cabal which
took charge and wrecked the show so badly
that it only recently got off the rocks. The
unions in Victoria and Westralia have been
the only ones so far able to score an award.
The Victorians got a minimum wage of 565.
prescribed for a 48-hour week; but they
intend to ask now for 755. for a week of 38
hours. All the States’ unions are linked
up ; but that alone won’t get over the main
difficulty, which will be to satisfy the Court
that there is a Federal dispute.
A SONG OF THANKSGIVING.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Our servant is going-
She’s leaving next week!
Her bragging, her blowing,
Her loafing - , her cheek,
Her pre-nuptial sewing,
Her sneers and her pique,
Had driven us dotty.
A problem too knotty
At last she became, and we just had to speak !
Our servant is going—
She’s leaving next week.
Our servant is leaving; '
We’ve sacked her at last!
You bet we’re not grieving,
No flag’s at half-mast;
Her fine trousseau weaving
We stopped with one blast.
She called the place “rotten,”
And bit at her cotton ;
She mumbled “Good riddance!” and sewed
very fast—-
Our tyrant is leaving,
The crisis is past!
N.S.W.
GWENDOLYN GOODHEAVENS.

Frederick Halcomb, M.A., Clerk of the
8. Aus. Parliament, maintains the tradition
that Parliamentary officials live long. He
was born in Wiltshire 79 years ago, gradu-
ated at Oxford, and became Parliamentary
librarian at Adelaide 45 years back. When
the late E. G. Blackmore was appointed
Clerk of the Commonwealth Parliaments, in
3901. Halcomb stepped up to his present
billet.
“Vegetarian”: I saw it reported that
.T. H. Mclntosh, recently killed by natives
of the Lakekamu River district (Papua),
was cooked and eaten. This is incorrect.
Poor Mclntosh was decapitated by three
savages for loot, and his body was left on
the ground,
where it was
found by some
other natives
and buried. Can-
nibalism is com-
mon in many
of the remote
inland parts of
the country, but
not so in
the mountains
where this mur-
der occurred.
Mclntosh was
one of the clev-
erest prospectors
amongst the
callable Austra-
lians here, and
had the largest
copper mine
thus far un-
earthed in the
Territory— the
Laloki. With
only a few sav-
ages for corn-
pan y, some
tinned stuff, a
pick, shovel and
dish, l:e would
wander through
the worst of
hostile highlands
on the quest
for metal. And
when lie had
found it he
mostly gave it
away to some-
body down on
his luck.
ASHFORD ,
N.S.W. Minister
of Lands.
Died at Willoughby (Sydney), at 82,
Henry Thomas, who came of an enterprising
family. liis grandfather was killed at Tra-
falgar ; his father went down in the Erebus
with John Franklin’s Arctic expedition; he
himself was wounded at Sebastopol (Crimea)
at the age of 17. Ilenrv came to Australia
in 1860, and had 55 years of peace, anyhow.
Ricciotti Garibaldi, at one time truant
officer of the Victorian Education Depart-
ment, was in London a month ago seeking
to raise £240,000 to equip a Garibaldian
legion to fight with the French. Says Ric-
ciotti : “You cannot conceive the stimulating
effect of 30,000 red shirts on the field of
battle. They will arouse the imagination of
the Southerners and make their warm blood
tingle. We must have them —the men and
the shirts. I shall wear a red shirt myself.
I am condemned to crutches, but I am unite
comfortable on horseback. I refuse to grow
old. J am only a little antique. I shall
take command without the least trepidation.”
On the strength of his not-many-years in
these parts, Garibaldi always claims _to be
an Australian. Six sons he has in this war
—or, rather, had. Four only are loft now.

“Darbalara” (William Burton) Smith, a
well-known N. S. Wales squatter, who died
recently in a private hospital, was eldest
son of William Kempton Smith, of Bon-
gongo, for many years manager of Ur.
Nicholson’s runs in Iliverina. The last
property W. Iv. Smith owned was Darba-
lara, in the Gundagai district, which
passed some 45 years ago to the son, who
retained it till 190(5. lie was one of the
oldest members of the Union Club, founded
in the ’so’s, and of the A..J.C.
The Peacock Government has one virtue,
at any rate: it appoints Australians instead
of importing alleged experts from abroad.
The Chief Commissioner of Railways was
so selected, and now, irrigation expert
Elwood Mead having resigned, senior com-
missioner Cattanach steps into his shoes.
The new chief was originally a bank clerk,
and then town clerk at Flemington and
Essendon; and he knows most about the
financial side of irrigation and closer settle-
ment. But as Mead was one of the State’s
few successful importations, Cattanach 1 as
but to carry out the policy the Yankee
planned and he won’t go far wrong in other
departments of the business.
“Chasuble” : Exit Bishop Moorhouse. He
found that the Victorian diocese had got
mouldy under Bishop Perry, who had
arrived in 1847 by the ship Stag, about the
same time as the R.C. Bishop Goold c-ame
overland from Sydney in his carriage with
four horses. Both were of the conservative
order. Carr’s energising after Goold was
like Moorhouse’s after Perry. The first
thing that disgusted Moorhouse _ was St.
James’s wretched cathedral, with its pepper-
caster tower. Forthwith he determined on
a new fane. First ensued a struggle over
the site. Perry had stupidly fixed the
Cathedral to be in aristocratic East Mel-
bourne. Moorhouse at once said it must be
at St. Paul’s, in the thick of Melbourne
traffic. lie had not only to lick the
East Melbourne barrackers, but also those
who wanted the Cathedral at St. Peter’s,
cheek-by-jowl with St. Patrick’s, on that
hill. He won ; yet without Baronet Clarke
and Ormond, tiie Presbyterian, he would
have fared much less well. Ilis foundation
of Trinity College, under Deeper, is trium-
phant, too, for it has already turned out six
bishops —Green of Ballarat, Armstrong of
Wangaratta. Stretch of Newcastle, Long of
Bathurst, Sadleir'of Maoriland, and Stephen
of Hobart. In manner Moorhouse was
Napoleonic and not beloved by the clergy.
But what of that if he could command their
obedience? And he could do that and more:
he even attracted Kent formerly of the
Congregationals, and Bailhache from the
Baptists; and in Kent’s case it was after
that preacher had attacked Moorhouse s
High Churchism.
From “Tlie Oldest Inhabitant”: —
1 think Mrs. Isabella Gorman, who died at Potts
Point on March 26, was the oldest living native of
N. S. Wales. A daughter of the late William
Johnston, who, with his wife, arrived in Sydney
from Scotland in August, 1817, she was born in
Sydney on July 30, 1819. In the following year
Johnston was appointed manager of the Government
farm at Bathurst and crossed the Mountains with
his family. In 1840 daughter Isabella married John
Gorman, a surveyor, afterwards a partner of T. S.
Mort’s, the founder of the Australian frozen-meat
business.
Harold Perdriau, who died the other
day in Sydney, was 50 years ago appren-
ticed to Mort’s Dock, and then joined his
father, who was the pioneer of the Balmain
steam ferry service. Later lie became a
partner in the Balmain ferries with his
brother Henry, the founder years later of
the Perdriau Rubber Co. On the ferries
being taken over by a company, Harold
Perdriau established an engineering works
with Mr. T. West. They purchased the
enormous punt that had been used in plac-
ing the spans of the Hawkesbury Bridge in
position, had it towed to Sydney and con-
verted it into a floating dock, now known as
Drake’s. In later years lie was foreman at
the Clyde works, and retired from that din
only a few months ago.
The just-dead Bishop Moorliouse spent
10 of his 89 years of life in Melbourne—
-1876-8o—and0 —and then left to become Bishop of
Manchester. A yarn told of his early days
in Victoria started in a railway carriage.
A wealthy Western squatter invited the
Bishop to stay with him during a pastoral
visitation of those parts, and Dr. Moor-
house accepted. He left Melbourne in one
of the old oblong saloon carriages, seats
all round. At Geelong a noisy lot of
squatters got in. The Bishop was obscured
by an open newspaper. The sheep and
cattle men were vigorous in language, so
vigorous that the tall, silent figure in the
corner rustled his newspaper to call “order,”
as it were. The western men were abashed
when they discovered the Gaiters, and the
squatter to whose house he was proceeding
introduced himself and apologised for the
“language.” “You know, my Lord,” he said,
“in this part of the country we call a spade
a spade.” “Do you?” said Moorliouse. “I
would have thought you called it a •
shovel.” He was no wowser. He smoked a
pipe, played a first-class game of billiards,
promenaded with a well-bred bulldog at his
heels, and had a tiff with his Synod once
because he invited a well-known actor to give
Shakspearian readings in the drawing-room
at Bishopscourt. A coincidence relating to
the first three Bishops of Melbourne
Perry, Moorhouse and Field Flowers Goe—
is that each, though married, was childless.

James Johnson, the only person who was
saved when the Dunbar piled herself up at
the Gap, just outside Sydney Heads, on
August 20, 1857, died last week. For no
great merit Johnson was given a job in the
lighthouse Department; and when nine
years later the Cawarra piled herself up on
the Oyster Bank at the entrance to New-
castle. the only survivor, F. W. Hedges, was
rescued by this only survivor of the Dunbar.
The loss in the first wreck was 119 lives ;
in the Cawarra disaster 59.
CLARENCE GOODE, Minister of Lands in
the new S. Ans. Government.
The mystery of the last will and testa-
ment of Nordica, the singer, is to be probed
at Thursday Island by a special U.S. Com-
missioner. who is the American vice-consul
in Melbourne. Nordica’s second will, dated
1914, is said to have been drawn up during
her stay at Thursday Island, and it differs
so strangely from her original will, dated
1910, that the U.S. Court has decided to
give the previous document a chance. In
1910 Nordica bequeathed all her £lOO,OOO
estate to her husbahd, but at Thursday
Island she cut him off with a small rever-
sion, or something, though they were writ-
ing loving letters to one another all the
time of her concert tour. Hubby therefore
is now battling for probate of the first will,
whilst his wife’s relatives are hanging on
to will No. 2. Vice-consul llartlett is in-
structed to examine witnesses who are
said to have been present when Mrs. Nor-
dica changed the destination of her dollars.
But he may find the position complicated
by the fact that the doctor who could best
certify to the lady’s state of health at the
time was so unreasonable as to die of blood-
poisoning the other day.

do.” —Sven Haclen.
He’d paint a picture, write a play,
And make an opera —sing it, too.
All things he could do ; but to-day
Grim ruin stands athwart his way
Because of things he can’t undo.
Young Itussen, of Launceston (Tas.),
who has gone to the Front, went under
circumstances that would make a decent
story. He did not like to leave Ins father
in bad health, so lie stuck to the Kussen
biscuit factory till the “governor —a we t-
known figure in Launceston died. tie
went to his father’s funeral on a bund ay.
But his hands were still not free lie
was engaged to be married. The girl, how-
ever, was willing; so, the day after the
funeral, young Itussen had a quiet wedding.
The next day was Wednesday, and that
day Itussen started for the Front.
J. D. Fitzgerald is the new president of
the N. S. Wales P.L.L., which is nominally
the Board of Directors of the Parliamentary
Labor party. A man more unlike hats old
idea of a political Labor boss —a man with
hobnailed boots, bowyangs and a clay-pipe
—could not be conceived. To begin with.
Jack Fitzgerald was a compositor.
Encouraged by his elder brothers, the two
big-hearted, modest Australian bushmanwiio
ran Fitzgerald’s Circus, he roamed over
Europe, and, having taken some interest m
Labor matters, saw a good deal ot John
Burns, and fell much under Ins influence.
Returning to Sydney he was carried into
Parliament on the first big Labor wave. But
he did not like Parliament half as much as he
thought he would. Labor-in-politics was
also more or less disappointing, and tor a
good many years he took only a nlMwso-
pliieal interest in the proceedings. .He
admitted to the Bar very early in hist public
career; but he decided lie wasnt cut out
for a fighting advocate either A bal ' l ' ist< ;*
who has devoted himself mainly to literary
work, a club-man who knows everv mctuie
gallerv in Europe, the gentle litzgeialci
has devoted himself to what may be called
the artistic side of democracy, and is much
more interested in town-planning and such
things than in Bill’s dirt money.
HIS PLACE IN HISTORY.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Who shall ride the horse of Odin?
Who shall swing from gallows tree?
Who shall reap the thing he sowed in
Wind-fields wide and free?
Shall valkyries, loudly calling,
Bear him up on pinions strong?
His shall be as deep a falling
As the rope is long!
IIo! The sun has lit the morning!
No death-woman comes a-near;
Odin’s brow is dark, and scorning
This pale thing of fear..
He by whom the peace was broken,
He who lit the fires of shame,
What shall be the sign and token
Of his blood-soiled name?
Ho! The sun is up and strtngled
In the black the death clouds fling!
Earth forgets the corpse that dangled
From a greasy string.
N.S.W.
D. M. W.

Amalgamated Pictures, Ltd.: —

Feb., 1915..11,219 10 p.c.= 1200 10 p.c.= 6070 67,190
This co. was formed primarily to take over
the moving-picture connection of the Tait
brethren in Victoria. Subsequently it pur-
chased the Australian rights in the kinema-
color film process and also acquired inter-
ests in various Victorian picture shows, the
idea being to make the “circuit” which every
amusement caterer nowadays aims at. But
the more money the Taits and other Aus-
tralian managers made by these and other
economies and devices the more the film-
makers wanted from them. The Australian
managers, presently got tired of bidding
against one another for the benefit of the
foreigners, and formed what is known as*the
Combine —really two cos., one to hire films
out; the other to show films. Amalgamated
Pictures, Ltd., threw itself into this Com-
bine. It sold its plant, etc., for shares and
debentures in the two Combine cos., and it
leased its theatres to them. It received
43,808 fl shares in each of the cos. That
was so much more than the book value of
the assets handed over that, in order to
adjust matters, those assets had to be in-
flated with hot air, as shown in the middle
of the table. For that reason the nominal
reserves of £07,190 needn’t be regarded very
seriously. As nearly as The Bulletin can
make out the hot air represents £67,616;
and, as that may be accepted as at any rate
approximately correct, it is also fairly cer-
tain that there are no real reserves at all
—or none worth mentioning. In other
words, the tangible assets are only about
sufficient to represent the capital.
Put it this way. Amalgamated Pictures’
liabilities are £15,956. Cash and book debts
(chiefly, of course, dividends uncollected
from the Combine cos. or advances made to
them) amount to £16,446. So the capital
is represented by a surplus of £490 of liquid
assets and by freeholds and leaseholds
valued at £40,052; leaving £32,168 to be
represented by the shares in the Com-
bine cos., debentures issued by them
(£11,718). and shares in subsidiary
cos. at Northcote, Moonee Ponds and
Geelong (face value £3700). Of the
more substantial part of the assets —the
£40,052 represented by real property—no
less than £31.597 is leasehold (the Majestic
Theatre). No doubt a great deal of this
value is practically goodwill, too. In other
words, if the bottom fell out of the picture
business most of the £31.597 would escape,
as well as most if not all of the value of the
goodwill of the Combine cos. and the sub-
sidiary cos. Meantime the profits are all
right. Had it been permissible they could
have paid 15 p.c. all round last year; and
then one doesn’t know how much profit the
Combine cos. made in excess of the dividends
they actually paid to this and other cos. Nor
is there any visible reason for expecting
that the corpulent profits will drop out of
the business. Certainly there will be keener
competition: but the fact that the keenest
competition is going to be felt in Melbourne
won’t affect this co. particularly, since the
Combine is inter-State. though the Taits’
own ventures were all Victorian.
Harking back to the leasehold, a sinking
fund is provided to amortise this asset.
Apparently £4OO a year is bitten out of
profits. It is invested in the picture busi-
ness, which seems the only imprudent thing
about the transaction.

When the co. was formed late in 1911 the
chief question to consider was whether the
assets were overvalued. “Steamships,
lighters and hulks, properties, plant, stocks
and stores” were said to be worth £680,653.
This apparently was the valuation of the
vendors; anyway, so far as the prospectus
disclosed, nobody else committed himself to
figures. On the basis of that valuation the
surplus of assets was £751.404. which the
present co. paid for with 375.000 cumulative
pref. shares and 375,000 ordinaries, all of
£1 fully paid. The public took 125,000
profs, and 125,000 ordinaries. Even on this
capital of £1.000,000, and assuming that the
extra £250,000 got in on flotation would not
earn anything, the average certified profits
for the previous five years held out much
comfort to the new shareholders. After
writing 6 p.c. p.a. off for depreciation of
vessels at their cost, and after excluding in-
terest paid on borrowed money, Troup, Har-
wood and Co. said the proprietary had been
averaging £71,713 —just enough to pay 8 p.c.
on the ordinaries after giving the prefs. their
6 p.c. But though the public put up
£250,000, which allowed the co. to repay
nearly £200,000 of deposits, besides reducing
other liabilities, the profits haven’t in the
past two years reached anything like the cer-
tified average. And even the profits set
out in the table may be overstated. Any-
way, £2746 of the profit for the past year
is really an addition to the Insurance Fund;
and an insurance fund may be anything—-
an accumulation against a risk still current,
or a stocking to drop redundant Fat into.
Huddart Parker haven’t got so much redun-
dant fat that they should want to hide any
of it.
The reserves credited in the table are:
Formal reserve. £4502 : insurance, £23.6->5;
undivided profit. £11,062; total. £39,219.
Besides that there is a reserve “for contin-
gencies. depreciation of properties and shares
in other cos., repairs and maintenance,
boilers and renewals, superannuation fund,
etc.” Its total was £30,507 twelve months
ago. Now it is £3(5,185, or £5678 more.
The object of thus lumping things —of mix-
ing up a superannuation fund with a fund
for renewals —is of course to prevent the
public ascertaining definitely what the co.’s
position is. But this a t least can be said;
if £5678 is all the provision made for meet-
ing 12 months’ wear and tear, as well as
providing for contingencies and a super-
annuation fund, then the bite out of the
profits isn’t any too much. On the published
figures, and granting that depreciation may
possibly have been written off direct (though
there is no trace of it anywhere), The
Bulletin should say that the true profit for
the year was substantially less rather than
more than £60,460.
Considering the capital employed, this is a
distinctly poor result. One or two things
can, however, be said in extenuation. The
co. has been putting into ships a lot of
money which has beeii dead for most of the
year. This comparison will show the 12
months’ changes in assets: —
Dec., 1913. Dec., 1914.
Ships, etc. . . £608,456 £857,376 Inc. £248,920
Cash . . . . 41,443 46,875 Inc. 5,432
Debts due to co. 74,888 69,306 Dec. 5)582
Investments . . 429,357 191,598 Dec. 237,709
Stores .. .. 39,695 34,926 Dec. 4, <69
£1,193,839 £1,200,081 Inc. £6,242
The co. also during the year took a hand in
floating the Hebburn Coal Co., and matters
have been unsettled in connection with that
property and the whole Newcastle trade.
The removal of these disturbing factors will
doubtless improve things. On the other
hand the co. most likely paid too much for
the old ship and other property. Further,
the certified average profit was for a period
when earnings had increased phenom-
enally. but expenses had not been
pushed up to correspond. Since 1912
wages, etc., have been overtaking the
tremendous rise in profits which
occurred, generally, between 1905 and that
year. The profits —still speaking generally
—for the latter part of the neriod were there-
fore quite fictitious: they had bolted ahead
of costs. The very awkward thing for those
who bought on that fictitious basis is that,
when the tide of prosperity runs out, as it
has been running out lately, costs won’t im-
mediately readjust themselves. They will
for 'a time be too high just as before they
were too low.

Caledonian Collieries, Ltd., which is
the coal-mining or most of the coal-mining
end of the Howard Smith business :

1915.-101,831 20,200 10 ~ „ 60,001 21,630 66,920
The Caledonian Coal Co., formed in 1895,
acquired sooner or later the AVest AA r allsend,
West Wallseud Extended, AVaratah, Aber-
dare and Aberdare Extended collieries at
Newcastle (N.S.W.). It was a Scotch con-
cern, into which the Howard Smith Co.
bought whenever the chance offered: and
that co. was also the managing agent in
Australia. Eventually Howard Smith’s
secured all the shares; whereupon Cale-
donian Collieries, Ltd., was formed with a
paid capital of £852,507 in 252,500 8 p.c.
cumulative pref. shares and 600,007 ordi-
naries. The Howard Smith Co. took the
ordinaries and 192,500 prefs. ; and the How-
ard Smith Co. shareholders individually
applied for 50,000, and were subsequently
allowed to have another 50.000 at Is. pre-
mium. The first balance-sheet, published a
year ago, showed a profit of £79,721 “after
providing for depreciation, amortisation and
preliminary expenses.” The total of the
preliminary exes, wasn’t mentioned; but
whatever it was it is plain, that, assuming
enough was written off against the wear and
tear of plant and wastage of the mining
property, the true net profit was more than
£79.721. The jump made by the past year’s
profit wasn't therefore as great as it looks —
again assuming that the writing-off was done
on the same basis in the two years. What
depreciation and other allowances were made
isn’t even remotely hinted at. All that can
be said on that point is that on Howard
Smith’s past record the amounts written off
are likely to be very ample, and the declared
profit is likely to understate the truth rather
than overstate it. Assuming that just
enough was struck off gross profits to. meet
all proper charges, then the year’s net profit
was equal to 134 p.c. on the ordinary shares,
after providing for the prefs. That the co.,
which had exceptional means of judging,
believed the nroperties were not overvalued
to begin with, and that they would yield
very large profits in excess of the pref
requirements, is fairly clear from t lie fact
that it was satisfied to hold no less than
£OOO,OOO of the capital in ordinary stock.
The area held by the Caledonian Co. just
before the transfer was 8025 acres of free-
hold and 9231 acres of leasehold; and pre-
sumably the present co. holds no less. The
financial position is very easy: liquid assets
exceed the liabilities.

The profit depends on the stocktaking; and
though there may be something wrong with
that in -one year, or even for three or four
years, it is almost inconceivable that any-
thing should be very wrong over a long
series of years. So it may be taken for
granted that Ivempthorne, Prosser & Co.
make, year in and year out, a comfortable
7 p.c., with a fair margin over after provid-
ing probably for all reasonable contingen-
cies. What provision actually is made The
Bulletin doesn’t know to a pound; but
there are signs that this is the sort of co.
which provides for all it can see or think of,
and then whips a bit up its sleeve to provide
for the things it can’t see or think of. For
instance, there is a reserve on customers’
accounts —a fund which has been standing
there for years to meet any extra bad luck in
the way of uncollectable debts. As it has
stood just where it is now for 10 years past,
it is plain that all the current bad debts
have been met, not out of the fund, but out
of current revenue.

In the 10 years £09,456 of new capital and
£8299 of pref. capital has been got in ; total
£77,755. All the extensions made during
the 10 years have been paid for out of that
money and the undivided profit; for, as
shown above, the debt to the public has been
actually reduced. And very fine extensions
there have been. Close up £50.000 must
have been put into the new Auckland acid
works alone —and, with the quick develop-
ment of agriculture handy to Auckland,
radiating from the State College and demon-
stration farm at Hamilton, there ought to
be a big future for that industry. We really
haven’t started to use chemical fertilisers in
Australasia yet; and Maoriland, with its
larger rainfall, will find them more valuable
even than the cockies over the Common-
wealth side.
# * * *
Skinners, Ltd. (Melbourne). —As plainly
indicated at the time the prospectus was
issued, this co. should never have been
formed —at any rate, on the basis submitted.
The late Henry Skinner had carried on a
catering business, and for the goodwill and
a trifle of substantial assets no less than
£25,000 was paid in shares as well as an
utterly ridiculous £16,500 in cash. The good-
will was obviously worth hardly anything:
the man who had made the business was
dead, and it was almost wholly a catering-
connection which was liable to be lost at any
time. This actually happened. The direc-
tors say :
Mr. Henry Greville entered into competition with
the co., and secured much of the work and contracts
previously carried out by the late Henry Skinner,
M.L.C., whose business the co. acquired. In order
to meet this competition prices had to be cut, pre-
miums increased, and many more hands employed
at racecourses and elsewhere.
The co. was beaten almost before it was born,
and sale was compulsory. “The best price
that could be obtained was £25,000 in shares
and £2600 in cash, but of this amount £5OOO
in shares was contingent.” This now is the
position. The public put up £14.146 in cash,
and 25,000 £1 shares were issued to the
vendors and promoters; total, £39,146. To
represent that the co. now has 18.943 shares
in Greville, Ltd. —a co. in 75,000 shares —and
£3182 in book debts (including £2600 cash
to be received from Greville’s). But the
co. owes £9702; so, assuming the Greville
shares are worth 205.. the co. has as its
entire net assets 12,423 shares in Greville’s.
Summed-up:—•
Loss on sale .. .. £18,900
Loss on trading . . 7,823
Balance left (in Greville
shares) .. • ■ 12,423
Total original capital.. £39,146
The monstrous thing is that, of this pom-
little £12,423, representing about a one-sixth
interest in Greville, Ltd., 64 p.c. is owned by
the vendors of the Skinner business and the
promoters or their successors in title. In
other words, all that the public which put up
£13,146 has is 4450 shares in Greville, Ltd.
Later on. they may get a few more shares ;
but that is contingent. Possibly it is use-
less to suggest to the people who got
£16.500 cash and 25.000 shares, mostly for a
business which nearly all melted at once into
thin air, that it would be a fair thing to can-
cel the whole or most of the 25.000 shares ;
but the suggestion is made, all the same.

It wasn’t all war either. For the first seven
months of the year revenue shrank £6<oo;
for the last five months, £I2,DUO. A loss
much greater than £7201 would have been
shown had not the amount spent on upkeep
been cut down further than usual.
The co. has a whole hive of troubles; but
the biggest of all is that the line serves a
mineral area of small or poor mines working
metals which are liable to violent fluctua-
tions. It was the success of the Silverton
(Broken Hill) Tramway Co. which spurred
the public to rush the Emu Bay prospectus
18 or 20 years ago. The crowd judged the
traffic a railway could get by the space the
mines were getting in the daily papers, and
the daily papers were, as usual, giving the
squibs whole columns. That is one trouble.
Another is that the promoters sinfully col-
lared £lOO,OOO in free shares at the start,
which overloaded the concern, and drove it
to get real capital on debentures. The in-
terest on this borrowed money is a first
charge. The third fly in the butter is that
the co. makes use of the Emu Bay and
Mount Bischoff Co.’s railway for part of
the distance covered; and the rent also
comes out before the shareholders have a
bite. Insect No. 4 consists of £lOO,OOO of
8 p.c. pref. capital, of which half (4 p.c.) is
cumulative. For two or three years past
this 4 p.c. has been paid, alohg with some
of the accumulations. Half the balance was
forgiven; the other half was funded, £5OO
to be provided annually towards buying the
“funded certificates.’’ Unfortunately the
paying-out of these dividends skinned the
cash-box, and when trouble came during the
past year the arrangement with the “funded
certificate” holders, though made only a
couple of years ago, had to go overboard,
and the debenture-holders had to go without
their interest. And there is really nothing
substantial on which to hang much hope for
the future. There are scores of mines on
the Tasmanian West Coast; but none that
are known are large, and the largest are
most refractory—-except Bischoff tin ; and
Bischoff tin is not worth much to the rail-
way, because the ore is reduced to metal on
the spot. But the thing will continue luring
people on ; for there is no siren in the world
like a refractory ore mine.

What sum may be properly regarded as
written off is open to discussion ; but, any-
how, the net profit, assuming a fair amount
was struck off against depreciation, was
£2464. This permitted alO p.c. dividend on
the very modest capital, and a rebate of com-
missions ranging from 10 to 20 p.c. The co.,
although it does a little manufacturing, is
mainly in the co-operative marketing busi-
ness. It and the Coastal Farmers have the
bulk of the distributing trade: and this at
least can be said: if they fix the price some-
times higher than the public thinks is a fair
thing, the actual producer benefits —there
isn’t a ravenous middleman scoffing as much
for 10 minutes’ work as the grower gets for
months of toil. Indeed, the butter business
of N. S. Wales is the best-organised of all
the primary industries ; and instead of fling-
ing rocks at the two companies which have
organised the marketing (and to a great
extent also the manufacture), this paper feels
inclined to go down on its marrow-bones and
implore them to take in hand also the fruit
business. Both producers and consumers
would certainly be tens of thousands to the
good every year. As usual, this co.’s liabili-*
ties are not heavy, owing to the excellent
policy of insisting on cash or fortnightly pay-
ments.
