The year lias been one of records. Profits
are the largest since the co. was formed;
the amount paid in dividend is the largest;
the lay-by is the smallest since the third
year of the co.’s life. Of a profit of £13,713
only a poor little £l3O is put by to swell the
very small reserves. Further, the amount
required for the 7 p.c. dividend tears along,
and it looks highly probable that profits will
no longer be capable of supporting the very
premature 7 p.c. dividend. The co. was born
in 1807, and consequently doesn’t know
what trouble is. Hence, while other com-
panies which have been through the mill are
piling up reserves, this youngster has been
leading the fast life, satisfied that trouble
could never come its way. Advances on
mortgages, etc., amount to £214,120, so that
ic wouldn’t require a phenomenal slump to
eat up the whole £9883 of reserves.
Advances made last year totalled £46,799,
so there are quite a lot of new risks —-always
the most dangerous—to safeguard. To put
things on a better footing the dividend
should be reduced. And shareholders
should all the more willingly consent to this
because its propriety is mainly due to the
exceptionally rapid increase of business.
White Crystal, Ardlethan, had a meeting
at Ballarat the other day, and the cheerful
chairman “anticipated that they would soon
find the company on the dividend list,”
although the co. has made a hobby of call-
making, and has only “practically £lOOO to
credit after allowing the value of concen-
trates on hand.” A new lease was bought
for £1250, of which £6OO was due on March
1; but as it wasn’t paid and the vendors
now refuse to accept the money, the new
lease represents mostly litigation. The
White Crystal raised £30.000 from the pub-
lic by the sale of 60,000 shares ; yet it actu-
ally started life in debt, and, mostly as a
result, it is going to spend its days in
sorrow.
* * * *
Great Cobar aoparently is not too anxious
or else is unable to resume operations even
at the present tempting price of copper.
When giving evidence in support of an appli-
cation for suspension of labor on the co.’s
Cobar Gold and Chesney leases in the begin-
ning of March, Hogan Taylor, the mana-
ger, handed round a nicely type-written
synopsis of operations which he intended to
carry out as soon as money became available.
All looked very nice on paper, and Bill the
Shaftman spent 6d. on a pint and looked
hopefully ahead for an early resumption of
wages. But up to the present nothing
new has happened. When money was
available, Taylor said, their intentions
were to put five men on Cobar Gold
Mine, 30 on Chesney, and 120 on Great Cobar
for about three months, and after that he
anticipated having sufficient men on to raise
and treat approximately 1000 tons Cobar
Gold, 300 tons Chesney and 4500 tons of
Great Cobar ore per week. Just before sus-
pension expired in the latter end of last
February, a cable was received from London
to the effect that Tellew Harvey’s recom-
mendations had been accepted, and permis-
sion of the English Court would be obtained
to enable the mines to reopen. Since then
apparently all hands have gone to sleep, and
Great Cobar has been forgotten. The local
management says it is in possession of no
now facts, so when suspension is applied for
again in a few weeks the Mines Department
might hint that it is about time somebody
woke up and did something.
Vegetable Creek (N.S.W.) is becoming a
bit of a favorite with some punters who
fancy tin shares. It has been badly handi-
capped by its debt to the landlord; but it
has a fair chance of paying dividends in
the long run —if the tin market holds. Any
concern with a large capital or share
market value and a small output of say 200
or 300 tons of tin oxide a year is a gamble
depending on high prices.
Some suspicious mining flotations are now
being attempted in Westralia. Here is the
latest. Gibraltar: Nominal capital. £30.000
in 60,000 shares of 10s. 45.000 are offered
to the public on payment of ss. per share
and balance in calls. Owners’ price, £(>000,
plus £3500 and 15,000 fully paid shares
to present option holders. And for what?
A few coarse gold specimens from an un-
developed claim in the vicinity of Bulla-
bulling. where shoots hitherto worked have
failed at a shallow depth and where capital
has already a little graveyard. There are
other flotations equally as impudent round
about Westonia.
* * * *
Black Range West (W.A.) is stated to
have got through the erosseotirse at No. 3
level and to be on the look-out for the reef
at that point. Meantime a 2d. call has been
made, and shareholders are being buoyed up
with the prospect of good values being
struck in the crosscut.
An Oroya Links circular issued in Lon-
don last month reckoned that as a result
of last year’s work the ore reserves in the
Eclipse lease show an increase of 30.(100
tons of ore 3d. higher in grade than the 1013
ore. A good body of oxidised stuff is stated
to have been opened up in the adjoining
Golden Eagle block.
-X- * * *
The latest W.A. show to be introduced
to Melbourne is the May Queen. As (he
name betokens, it lies in the Yllgarn field,
but a long way from the Edna. As far as
quality goes the mine may he all right. It
has doled out 010 tons of 2|oz. stuff. But
it has taken 205 ft. of sinking on the under-
lie to yield up that little bit of treasure.
It looks as if the May Queen ought, to have
been left to bewitch a prospector’s party
rather than to flaunt on Stock Exchange
steps. A. J. Mac George’s report points
that way, too. All he gives to the reef now
being worked is 700 tons for each 100 ft.
sunk, with a profit of say £3500 for each
lift. This is a lot more than bread and
butter to a working party, but it is small
potatoes for a company. Something bigger
may come from the parallel reefs, but if
these are O.K. why haven’t they been
developed before?
* * * *
From one of the most experienced mining
men in Westralia :
In many—l believe it is safe to say, most —in-
stances the shows at Westonia are being worked to
suit the Melbourne and Adelaide market operators,
among whom are some of the directors. This is
nothing short of rascality. If it plays with this fire
the public must get its fingers burned in the end.
* * * *
“Izzat”: From Port Davey (Tas.) a pro-
spector has brought to Hobart about 2cwt.
of fresh bitumen found, he alleges, on the
banks of the Deep River, which debouches
into one of the arms of Port Davey. The
bitumen is of fine quality, and the prospector
says “there’s acres of it,” Unlike the bitu-
men found on the coasts of Victoria and
South Aus., this Port Davey stuff is de-
scribed as found up a river miles from the
ocean,- and above a bar across the mouth of
the river. If this is true the ancient reason
for the existence of bitumen on the coast
from Cape Otway to Eucla, that "it has been
brought in by oceanic currents from some
far away place,” will not do duty. Almost
simultaneously finds of bitumen have been
reported from Recherche Bay, south west of
Hobart, between South Cape and D’Entre-
casteaux Channel. W. H. Twelvetrees,
Government Geologist, has been engaged for
ten weeks examining the district between
Catamaran and the New River, and he in-
tends eventually to work his way north-west
to Port Davey. The region is comparatively
unknown, and Twelvetree’s report will be of
more than ordinary interest.
Almost simultaneously with the reported
finding of bitumen at Deep River near Port
Davey and New River (Tas.) comes word
that Dr. Wade, the oil expert, spent three
weeks at Ilailton and Latrobe, and thinks
very well of the quality and quantity of
the shale beds. The Ilailton-Latrobe Shale
Co. now owns the oil areas and works of
the defunct Tasmanian Shale and Oil Co.,
which was killed by poor management. The
works and leases were bought for a song.
Mount Lyell has spun through another
half-year and its latest return —725 tons of
blister in four weeks —suggests that it pro-
duced 4000 tons for the six months to the
end of March. The outlook is very cheerful.
The management is continually improving
the equipment, and very solid progress has
been made in opening up a fine ore body
at 1100 ft. and preparing to crosscut from
a winze at 1200 ft. But as low prices were
obtained for copper in October and Novem-
ber, and the economy of electric power has
hardly had time to prove itself, it would be
unreasonable to expect very big profits for
the period just closed. During the last 12
weeks for which figures are available, costs
must have been cut into considerably. In
that time 1522 tons copper were produced
from 76,979 tons ore and secondary pro-
ducts. This means that the furnaces re-
quired slightly less than 43 tons of crude ore
to produce a ton of metal.
Toora tin. Southern Gippsland, has passed
into the hands of the Court on the action of
a machinery-man. London spent a heap of
cash in assembling there one of the biggest
individual sluicing plants deposited on Aus-
tralian drifts, but jibbed at a last payment.
So unless Tom Birkbeck gets Toora into
the embrace of a more persistent crowd, the
Toora virgin tin drifts will have to remain
to tempt a new generation of mining sports.
The St. Aignan’s first report, for the nine
months to the end of January, doesn’t carry
conviction that the mine is worth the
£lOO,OOO which the owners are understood to
be willing to sell it for. The property is on
11 isima Island, off Papua, and is part of the
same low-grade body that Broken Hill Block
10 is working under option. The ore body is
said to he anything from 10ft. to 230 ft. wide,
and to average 80ft., and the stuff can be
shot down for a few pence per ton. So far,
2780 tons have been treated. The manager
says “the average value of the lode is about
Sdwt., and runs from 14s. to 60s. per ton” ;
but the actual extraction ran only from
9s. Id. for 605 tons (taken from the surface
down to lift.) to 15s. (a lot of 515 tons taken
100 ft. lower down). The tailings are worth
from 2idwt. downwards. The mine has a
mill and cyaniding plant; but as some of it
was only lately set going, the term doesn’t
show a profit. The mine is now self-support-
ing, the manager says; but that’s no good to
shareholders. The report doesn’t make it
clear whether the lode has been yet systemati-
cally sampled; and on that everything de-
fends, seeing that at best the margin over
costs can’t be very wide —probably not enough
to allow of much picking and choosing of ore.
Interesting things are happening in con-
nection with Malay tin-mining in which
Australia is largely interested. Austral-
Mala.v Tin, Ltd., a Sydney concern, joined
some London people in floating the Kamun-
ting. Shortly afterwards it floated on its
own account in Australia the Kampong
Kamunting and Larut cos. The Ivamunt-
ing has one big dredge, which has just been
put to work. The earlier hours of a dredge
are devoted, naturally, not to the testing of
the claim, but to the adjustment of the
plant. The Kamunting appears to have
had exceptional luck. Starting on March
22 it was given a run aggregating 117
hours, and saved 10 tons. In the next 181
hours the harvest was 14 tons. Say £2250
worth for 298 hours’ work —excellent, see-
ing that the dredge was merely, so to speak,
trying its ’prentice hand. Kampong had less
luck. Apparently it didn’t happen to start
right off into such good ground, and 105
hours’ preliminary run gave 72cwt., worth
£350. Even this is over 9d. per yard. It
expects to have completed necessary ad-
justments and to start continuous running
within a fortnight. A second dredge is now
being put together. Austral Malay. Ltd.,
is a prospecting and holding co. only. It
is in 20.000 shares, and on December 1 it
held 5000 Kamunting and 8555 Kampong
shares, besides various other interests. The
co. has a good organisation in the Malay
Peninsula and is continuously testing new
areas. One. the Kulim, which looked all
right at first, was recently turned down;
but there are others in hand.
“Lakekainu” reports a movement: —
Dredging for gold is taking on in Papua. The
Yodda Valley Co. has its machine on the track to
the claims, but owing to shortage of labor or some-
thing has called tenders privately for the transport
of boilers and other ironmongery. When the gear
gets on to the wash around the Yodda things ought
to be very bright for the shareholders, but the cart-
ing of heavy boilers and dredge parts over a steep
range will eat up much of the capital. However, it
is a start, and if they get busy and clean a decent
prospect off the plates it might induce some more
of the speculating crowd in Australia to come over
and work the other auriferous rivers, lots of
which are much more get-at-able than the Yodda. A
co., promoted by Charlie Priddle in Brisbane, is
commencing operations on the Lakekamu Flats, and
if there is as much gold there as was won from the
Ironstone and other creeks, it should be a gilt-edged
proposition for the men who are taking the risk. A
dredge will be dragged up the river 72 miles ; thence
it will have to be hauled on trucks and rails to the
scene of operations. It is all flat country, so the
job will not be too much for the strenuous Aus-
tralians who are taking it on. Now that Northern
New Guinea is British the Kaiserin Augusta, Mark-
ham and other large rivers should be an attraction
to the prospector, for they are all known to carry
gold, the few men who went there having turned
these places down owing to the large amount of
German culture they met with.
The Hydes, who worked the Main Ridge
(N.S.W.) alluvial for years before they sold
out to a co. and the short-lived boom
started, are still working the old ground
and making more than a crust. Taken in the
aggregate, there is a tremendous quantity
of gold in the deposit. Practically all the
wash carries metal, and there are miles of
it, ranging from 4ft. to 20ft. thick, assay-
ing probably ldwt. to the ton. Mathematic-
ally it is easy to estimate totals that would
make the hair curl. But present methods
have only proved equal to extracting the
richer portion from the 100 ft. or so
wide of the “lead” itself. Take away
its basalt overburden, and it would he
one of the biggest things on the planet.
Without that, however, there is much gold in
the Main Itidge lead that can be profitably
won, and it probably will not be very long
before a good deal of it is won. A lot of
the richest portion of the lead is still un-
worked, and experienced local men have
belief enough and energy enough to punch
tunnels in after it. They are likely to do
more good than mushroom booms, in which
a, dozen optimistic cos. strenuously await
the results achieved by the only one which
works.
After selling' concentrates and mill resi-
dues, Clark’s, Charters Towers, reckons its
total return from 182 tons at £l7OO. which
averages £lB Os. Sd. per ton. A crushing
worth £2O per ton was previously taken
from this narrow streak of mineral. Since
these old claims have been reopened the
total return has been £7807 from 580 tons.
Clark's was encouraged to make a start in
this locality by its lucky neighbor, the
Papuan Brilliant and Victoria, which has
paid £lO,BOO in dividends from the Moon-
stone and its cross reef. Still. Charters
Towers knows how patchy this Moonstone
locality is, and the local market isn’t nearly
so enthusiastic as when the Brilliant Deeps
struck a rich make of stone.

WOMAN'S
LETTER
Sydney, April 19, 1915.
My Dear Arini, —
G.-G. Ferguson will be here to take the
troops’ salute at the end of this week. Mean-
while he bangs his grey hard hitter down
on his brow, and joins the three tourist
Governors at Canberra. Col. Foxton, the
Queensland man who shadows his steps
when the delicate Fane is resting, will see
that no one borrows the Ferguson’s kilts on
the overland trip. Owing to the present
famine in the Help Department, Aide Mul-
holland, of South Australia, will have to
drag three State Governors out of bed o
mornings during their tour round our best
scenery.
The rumor that the G.-G. desired to dig
the King’s School nursery out of old Govern-
ment House at Parramatta and sit there
himself, is said to be a frenzied joke of some
wag unknowm to official circles.
Captain Pasco, R.N., just appointed to
Governor Strickland’s stall, is well known
hereabouts, lie is in charge of the Admi-
ralty Survey work in these waters; —a mat-
ter temporarily shelved by the Big Fight.
As Aide d’Apice was beginning to wilt in
the tracks of so swift-footed a sub-royalty
as Strickland, more help was needed. Young
and dashing bucks of the kind the Society
Miss loves to gaze upon are not to be had
while the row goes on in Europe. So local
Governors have to lay hands on anything
that’s on the sunny side of an old-age pen-
sion. Pasco, who is more than 17, by the
way, is a direct descendant of the signalling
officer who ran up the famous “England
expects that every man” —and so on.
Victorian Governor Stanley and Sir Henry
Galway, of S. Australia, are the guests of
the Stricklands during their trip this way.
Our sub-royalty, with official secretary
Budge, meets the visitors at Albury. _ A
rush to Canberra, Yanco and Burrinjuck
will be wedged into a tour that includes
Jenolan Caves, Duntroon College, and a look
at the naval kids at Jervis Bay. If the
three Governors get back with unbroken
bones, the two visitors will have a few days
at Cranbrook to get their breath and eat
several extensive dinners. The chief feasts
in their honor will be paid for by the Gov-
ernment, the Mayor and the Union Club.
Stanley is a son of Earl Sheffield, the lively
old gentleman who gambolled about during
the 1913 overseas Parliamentary visit to this
State. So he follows in daddy’s footsteps
when he inspects the Canberra dust-holes.
The Strickland family is again scamper-
ing on the lawns of Cranbrook after a
summer airing at Moss Vale. Her Ex. is
said to be better, and the baby, who is a
Westralian, is growing fat and saucy. The
kindergarten rises, as usual, before the milk-
man bangs the side gate or local Society
turns in its first sleep.
The Art Students’ tableaux in the Reper-
tory Theatre were brilliantly produced. The
brothers Clint gave about £lOO worth
of such scene painting as rarely falls to
the lot of an amateur show. “The Awaken-
ing of Australia” was presented in a series
of living pictures, with backgrounds that
were works of art —as scene painting goes.
Florence Rodway, as Mrs. Jason, in “The
Golden Fleece” scene, was a glorious vision
with yellow hair hanging down its _ back,
and two curly-horned rams butting in the
foreground. The book was written by Res
Robinson; the orchestra was made up of
Cyril Monk. Frank Hutchens and Carl
Gotsck. The weak patch was the
living statuary. The statue, who was
mostly dressed in ground rice, got into
position behind a transparent curtain.
Hence the mirth while “The Dying Gladi-
ator” arranged his limbs. In spite of a
wet night the theatre was full, and a goodly
sum for the motor-lorry fund is the result.
The inter-State tennis carnival, which
opened on Friday, had brilliant sunshine for
Saturday’s dash. Outside the playing greens
the ground was, however, still like a wet
sponge, and those who risked a seat near the
lines enjoyed wet feet. N. S._ Wales teams
volleyed awav from competition, and gave
the inter-State visitors a week-end drubbing.
Gate-money, which goes into the Belgian hat,
wasn’t as good as usual for a Saturday after-
noon, in consequence, maybe, of Tatt.’s races.
Governor Strickland and the blue-eyed Eld-
est Daughter, shadowed by a gentleman help,
wriggled in the uncompromising seats that
are like a step-ladder.
Some of our best tennis players are now
in the trenches or crawling out to dry them-
selves in the sun. They include the brothers
Glen and J. .T. Addison, of Victoria. After
explaining these things, Melbourne tennis
visitors remark that they really don’t feel
hurt at being beaten in the present inter-
state rally.
A day or so after Professor Andy Stuart
had told a rather astonished world that the
big Prince Alfred Hospital spends only
about £5 a year in alcohol —including cham-
pagne—there was a quaint notice in the
papers. A score or so city business and
other people were said to have pledged them-
selves to follow the example of the King and
abstain from grog during the war. You
jumped at the list in the hope of finding
something to suggest a Great Renunciation.
There are club men in Sydney who would
miss their 11 o’clock taste, and their appetiser,
and their small bottle at lunch. And when
you turned to find out which of these had
made the sacrifice, to be as much like George
as they are able to be, you find such shock-
ing and notorious tipplers as Archbishop
Wright, little Dr. Arthur and ex-Mayor
Arthur Cocks, who have stuck to milk-and-
soda all their lives, along with a few com-
panies which, being quite impersonal, haven t
a throttle to wet or a soul to save. As
proof of a Great Renunciation the list was
a thorough failure.
Surgeon Phipps, of the A.M.C., whose red
lamp burns at Mosman, will shortly change
his address, and telephone messages sent to
“The Front” may find him—or, again, may
not. Mrs. Phipps goes too; and her address
will pass the Censor. It is London, where
a sister, the wife of Naval-Surgeon Hughes,
lives. The Phipps family mostly swings red
lamps. Father used to write his prescrip-
tions in Manchester; one son is in charge of
a sick bay somewhere in the North Sea,
another is up Brisbane way, and this third
is now packing up his hospital gear.
Beatrice Grimshaw, of the many tales of
the Tropics, is about to transfer herself and
her fountain pen and her typewriter from the
dry belt of Port Moresby, where she has kept
house for the past seven years or so, to
Woodlark Island, where the rain it rainetli
255 days in the year, and the gauge records
a fathom of wetness per annum. In the
mountains, BO miles or so out of Papua’s
capital, the writer-lady grows spices and
such-like garden produce, and the present
move is being made to a cocoanut patch two
days away from the old home.
The John Hughes family, which inhabited
Rockleigh Grange, North Sydney, until it
was acquired as a mia-mia for his Grace
Cerretti of Corinth, is now located in the
new Gregory Toohey chalet on Cremorne
Point, where the view is just as good but
different.
Dr. David Thomas, the Manly surgeon and
potentate of the local cottage hospital, left
last week with his medical son and his wife.
All three expect to get busy behind the
trenches. Mrs. Thomas —a sister of Dr.
Hetlierington, of Burwood —was a nurse in
her younger day; and when her medical son
had to undergo lately an operation, she
partly in pity for an overworked staff —re-
joined her old brigade at the hospital. Her
best wish now is to be given work alongside
her husband and son.
Maitland Levy, the eldest offspring of
the Sep Levys, departed last week to offer
his services to Kitchener. A Iveigwin
youth travels with him and also prays to
have a shot at the Huns.
Mr. Carson, of the big wool firm, is
making a holiday trip to the North Island
of Maoriland. Like a thoughtful husband,
he took his wife with him. The glacier
season in the South Island is now drawing
to a frozen end. The last Australian tour-
ists to make a dash to Mount Cook are the
William Little couple, of Pymble. After
doing the usual fancy steps on the Tasman
Glacier they will catch the final car from
that frost-bitten address on April 29. In
the Little party are the Donald Mackays
and Miss D. Griffiths.
The Swedish Consul, Yon Goes, a large,
smiling person of exuberant hospitality,
gave a gorgeous lunch at the Australia to
the departing ’Murkan Consul Bray. Every-
thing was gilt-edged—from the menus to
the cards that indicated the table address
of each guest. A total abstainer —or next
door to it —of my acquaintance could only
mourn, amongst the ashes of a big cigar,
that he was unable to appreciate the old
French wines present. Premier Ilolman,
of the P.L.L. chain, was in lively vein,
and Consul Chayet —escaped from the dirge-
like duties of patriotic teas —was also a
very cheerful man.
Captain Gordon Finlayson, a son of David
of that ilk—dad was formerly manager of
the Union Bank in Melbourne—has been
handed the D.S.O. for his services in France.
Gordon, who belongs to the Royal Horse
Artillery, married Maisie Richmond, who
belonged to a wool-growing family.
Poor Marjorie Micliie, who married Bel-
field Woollcombe, the Maorilander who was
killed in the Singapore mutiny, was still
wearing her trousseau finery when she was
included in the death-roll. The wedding was
to have taken place in the Dominion. But
when the top blew off the world’s peace the
knot was hastily tied in England, as the
’groom had to pack up for Singapore.
Mrs. Phillips- Fox has gone to join her
artist hubby in Melbourne. We Avill pro-
bably see them both in this hamlet in five or
six weeks.
An Australian dinner in Calcutta was
eaten lately at the Dalhousie Institute.
Padre Birch, Garrison sky-pilot at Fort
William, gave the toast of the evening,
“Australia.” Father Birch served as a
kangaroo soldier in the South African
squabble, and then joined the black-coat
throng. A Westralian named Gutmann
rounded off a column of remarks _ with
honeyed comments on Gibson Carmichael,
formerly Excellency of Victoria, and now
the just-as-popular Governor of Bengal. The
spread is to be an annual fixture.
The fete for the Waitara Foundling Hospi-
tal will be opened on Wednesday of next
week in the sub-regal presence.
The youngsters’ pageant in the Norland
College grounds ought to push a cosy cheque
into the Belgian coffers. Dr. Grace Boelke
stood at the receipt of custom at the front
gate, and Mrs. Greig-Smitli rounded up the
kiddies for the antics in historic fancy-garb.
Mr. Locke, a Broome pearl-fishing potentate,
gave the first prize—a pearl brooch. It went
to a lady Viking. Maisie Greig-Smitli, as
Margaret Tudor, was to have been awarded
first, but mamma stonily refused to let her
have the gaud, so she took a meek second
place. Mrs. Hugh Ward, who had promised
to do the judging, arrived late but intact,
after a series of minor motor mishaps such
as are generally dealt out to the Best Girl in
the movies.
A week or so ahead of the carnival, fay-
like Doris Waterhouse, of Burwood, who
had booked herself to be a Norland nurse
and then consented to a more permanent
engagement, was married to Dr. Hugh Bay-
son. After their honeymooning they went
out to heal the afflictions of Nyngan.
• The Alexandra Hospital committee can
rely on a very wet day for its annual meet-
ing. So the prevailing golosh last week was
quite in order. Instead of serving up the
accounts in the open the authorities had to
hustle as much of the company as possible
into a long thin room, while the overflow
took up a commanding position round re-
freshment tables on the verandahs. The war
seems to have miraculously opened hitherto-
closed purses. At any rate, the most
astounding figures which secretary Moss
poured over the proceedings showed that,
after a year of greatly increased expendi-
ture, the institution’s income still exceeded
its bills. Dr. Clubbe, in dilating on
these glad tidings, threw a bouquet
at the public, which had contributed two-
thirds of the cash. Mayor Richards handed
over a cheque for £lBOO from the Dolls’ Car-
nival ; also a second slip of paper for £7OO
or so, the result of the Willie Cooper dame’s
raid on country hamlets.
Ruth Morton, a daughter of Philip, is
engaged to Russell Keigwin, of the Orme-
Iveigwin whisky firm. As you have heard,
young Keigwin left the other day to per-
sonally help Our Cause in France. _ This
engagement has been mentioned in whispers

Dr. F. G. Stephens, the spectacled young
man from Rose Bay who went off to the
islands on the hospital ship Grantala, re-
turned engaged to one of the nursing staff.
Now he is wed —the knot was tied on Satur-
day—and the Nightingale who was _ Sister
Burtinshaw of Prince Alfred Hospital is
Mrs. Stephens.
Noel Bridges, a son of the Major-General
who is in charge of the first Australian
expedition, is in camp (or was a few weeks
ago) with a British regiment at Aylesbury.
His peace-time billet was in the Land Sur-
vey Department in the Malay States.
The Red Cross Carnival in the Stadium
brought in about £lOOO. The quaintest item
was the boxing schoolboy-competition.
Blindfolded they hammered the air or an
adjacent juvenile nose, to the great joy of
everyone but the organ’s owner. A plump
lady named Sadie, in scarlet satin tunic and
short breeches, showed us how K overthrow
a Bad Man in a jiu-jitsu argument. Woman,
who is, as a rule, not fond of boxing con-
tests, watched the joyful sparring match
between the Belgian Demlin and Dave
Smith without any inclination to scream for
help. J. J. Corbett sauntered on to auction
King Albert’s picture, but the canvas had to
be put to bed again for lack of high bids.
The drawing of an art union, with Mrs.
Hugh Mclntosh amongst those responsible
for pulling out the numbers, was the chief
event of the show.
After baritone Parker’s huge voice had
rolled round the Town Hall on Saturday
evening, there were thunders of applause.
Without the beard that accompanied so much
of his massive singing with the Quinlan
Opera Co., Parker looks quite a youngster.
He is as tall and straight as a gum sapling,
and when he opens his mouth in a crescendo
top-note the welkin trembles. In spite of
a tendency to force his lower notes —a quite
unnecessary effort, for they boom themselves
—the Canadian is a very fine artist. He
sang “The Time is Past,” from “The Flying
Dutchman,” and “Even Bravest Hearts,
from “Faust,” as we’re not likely to hear
them again for a while.
The hidden treasure of the Parker Concert
Co. is the little girl Florence Scapini. She
is 15, conies from Christchurch (MX.), and
was fiddling under di Grassi in Paris when
the war stopped her studies for a while.
This youngster has temperament to add to a
streak of genius, and some day she ought to
make a big flare in the world. Mrs. Carmen-
Harris —formerly Miss Pinscliof, of Mel-
bourne —is highly Commended for resonant
high notes and delightful frocking. Some
of' the frocks, by the way, were in the
southern mail smash, and I don’t know how
they got on. The white and blue creation,
worn on Saturday night, came on with
Mrs. Harris the day before. Mrs. de
Brandt, described as a French pianist, might
oil her finger joints and generally rub up
her technique with advantage.
The Bush Book Club will sort out its
ideas under the sub-regal roof before you
read this. It is the annual counting-up,
with Lady Edeline iu the best chair.
A Burwood lass, Miss Dawborn, landed
the biggest trout at the Kosciusko anglers
carnival. The pond in the Government
hostel grounds covers about six acres. It
has been carefully preserved for about five
years, so bites were numerous. Some or
the competitors were more enthusiastic
than proficient, and a barbed-wire fence m
one corner of the lake was frequently hooked
with great slaughter of adjectives. One
visitor—l fancy Mr. Bob Lee—swished a
salmon rod round the stars, but there weie
no casualties. There was some golf to
round off the Isaak Walton party. Mr. J.
Vicars and the Holts motored up; so did
solicitor Arthur James, of Newcastle, and
his family.
A brilliant young Maorilander, Eric H
Tayler, a son of the Archdale Taylers, of
Auckland, recently died in France, roung
Tayler topped the list of MX. candidates
for a commission in the Imperial army
before the war set Europe in flames. He
joined the Ist Battalion York and Lanca-
shire Reg., which has been pegging away at
the enomv for months. Double pneumonia,
after the long winter in the wet trenches,
was the end.
Superintendent Mitchell, who lately suc-
ceeded the brilliant but short-lived Day as
chief bobby of N. S. Wales, recently
snatched a holiday at Kosciusko, lhe tail
and solid Robert got off the car at Jmda-
byne to have a chat with his local proxy.
Not recognising his chief, the _ proxy was
quite easy and conversational, till a sudden
gleam of remembrance or something struck
him stiff. Then he quick-fired “sir” at the
visitor, and the conversation melted away in
a great official salaam. And yet the chief
constable has the big frame, the geneiail.v
tough and healthy appearance, _ and some-
thing of the manner of the man in blue who
holds up his paw at street corners, so
that even a very young p’liceman ought to
pick him out as something on the beat.
Early this week the subscribers who
parted with five guineas apiece to the
Artists’ War Fund exhibition will know
what they are going to get in return. The
prizes, however, won’t be handed over till
April 28, when the .vanmen will bump the
goods into their chariots outside Vickery s
Chambers.
Gladys Reynell, of Adelaide, lias been
showing her brush work at the Women
Painters’ Exhibition in the Fog. Edith
Hope, a Sydney girl, has also been an ex-
hibitor at ’ various recent overseas’ shows:
the Royal Academy, the old Salon, and other
august bodies have hung her work; and the
National Gallery, Ottawa, bought one of her
pictures. Sister Isabel toils in the handi-
crafts division.
Saturday afternoons are now thickly
studded with money-gathering patriotic
parties. The W. Mellraiths’ effort, m
their capacious garden at Killara, was one
of the solid events of last week. The Bel-
gian larder is likely to be the fatter by
about £3OO.
Kathleen Garvan, a sister of the polo-
playing Garvans, is going to whisper I
will” to an Englishman who signs himsell
Fulke Knatchbull Prideaux-Brune. He be-
longs to a Cornish family.
The annual party at Sailor Cook’s land-
ing place at Botany Bay is to retire behind
the war clouds this year. It is something
of a social tea-fight when it does come oft.
Kurnell, off which the old Endeavor dropped
anchor in the long ago, lends itself to a
picturesque flag-flapping. But the present
moment, when our warships are busy else-
where, is considered by the trustees to de-
mand attention to more serious affairs.
Cook’s ghost won’t mind, though various
correspondents are writing as if the drop-
ping of the picnic will make the manner s
shade turn in its winding sheet.
Yours affectionately,
VANDORIAN.

THAT OF A YOUNG GIRL.
LONG-GUARDEI) FAMILY SECRET
LEARNED THROUGH A PROVIN-
CIAL LADY.
“From mother to daughter, generation
after generation,” writes a lady now <»*
years old, “we have been noted throughout
the little provincial town where we live for
the beauty of our complexions, preserved
even to old age. Of late so many of mu-
friends seem to have been deceived and dis-
appointed by harmful skin remedies that
we have decided it is not right for us to
keep to ourselves any longer the formula
that has always been considered as our fam-
ily secret, and to which we owe our eteinal
youth.’ Therefore we take this opportunity
of communicating it to our sister readeis.
Simply take 2oz. rosewater, 1 dram tincture
of benzoin and 2oz. Flowers of Oxzoin, and
mix them yourself at home. Any chemist
can supply you with what is necessary.
Shake the lotion before using and apply
with a clean piece of cotton wool or a sponge
regularly night and morning. In less than
a week you will surely be asked what has
caused such a wonderful improvement in
your appearance. The freshness of youi
complexion will be dazzling. Me know be-
cause we have often made the very
experiment among our friends, and tins is
the only thing we ourselves have ever used.
It is our family remedy for all complexion
troubles, and has never failed to give the
very highest degree of satisfaction; in tact,
we' consider it an ideal means for pu’serv-
ing the natural health of the skin. 1>
formula is old-fashioned, simple and inex-
pensive, but always efficacious. M e wouk
he very happy to give you others but this 1
the only one we possess of which we knoi
the benefits from personal experience.

noted dermatologist proved conclusive y
the above lotion is unequalled for i I
noses for which it is recommended. ,
may therefore be regarded as being
oughly reliable .*
MELBOURNE CHATTER
Melbourne, April 10, 1015.
My Dear Yarrie
A medley of sound, composed of the
clashing of cymbals, mashies, putters and
stymies, the sneezing of caddies, and the
rhythmic plashing of falling tea, signified,
last week, that the Berwick Golf Club was
opening for the season. Berwick is in-
habited from time to time by Chirnsides.
Wilsons. Pearsons, Sharps. Knoxes and
a Becketts; also by a few miserable
creatures with less than £5OOO per annum.
Everyone who could afford the price of a
cleek was there, and some extremely heavy
feeding transpired. In the absence of Mrs.
President hßeckett (prostrated by a ner-
vous attack or something similar) the
honors were done by Mrs. Councillor Sharp
and Mrs. Willie Pearson. It was a cheer-
ful, if uneventful, function.
The Tony Horderns are here with a
motor-car that looks as if it had just floated
out of a pantomime, and many other evi-
dences of preposterous wealth. They are
at the Oriental, where I am told they have
a Suite. I am not surprised about the
Suite. I wouldn’t turn so much as a switch
to hear that they had the hotel.
Small dinner-parties have been occurring
at Government House. Melba and Robert
Parker have been singing t> the favored few
in the drawing-room after coffee. Among
recent guests were Lady Ellison-Macartney,
from the Apple l'sland; and Mrs. Scott,
mother of the late Captain Scott, of the
South Pole.
By the way, a hideous story comes to me
of luTw a local hostess sent Melba into din-
ner the other night in front of the Madden
dame. A butler with experience of the
best families immediately swooned,. and of
course the whole party was ruined. This
comes of letting people entertain before they
have passed their exams, in Polite Deport-
ment and Table Etiquette, or even so much
as joined the Dinner-givers’ Union.
A number of kiddies in St. Ivilda and
Middle Park have lately been giving
pennies, threepences and sixpences weekly
to a dear old lady who visited many houses
and pleaded the cause of the Belgians. The
other day it occurred to a St. Kilda woman,
who had been providing her little girl with
sixpence a week for the fund, to ask the
collector what she did with the cash, who
had authorised her to collect it, and a few
other pertinent questions. The venerable
philanthropist faded away like any con-
fidence woman, and has not been heard of
since. It makes one wonder how many of
the 4371 (or thereabout) casual pleaders
who infest the city and suburbs are shak-
ing dishonest coin from the skirts of Bel-
gium’s unhappy mischance.
Young Dr. Jack Gordon, some time of
Melbourne, but more lately of Mena House.
Egypt, has written to friends _ about his
“humpy.” The latter was, in point of fact,
a marble palace. It was originally intended
for a gambling casino—a rival to the famous
one at Monte Carlo. But the scheme fell
through and the vast building merely became
a white elephant, which is not nearly so
lucrative as a gaming hell.
Mrs. G. Macleod Redmond came back from
Sydney and her great misfortune last week.
She is being nursed to health and
happiness by her only sister, Mrs. Joe
Woolf, wife of the man you go to when you
want to be guided through a legal maze.
No man has tried harder to make himself
popular with dogs than headmaster Adam-
son, of Wesley College. He is president of
the Home for Lost Pups, and a big bull
terrier of his own is with him pretty well
everywhere. Yet the other day, when the
poor man chanced to step between a dog
and'some bones, 601 b. of bull terrier (not his
own) hurled itself at his neck and got as
far as his arm. The pedagogue was so
badly mauled and shocked that it was several
days before he could get about. The aris-
tocratic bull terrier’s explanation is that he
regards the president of the Lost Dogs
Home as a dangerous Socialist whose efforts
on behalf of the lower orders are calculated
to destroy those social barriers that are so
necessary in properly constituted society.
It was at a festive bohemian dinner given
by a melodious barrister to a select gather-
ing. Pellican, the celebrated tenor, was
among the number, and somewhere between
soup and nuts he arose suddenly, and called
across the table to his host: “Oh. I say,
you’ll forgive me. won’t you i But 1 forgot to
ring up Lady Blanket. I’m awfully sorry,
but I promised Lady Blanket I would ring
her lip before eight.” He went out, and rang
the telephone bell, and after a brief conversa-
tion returned to his mutton, or whatevei
was going. Later in the evening I ellman
said to his host in a gurgling aside: ‘ lou
know, dear boy, I really hadn t to ring up
Ladv Blanket, but I thought I’d give your
old dinner some tone.” One minute later
the man of law was leading the tenor to, the
front door. "First turning to the left, he
said, “and don’t come hack. ’
From my Adelaide correspondent:
It rained and rained on Saturday, and then it fell
to and really rained, despite which (for we are a
hardy lot) Prince Alfred and St. Peter’s Colleges held
their sports, and, at Morphettville, Tattersall's
Autumn Meeting occurred much as usual except for
the fact that goloshes replaced the footwear of other
days.
Talking of dreadful garments, have “jungle” coats
struck, your city yet ? We have them here in scores.
They are supposed to be made from the hides of
tigers and leopards, but what they look like is the
lining of a waterproof rug.
Partnership’s £59 in the Breeders’ Handicap, and
for second place at that, sent a select few away
happy, but they were very few. lie only attracted
£7 15s.
Our Gladys Revnell has a couple of oils on view at
the Anglo-Belgian Exhibition which is being held at
the Grafton Galleries under the auspices of the
Women's International Art Club. Since 1913 her
canvases have been hung at the Old Salon, the
Walker Gallery, Liverpool, and the Royal Academy.
Vera, second daughter of Mrs. Hall Henderson, of
Tintinara, is engaged to Frank Leslie, the second son
of the Frank Russells, of Salisbury. His good-look-
ing sister was married on the Saturday after Faster
to the youngest son of the late Jenkin Coles.
A Rose Day will occur on April 28, in aid of the
Wattle Day League’s motor-ambulance fund. If that
fails a Cauliflower Day is to be organised to push on
the Wattle-Rose movement. Meanwhile, we are liv-
ing patiently through a Pound Week on behalf of
the Babies’ Hospital. This latter is a more or less
new institution, but Lady Galway approves of it, and
has allowed Government House to be a depot for
“pound” contributions; so its future is glittering
with promise.
Professor Henderson’s first war lecture was given
at the Town Hall on Wednesday night. He has two
more up his sleeve, and there are suspicions abroad
that they will be rushed.
F. S. Holder, eldest son of the late Speaker, came
back from Buenos Aires with his wife and family
aboard the Geelong. He has been doing electrical
engineering work there on the tramways.
Amongst the bequests left by the late .1. 11. Fin-
layson, formerly of the Register, is one of £2OO to
the Adelaide University to form the whole or part
of a fund for the foundation of a scholarship in
political economy; £5O to the Cottage Homes
Society, and £5O to the City Mission.
A St. Ivilda hash-tower is sheltering a
real English soldier who has been invalided
from the war. The surrounding girls are
half mad with excitement and pity and
interest and admiration and a yearning
aspiration to make his stay here enjoyable.
To that end they call persistently on any
old friend or acquaintance who happens to
live in the favored mansion, and, having got
a footing in the establishment, they parade
the terrace and roll languishing eyes at the
Khaki Man who has such a pull over the
boys who are merely going to the war.
However, there is little conversational
change to be got out of the hero. Earnest and
sympathetic cross-examination merely elicits
this sort of thing: “We were in the trenches,
y’know —feahful noise, fiendish din. and I
got hit, y’know.” And then the warrior
relapses into pensive silence. “Goin’ back
in a week or two.” he adds explosively.
After which silence falls again with a thud
between him and the adoring flappers. They
love this pale Othello for the dangers he
has passed, but he. apparently, doesn’t care
a dump that they do pity him.
Rose Day acted as a blight on the Rose
Show —or maybe it was the weather that did
for the blooms. Anyhow the event which
happened last week in the Town Hall was
a disappointment. The Mayor introduced
the suburbanites present to the flowers,
such as they were, band music burbled-
along behind a rose trellis, and some local
talent sang sentimental songs about hearts
and roses. But altogether it was a poor
show.
The Vic. Artists’ Society, which declines
to be downcast by the secession of. the
biggest brushes, opens its annual exhibition
at the Albert-street Galleries on Tuesdav,
the 27th, and will keep it open for nearly
three weeks. If the young, aspiring mem-
bers are well advised, they will offer their
goods at “war prices.” After all. the best
advertisement a painter can get is. from
the sale of a picture. The purchaser invites
friends to come and see the masterpiece and
talks of the day when kindred works will
fetch 50 times what this bargain cost. But
these painter people have no heads for busi-
ness. They’ve only souls for art —some of
them.
Quaite a scene ,at tea time the other day
in a Collins-street cafe. The band played
“God Save the King.” and two ladies present,
who perhaps had no ears for music, omitted
to rise. The rest of the chatterers were
furious. Finally a majestic matron
stalked from her table to their end
of the room and said something. Two
startled and indignant dames turned
and looked her up and down. “I
beg pahdon, what do you mean?” one of
them gasped. After that there ensued what
the daily papers, had they been there, would
have termed a Regrettable Scene. It ended
in the management having to sort out some
of our haughtiest dames and convey them in
hysterics to their cars.
Actress Mary Worth has left for America.
She is returning later to play a steady role
under the management of one Cupid.
One of the saddest men in Melbourne is
William Drummond, whose handsome
nephew Noel died suddenly in Condon last
week. The young man was a sportsman
and very popular hereabouts.
Mrs. Pat Osborne is oyer here enjoying
a pow-wow with her family after so many
months of separation. By the way. I hear
that she is to undergo some sort of nasal
operation. So long as it doesn’t affect the
appearance of her dream of a nose I have
m> objection to lodge.
The newly-returned Madden dame now
wears eyeglasses. Her handsome eyes have
gene so far wrong that she can’t either
read or ply a needle. She is to be operated
on presently, after which the doctors expect
that things will he in all respects as they
were.
From “Rosna,” in London: —
Adelaide artist Margaret 11. Macpherson has two
pictures on the line at the Society of AY omen Artists’
show in Suft'olk-street, and the critics generally shout
for joy when they see them. The Atiien.eum, opening
its ;notice with the Australian’s exhibit, remarked:
“They seem to us the best things on the walls.”
Frank Rutter, in the Sunday Times, pronounced her
“Tulips” one of the best pictures in the exhibition ;
and Sir Claude Phillips, in the Daily Telegraph,
spoke of her “bold yet delicate color harmony.”
The Standard, also giving her exhibits first place,
said “they would lend freshness to any exhibition” ;
and the Morning Post shouted of her “cleverly
arranged and painted subjects.” The Adelaide girl
has made a good record since coming to London IS
months ago, having been accepted for every show,
from the New English Art Club to the Royal Acad-
emy, and she has been on the line every time.
The Athenaeum Club is (and I don’t
wonder) becoming rather fed-up with
Belgian appeals. Some of the older
members snarled fiercely over the two
big Rose and Flag raids made by
indiscriminate Sassiety maidens. It is
believed that after this a decision unfavor-
able to the goddess of charity was arrived
at. Anyhow, it looks like it. The upper
hall of the premises had been promised for a
Repertory Club auction. A charming
woman with an ingratiating manner—a
popular artist’s wife —begged, in addition,
for tea-party concessions in the form of
side rooms and chairs. It was the last
cadge which broke the Club’s temper. The
Powers said they’d be dog-goned (or worse)
if they’d give another inch. “Take the hall
and do with it,” their representa-
tive, and turned disgustedly away. The
women who got up the auction are Availing
about the club’s “want of patriotism.” But
reahly, my dear, don’t you feel in your
secret heart a giggling sympathy with the
badgered men? It is they who have spent
the money at the interminable round of
auctions we have amused ourselves by
organising.
This dependence on Man was proved up
to the hilt at the Rep.’s attempt with the
aid of the plausible Niblo on the block.
There was a shortage of men —the quota
being, in point of fact, about one man to
20 women. And the sales were as dull as a
battered bargain counter. Niblo, with a
wreath of chicory weed on the brown wall
behind his hero-head, coaxed, threatened,
implored-and thundered, but could never get
his “offers” in anything but shillings. Even
a pup which dabbed at Niblo’s cheek
could not tempt pounds. There were some
rare Belgian sabots, and there was a green
heart that had been buried with a Maori-
land chief. There was a live goose fattened
for table (that got some discreet house-
wifely bids) and an Egyptian lamp said to
have belonged to Aladdin’s uncle. But
none of them brought anything to speak of.
Amy Castles sold Belgian flags and made
some pennies. Mrs. Ambrose Patterson’s
three children, dressed as French peasants,
retailed sweets. Also there were strange
garments for sale which gave the wa 11s on
which they were hung a quaintly old cl o'
shop effect. There was one gem of a padded
dressing-gown (dull puce with pink cords)
that stood up by itself and absolutely
prattled to me of embonpoint and felt
slippers, and screw-pins twisted tightly into
middle-aged hair. I don’t fancy it \\ r as sold.
Robert Parker sang on Thursday after-
noon in the Auditorium. The show lasted
but an hour and a half, and it was prac-
tically all Parker, the small violinist only
appearing once or twice during the pro-
ceedings to give him a breathing spell. Lady
Stanley and Melba turned up in an appre-
ciative mood. Melba wore purple plums
stowed away beneath a hard sailor hat
which was tilted at a rakish angle; also
long white suede gloves which she clapped
high in the air at any special prowess on
the part of Parker. Lady Stanley
appeared in a fur hat with a pink rose
aboA r e her high-backed chair and a. pair of
very slim black silk ankles below it. Mrs.
George Armstrong’s cherry-colored military
collar blazed backwards, so to speak, at a
dazzled house. Mrs. Armytage—the dow-
ager of the clan —sported a bonnet of old-
fashioned Tuscan straw, aud there was some
royal purple among her voluminous skirts.
The two star listeners and a chosen few
handed Parker a stirrup cup of tea at the
Quamby Club before he dashed for the
express. Everybody seemed to be taking
nourishment but the poor, worried accom-
panist who, after packing the music, was
seen tearing along Collins-street to the
train in a motor. Mrs. Harris, in the
loveliest navy blue travelling duds, had a
Franeatelli party before she made her dash,
and I also got a fleeting vision of Mamma
Wiedemann Pinschof roaming through the
traffic on the Block with huge posies of
flowers —all by herself and wearing vast
round spectacles. It is a business, getting
these artists moved.
Dr. Springthorpe has been sending home
happy and interesting letters from Egypt—
—not to me, alas! but to his men friends.
“Springy,” as I always call him to myself,
was ever my ideal of what the military
doctor should be. That is as regards appear-
ances. lie would bring a gleam of light to
the saddest of funeral parties. On the
steruer side he makes a specialty of field
ambulance work. lie was president of the
St John Ambulance movement here.
Vanda Clarke, who is now Mrs. Norman
Hodges, wore the sort of bridal gown that is
affected by the Princess in the fairy tale. It
was of fine silver lace mounted on white
tulle. Over this was a Court train of white
silk and silver-thread brocade. It was won-
derfully beautiful; also expensive. Her sis-
ters, who gave her moral support at the
altar, were also distracting visions of silver
lace, variegated by pink and mauve tulle.
Norman could only wear the dull attire of
the male, but he looked beautiful, all the
same, and heightened the effect by sporting
a valuable pale mauve orchid, which is
named Vanda, after his bride.
The wedding was “quiet'’—only one Gover-
nor and nothing at all in the way of brass
bands or megaphones. Lady Stanley wore
rose-color and soft grey furs and a duck ol
a hat. She also wore all her children in a
sort of garden posy round her. Dean Godby
did the knot-tying with the ease of the old
hand. At the subsequent house-party at
Winmarleigh Governor Stanley proposed the
health of the bride, and a very happy little
effort it was. suggesting that he had read her
up as carefully as lie is reputed to do so
many of his other subjects. Dr. Johnson (a
modern young man —not the ghost of the
learned and ancient Samuel) supported Nor-
man as second-best man.
Vanda left for Macedon in a neat travel
ling outfit of dark blue; but when last heard
of she and Norman were tearing across coun-
try in a vast touring car to the Clarke
family’s Mount Seliank station in S.A. Later,
they are leaving for England, whence Nor-
man intends to go to the Front.
Willy Kelly’s clever sister Maisie was mar-
ried last week. The bridegroom is Captain
Joe Kelly, It. N.—not related to the local
Kellys, by the way—-and he has just done
something stupendous in the Dardanelles. I am
not sure that he hasn’t been knighted for it.
an event which, While an everyday occur-
rence in the life of mayors, carries consider-
able significance where sailors are con-
cerned. Maisie Kelly, who has lived
hitherto with her brother F.S., the pianist,
herself plays and sings like a professional.
She first met her husband years ago, when
he was stationed in Australia.
Lady Massereene and Ferrard is the latest
British aristocrat to become a nurse. You
will remember her visit to Federal Govern-
ment House a few years ago. Her tall and
alluring figure and pretty Irish face caused
a distinct stir among the baldheads at
theatres and restaurants. Her somewhat
depressed-looking husband has been at the
Front for some months with the 1< th
Lancers.
Yours sincerely,
JOHANNA.

GROSS, FEVERISH, BILIOUS CHILDREN
NEED "CALIFORNIA SYRUP OF FIGS."
Look, Mother! See if the tongue is
coated; this denotes sluggish liver
and bowels.
Mother! Your child isn’t naturally cross
and peevish. See if the tongue is coated;
this is a sure sign that its little stomach,
liver and bowels need attention at once.
When listless, pale, feverish, “stuffy,” with
cold, throat sore ; when the child has tainted
breath and doesn’t eat, sleep or act natur-
ally, or has stomach-ache or diarrhoea, re-
member, a gentle liver and bowel cleansing
should always be the first treatment given.
Nothing equals “California Syrup of Figs”
for children’s ills; give a teaspoonful, and
in a few hours all the waste matter, sour bile
and fermenting food clogged in the bowels
pass out of the system, anl you have a
healthy and playful child again. All chil-
dren love this harmless, delicious “fruit laxa-
tive,” and it never fails to effect a good
“inside cleansing.” Directions for babies,
children of all ages and grown-ups are plainly
on the bottle.
Keep it handy in your home. A little
given to-day saves a sick child to-morrow,
but get the genuine. Ask your chemist for
a bottle of “California Syrup of Figs,” then
look and see that it is made by the “Cali-
fornia Fig Syrup Company.” Counterfeits
are being sold here. Refuse substitutes.
All leading chemists sell “California Syrup
of Figs,” 1/li and 1/9 per bottle.

WHISKY.
A UNIFORM STANDARD of QUALITY.
ABORIGIN-
ALITIES
“H.G.” : Allow me to butt in to this coach-
driving argument and nominate “Tommy” Cawker,
of Casterton, tor the prize. Sixty-two years ago,
when he was a youngster of 10, he made his debut,
and took mails along the line from Geelong to Ham-
ilton in a spring-cart. There were no bridges then,
but there was plenty of excitement. In the ’so’s he
made a change to the Geelong-Ballarat trip, and
later ran the coach from Ararat to Port Fairy. In
1861 he bought the line from Penola to Hamilton,
and later from Narracoorte. Soon afterwards he
ran another line from Port Mac Donnell to Lacepede
Bay, and in that year (1862) ran the first coach
through from Mount Gambier to Victoria. This
line was established to join the Adelaide to Mel-
bourne coaches at Tullock, in Vic. Later the Mount
Gambier to Casterton run superseded it, and is still
going strong. The building of the Adelaide to Mount
Gambier line, and later the Beachport to Mount
Gambier line, stopped the coaches along those
routes. Late last month “Tommy” drove the last
regular horse-coach from Mount Gambier to Caster-
ton. He had driven the first, and was determined to
be in at the death. The price of fodder has made
horse flesh too expensive, and the neddies have had
to give way to a motor. “Tommy” did not like
making the change, but it had to come. There isn’t
enough paper in stock to calculate how many miles
he has held the ribbons. Anyway, it’s about 7000
more than it is to Tipperary, and then possibly
a mile or two has been missed. When he drove
George, now King, then Princeling, from Narra-
coorte to Hamilton, “Tommy” was invited to drop
in if ever he visited England, and have a spin
behind one of the regal teams. “Tommy” said quite
frankly that he wouldn’t think of going to England
to see the Prince, but intended to go home some
day and see his sister, and if he had time he would
call on his Highness. He is 78 now, straight as a
rush with his 6ft. odd, his hand as steady as any
young man’s, and he thinks that in a few years’
time, after he has done a lot more work, he may go
to England. But, on second thoughts, he always
reckons there is plenty of time yet.
* * * *
“Reefer”: For the Wetness Handicap
please put Papua on scratch. I haven’t the per-
formances of the really damp places, and last year
was the droughtiest on record, but at Lakekamu the
trickle was 179 in. for 224 days; at loma 181 in. for
201 days, and liikori was sprinkled with 155 in. in
203 days. There are no gauges away in the moun-
tains, where it rains by the foot almost every after-
noon. A thousand-gallon tank might do, provided
the measurement was taken and the gauge emptied
daily.
* * # *
“Outcast” : The most curious Papuan cus-
tom connected with betel-chewing is that found in
Rossel Island, where some 600 Chows were once
eaten. The betel quid is allowed to accumulate along
the eight upper teeth until it becomes level with
the upper lip, the material, of course, becoming hard
and fixed in course of time. The mass presses the
lip outwards, and when the wearer smiles the lip is
necessarily drawn inwards and upwards, giving the
sroiler something of the appearance of a snarling
dog. The right to use this beautifier is strictly con-
fined to the chiefs, so that when you see an extra-
ordinarily horrible-looking person on Rossel you will
at once know he is a State Governor, or ex-Minister
at £4 a day and expenses, or something like that.

“Copabella”: There’s a gate notice near
Lankey’s Creek (N.S.W.) which beats all others. It
is in white letters along the top rail, and reads:
“Can’t you shut the damned gate?” Appealed to in
that way, they mostly can.

“Bad Australian”: Camped on the Cen-
tral Andes (S. America), I was out with a 12-bore
shotgun trying to collect one of the semi-occasional
ducks which drop in on their way to Brazil or Pekin,
or somewhere, and made for a small arroyo, which
soaked out of the lower end of a salar. When I
struck the bank of the arroyo I looked cautiously
over, and was suddenly up against the limit. An
old-man puma about the size of a full-grown mastiff,
but set on much shorter legs, was standing at the
foot of the bank, at a distance of about 30ft., look-
ing into my eyes. 1 am free to confess that at that
moment 1 was about the worst-scared individual in
S. America ; but on the principle of “one hit before
I go” 1 loosed off both barrels of No. 2 into the
front elevation of that cat. And, 10, he crumpled
up like a wowser in a brewery, and the tale of his
days was told. When I planked the pelt down in
camp my little following of Indians were the most
astonished crowd since Manco Capac; they couldn’t
figure out how any human being should be so
adaptable as to go out after a duck and bring
home a “leon.” I have seen the “leon” in all his
pristine ferocity run down by a pair of half-naked
Indian boys, mounted on mules, lassoed fore and
aft to a sapling, like a poddy calf, and outed by a
bash on the head with any old missile that happened
to be hand} 7 . I have seen the jaguar in his native
wilds, and I have never known him to jag amongst
humans to the extent of one square foot of sticking-
plaster ; and tht rest of the “ravening scourges of
the wild” are, in my opinion, exactly the same kind
of fraud. The only genuinely dangerous and abso-
lutely ferocious “scourge” I have bumped against on
this continent is the half-breed when he has taken
on his load of pisco or a ana, and feels that he really
must tear the universe inside out. When I see that
scourge raging I always reach for my siege gun so
as to get in early and with sufficient punch to even-
up the discussion.
* * * #
“Pipitea”: About 40 years ago a bishop
and his retinue made a trek through that part of
the main country where natives were still healthy and
of a most obliging nature. He was given a hurray
reception, and when the shades of night had fallen
fast the ganger of the chief’s harem arrived, pur-
porting to top off the menu according to custom by
advancing his Gaiters a wife. The parson who was
in front of the house for the bishop was horrified at
the thought. The Bishop, he said, would be shocked
beyond expression about it. “Don’t you understand,”
he asked the Henare:“his Lordship’s a bishop?” The
delegate scratched the right half-section of his upper
deck thoughtfullly, and got a notion. “Oh yeh,”
said the inspired editor of the harem. “A bih-iiyop,
hey? Bee-ee-g ferrer! By crite, me give him two.”
“Pick”: If any stockman knows how to
head off or wheel a desert, Jet him issue a com-
munique to the fettlers at Billilingra, on the Cooma
(N.S.W.) line. Billilingra, once a splendidly-grassed
sheep-run, has now been reduced by rabbits to a
sandy waste, whose surface races eastward with every
westerly blow. Fences and trees are buried, gullies
levelled, and on extra-windy days the Eumeralla and
’Bidgee . rivers, which sough along that desolate
way, are temporarily dammed. When the advanced
tongue of the moving country reached the railway,
the fettlers walled it back with discarded sleepers
and brush, but the sand soon tumbled over the
barricades. Now the whole desert is gradually en-
croaching upon the line, while the weary fettlers,
madly whirling their shovels, inquire what they are
to do next. Sure enough there is trouble ahead.
Where there is nothing to eat and millions of rab-
bits. to eat it, Port Pirie and marram grasses, which
have been successfully employed to pull up travelling
sand elsewhere, would have no show. Whatever the
solution, the fettlers take their frenzied oath that
there is a limit to holding back a ten-mile desert
with old railway sleepers.
“IN A S INGLE BREATH.”
“Deer Taubada, —Plenty New Guinea man
and Kanaka man want make big row along some
feller who make picture in The Bulleytin. You
know what he do? Every time he make picture of
New Guinea man or Kanaka man he put grass ramie
on him ! We feller wild proper ; we wild like Hell!
Grass ramie no belong man. Grass ramie belong
woman ! S’pose man he wear clothes belong woman,
he kava, I think ! That’s what for we feller growl.
Vn hat’s .matter you feller no hammer picture man ?
By cripes, s’pose he come New Guinea we kai-kai
him. —Your cannibal brother, Papc-llanak-McNorth.

A SONG OF RAIN.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Because a little vagrant wind veered south from
China Sea ;
Or else' because a sun-spot stirred; and, yet, again,
mil}' lie
Because some idle god in play breathed on an
errant cloud,
The heads of twice two million folk in gratitude are
bowed.
Patter, patter... .Boolcoomatta,
Adelaide and Oodnadatta,
Pepeyoona, parched and dry,
Laugh beneath a dripping sky.
Riverina’s thirsting plain
Knows the benison of rain.
Ararat and Arkaroola
Render thanks with Tantanoola
For the blessings they are gaining,
And it’s raining- — raining—raining !
Because a Heaven-sent monsoon the mists before it
drove;
Because things happened in the moon; or else
because high Jove,
Unbending, played at waterman to please a laugh-
ing boy,
The hearts through half the continent are raised in
grateful joy.
Weeps the sky at Wipipipee,
Far Farina’s folk are dippy
With sheer joy, while Ballarat
Shouts and flings aloft its hat.
Thirsty Thackaringa yells;
Taltabooka gladly tells
Of a season wet and windy;
Men rejoice on Murrindindie;
Kalioota’s ceased complaining ;
For it’s raining—raining — raining!
Because a poor bush parson prayed an altruistic
pray’r,
Rich with unselfish fellow-love that Heaven counted
rare;
And yet, mayhap, because one night a meteor was
hurled
Across the everlasting blue, the luck was with our
world.
On the plains of Winininnie
Wattle low and horses whinny,
Frolicking with sheer delight.
From Beltana to The Bight,
In the Mallee’s sun-scorched towns,
In the sheds on Darling Downs,
In the huts at Yudnapinna,
Tents on Tidnacoordininna
To the sky all heads are craning,
For it’s raining — raining—raining !
Because some strange, cyclonic thing has happened—-
God knows where —
Men dream again of easy days, of cash to spend and
spare.
The ring fair Clara coveted, Belinda’s furs are nigh,
As clerklings watch their increments fall shining from
the sky.
Rolls the thunder at Eudunda;
Leongatha, Boort, Kapunda
Send a joyous message down;
Sorrows, flooded, sink and drown.
Ninkerloo and Neerim South
Ilail the breaking of the drouth;
From Toolangi’s wooded mountains
Sounds the song of plashing fountains;
Sovereign Summer’s night is waning;
It is raining — raining—raining !
Because the breeze blew sou’-by-east across the China
Sea;
Or else because the thing was willed through all
eternity
By gods that rule the rushing stars, or gods long
seons dead,
The earth is made to smile again, and living things
are fed.
Mile on mile, from Malacoota
. Runs the news, and far Baroota
Speeds it over hill and plain,
Till the slogan of the rain
Rolls afar to Yankalilla;
Wallaroo and Wirrawilla
Shout it o’er the leagues between,
Telling of the dawning green.
Frogs at Cocoroc are croaking,
Booboorouie soil is soaking;
Oodla Wirra, Orroroo
Breathe relief and hope anew.
Wycheproof and Wollongong
Catch the burden of the song
That is rolling, rolling ever
O’er the plains of Never Never,
Sounding in each mountain rill,
Echoing from hill to hill. . . .
In the lonely, silent places
Men lift up their glad, wet faces,
And their thanks ask no explaining,
It is raining — raining—raining I

“Wandi”: Recently a couple of immi-
grants arrived in Sydney whose luggage included a
supply of sickles and reaping-hooks. They were
evidently < reapers, who expected lucrative employ-
ment heri in that line. It is a big leap from the
reaping-hpok to the reaper and binder, not to men-
tion other efficient harvesting machinery in use in
Australia to-day; but there was plenty of use for the
old hook at one time, and a field that can now be
quickly harvested by half a dozen men provided
a lengthy picnic for a little regiment. The
expert reaper was proud of the skill with which he
used the curved blade, and of the number of sheaves
he could cut in a day. So was the veteran who
wielded the scythe—which was an improvement on
the reaping-hook. Mowing was a good occupation,
for there was plenty of it. Thousands of acres
every year awaited the scythe; and the men who
could mow an acre a day were the people who mono-
polised the conversation. Now they merely share
the boneyard.
“G&S” : I concur, as Justice Barton would
say, in “Pick’s” statement (B. 25/.2/’l5) that Gov-
ernment officials are frequently responsible for the
misspelling of Australian place names. Bowenfells,
on the N.S.AY. Western line, was always correctly
spelled like that on the name-board of the railway
station until some departmental satrap got to work
and substituted “Bowenfels.” “Fels” is German for
a rock; “fells” is Scotch for a chain of steep hills.
Andrew Brown, who came from Perthshire, named it
the latter appropriate way. Bowen was a surveyor,
it’s said.

“McNortli”: In the matter of defence
many of the far-inland mountain tribes of Papua
are no slouches. Wherever possible they perch their
villages on high razor-backed spurs or peaks—and in
that topsy-turvy country such formations are fre-
quent. A high palisading of split slabs is built
around the village and watch towers erected. From
these latter, which often consist of a house built in
a tree, a constant lookout is kept, and because of
their elevated position and the fact that the ground
slopes away- abruptly, on both sides they are very
effective. Entrance is obtained to the compound by
means of a small hole in the stockade through which
only one person at a time may crawl. Sometimes
a cheerful savage with a club is posted just inside
the door, so it’s as well to send some of the carriers
through first. The gardens are usually low down the
spur on both sides, and in such positions that they
are in full view’ from the village. Therefore the
women, who do all the agricultural work, can’t come
home early with the excuse that they were disturbed
and pursued by strange men. When there is trouble
with other tribes, arrow-heads are buried in the
ground, with the points sticking up an inch or so
and slanting slightly in the direction from which
the enemy is expected to come. Many a night raid
has been spoilt by the yell of one of the would-be
attackers as he put his bare foot on a hidden arrow
point. Years ago I was chasing the nimble penny-
weight in a region 18 days’ walk from the coast. The
niggers were distinctly hostile, but the fact that I
could walk without harm over arrow-heads, which, of
course, couldn’t penetrate my thick mountaineering-
boots, was the cause of considerable astonishment,
and made those wondering niggers have a great re-
spect for the strange white man. The use of a
little discretion and kindness will often turn them
from enemies to friends. At one place, 6000 ft. above
the sea, there were five stockaded villages strung
along a narrow, sloping spur. I was in a narrow
.ravine 1000 ft. below, with half a mile of carriers
and camp followers. All day long the niggers above
had been yelling and firing arrows and rolling stones
down on us. Under the circumstances that ravine
didn’t seem a very healthy place to camp. So I
decided to climb to the top of the ridge, and have
the unfriendly nigs.- below me. To do so it was
necessary to pass through the whole five villages.
Half-way up the yelling was terrible, and 1 shall
never forget the sight of those scores of naked
savages, their forms silhouetted against the sky,
frenziedly dancing and slapping their thighs and
shaking their spears in anger at us. Flocks of
arrows and stones fell around us, and although no
one was hurt, there were some narrow squeaks. Before
I got to the first village the noise had stopped, and
on breaking an entrance through the stockade I
found the village deserted. But here and there,
in the long la-lang grass which flanked the spur,
could be seen the heads of the hiding warriors.
Each village was deserted in turn as I approached
it, but the inhabitants returned as soon as my
party had passed through. At the last village I
decided to camp, and an hour or so later two old
men cautiously approached. I made signs to them that
it was all right, and placed some salt and a looking-
glass on the ground near them, at the same time
exposing a box of trade goods. The old nigs, gin-
gerly picked the salt up and tasted it. r I lien they
shouted to their friends, and in a little while the
compound was crowded with the villagers bringing
presents of sweet potatoes, yams, etc., and the chief
was telling me what a good fellow I was, anil
remarking that for another fistful of salt he would
make a blood-brother of me. - Half a dozen pigs
were killed in my honor, and everyone had a great
blowout. I left that' spur with regret. In fact,
if the Germans beat the Allies and take Australia,
I’m going back there. Life would be much pleas-
anter with those primeval savages than under the
civilised fiends of Bill the Brute.
“Pick”: Louis JGsson’s poem., “The Mo-
poke” (B. 25/3/’lo), once again calls up for someone
to correctly report the language of the boboolc owl,
whose night cry has been variously translated by
natural-history poets and writers as “mopoke, ’
“mokepoke” and “morepork.” Every bushman with
a correct ear knows that the bird’s call is a reiter-
'ated plea for “work, work.” The bobook is, in fact,
the feathered sundowner. He begs for work every
evening, and hides from it every day. His dour
aspect and embittered profile are the outcome of
long brooding over the libels that have been written
about him ever since King Binghi abdicated the
throne.
“C.H.N.”: “Pick’s” remarks re leeches
(B. 10/12/’l4) remind me of a worse experi-
ence here in Papua. After capturing all the Shar-
mans, and having nothing better to do except fight
Waria cannibals, I strolled along the so-called beach,
mostly consisting of mangrove, crossed the Waria,
Wu Wu and Eia rivers, and came to what is called
in this ungodly end of the earth a sago swamp, but
in reality is square miles of soft mud and river
scum up to your middle. I made for a point which
looked like civilised beach. But it was only more
scum and more sour mud. I picked off all the insects
I'd accumulated —when you talk of insects in this
blighted hole you do the vermin a compliment—and
sat down to grub. Then out they came. The
niggers said they were sand flies. Skeeter, scorpion,
Tas. devil, leeches and the million other works of
Satan are nothing compared with these detestable
little yellow-bellied, blue-winged works of hateful-
ncss itself. They start by enveloping you in count-
less numbers, get in your ears and eyes, make a
break through every exposed inch of you, and then
calmly burrow through your skin and settle inside.
The burrowing process itself isn’t painful, but after-
wards words fail. Yet some of these Papuan canni-
bals live on the beaches, and I’ve seen them camped
between the so-called beach and a malarial swamp
barely 20 yards inland. Why, an old Presbyterian’s
description of Hell is not within leagues of it.
IF COWS WORE CORSETS.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
If cows wore corsets I would go
To see the Sydney Easter Show.
But at nude cows I cannot gape
When they are always the same shape.
A variation of the line
Is vital to each feminine.
I can’t see why the law allows
Exceptions in the case of cows.
And, while I wonder thus, I. stay
From every Easter Show away.
For when each 3'ear square cows I see,
Square cows no longer int’rest me.
But I would hurry to the grounds
If hour-glass cows walked on their rounds.
To ev’ry show I would
If Ayrshires used the JKifting waist,
And Jerseys, fawn and.mild of eye,
In whalebone-moulds' wept pacing by.
With cash no Show will I endow
For this monotony of cow.
But give them corsets and I’ll go
With keen delight to every Show !
N.S.W.
THE MAN MILLINER.

Business, Bobbery, etc
A wise old beak sits on the bench at
Oamaru (M.L.) —a dry district where the
wowser rages in his bleakness. The other
day an old party charged with making and
selling good wine was fined £25 and costs ;
but when the police applied for permission
to confiscate the liquor and destroy it they
were met by a firm “No!” Solomon pointed
out that tiie police and everyone else had
sworn that the wine was excellent stuff, and
as he considered waste a sin the grog would
be handed over to the local hospital. The
Me Wowsers have been shaking their heads
ever since about such a verra ter-r-rible
outrage.
