“N. or M.” : “Iv” wrote some ’ weeks ago
on the alarming increase in the rabbit popu-
lation of Western Victoria. It is the same
here in Tassy. With the outbreak of war
the price of skins of all kinds dropped
pretty well to zero, and the net result was
a close season for bunny. The drought,
which might have been expected to keep
down the birth-rate, did nothing of the sort,
and harassed farmers are making anxious
inquiries about wire-netting and wondering
why Moses didn’t afflict Pharaoh with rab-
bits instead of playing round with locusts
and frogs. Minister of Lands Belton sug-
gests trapping, and at the same time doing
a good turn to Belgium by sending ship-
ments of frozen rabbits to that Hun-ridden
country, and it is to be hoped something
will come of the scheme. But at present the
freezing accommodation is very limited.
Space is required for sending meat to the
Front, cold storage is chock-a-block, and
Tasmanian orchardists are on tenter hooks.
These orchardists have suffered from many
and divers slings and arrows of late. The
weather, after doing nothing for long, rain-
less months, fell upon the ripening fruit
in chunks, with storms of hail and howling
winds. Now the orchardist doesn’t know
whether his space in the apple boats may
not be commandeered, in which case the
remainder of his year’s crop will be thrown
on a glutted Australian market.
“W.D.Y.” : For some years past the Gulf
Steamship Co. has had a monopoly of the
trade of St. Vincent Gulf ports and the
Adelaide Steamship Co. has had the same in
Spencer Gulf, on the other side of Yorke
Peninsula, the most prolific wheat-growing
part of S. Aus. There seemed to be an
understanding between the two companies
not to do any poaching. However, some-
thing went wrong, and a few months ago
the Gulf Co. (much the smaller concern)
started running in Spencer’s Gulf. The
Adelaide Co. retaliated by trading in St.
Vincent's, and fun began. Freights dropped
75 per cent, in a day ; fares ditto. St. Vin-
cent’s Gulf ports had a boat practically
every day—two a week sufficed before. Any-
one sending over half a ton or so of goods
rarely had to purchase a ticket, an invita-
tion to travel being almost invariably
offered. Each co. circularised the Peninsula,
showing the exorbitant fares and freights
charged before. The public of course wel-
comed the new co. in each place, and the
companies responded by banqueting the lead-
ing citizens. But the fun didn’t last long.
The smaller co. put up a good fight, but was
soon swallowed. Freights and fares jumped
to the old scale the day after the swallow.
Then services were cut down to half, and
complimentary tickets were no longer on
tap. Season tickets were at one time issued,
but these are new out of favor, and a long-
suffering public is daily expecting a further
increase in fares to make up for the reduc-
tions during the wild time of competition.
“Full-weight” : It is about time some other
robbers besides the baker and milk-and-
water man were touched up for short
weight. I’ve been handling soap for some
time, and of bars that are sold as 40 to the
cwt. one lot recently weighed 851 b., another
1001 b., another 951 b., and still another 861 b.
Some are wrapped in paper and some are
nude. The heaviest is unwrapped, and pro-
bably weighed a little over 1001 b. when it
left the factory. Jams are sold in tins as
one and two lb. The 11b. case contained
to 140 z., and the 21b. 270 z. to 28oz.
Currant cases supposed to contain 561 b.
weighed gross 601 b. to 621 b., and the case
panned out about 81b. One-pound tins of
baking powder contained 13oz. to 140 z., re-
puted quart bottles were l-6th of a gallon.
Kapok dealers allow 41b. for the bale, which
often weighs 101 b. to 111 b. This means with
an 801 b. bale at Bd. lb. (about the average
price), 4s. 6d. for an old jute bale of no
value afterwards. As kapok posts less than
fourpence in Java this is a bit stiff. About
the only honest weight we get is from the
so-called monopolists. A 701 b. bag of sugar
or a 501 b. bag of flour contains those weights ;
and treacle and sugar are the weight
marked on packages. Nearly all imported
goods, especially English, contain the full
number of ounces they are sold as. Salmon
and herrings contain Boz. or 160 z.. as the case
may be. So does cornflour and arrowroot.
Tea lends itself to swindles and big profits,
but it is honest weight, while coffee is some-
times 14oz. to the lb. If the law compelled
manufacturers to put net weight in all
packages the public would know what _ it
was buying. It is done in other countries
and should be made compulsory in Australia.
“Clinker” : My final consignment of super-
phosphate for the season has arrived, just
seven days after it started on its 300-mile
journey from Sydney. It recalls the time
the Western line was completed as far as
Wallerawang.. As usual, the day the first
train arrived was made a gala day. Amongst
the crowd of sightseers was an old chap
named Gleeson. He had walked in, and
when he arrived at the platform the engine
was just shunting away. Gleeson ran after
it as fast as his 70 years would permit,
shouting to it to stop till he had a look. Of
course no notice was taken of him. and he
was speedily-distanced. “Be the t’underin’
hell, now,” said he, “whin ye’re as ould as
me ye won’t be so fast.” I’m beginning to
think he was right.
“The Oldest Inhabitant” on ancient signs:
In the first ’so’s William Howitt wrote of a pub
he saw at Flemington, three miles from Melbourne.
It was the Bull and Mouth—the sign a formidable
bull standing upon a huge month, which looked like
a great oyster, and over its head an eye enclosed
in a “glory,” meant to represent the Deity! Around
it were the words, “Fear God and Honor the Queen.’
“What a sign,” says Howitt, “for a pot-house!”
In 1841 a pub on the Melbourne-road, seven miles
from Dandenong, was named “The No Good Damper
Inn.” It kept a gentleman named De Villiers. In
1852 The Pick and Shovel was at Tourbournie, Gee-
long district, a station formed by .1. H. Patterson.
The Old Lamb Inn, a wattle-and-dab hut in Market
Square, Melbourne, was the start of “Scott’s.” The
Menzies’ of to : day had its birth in an obscure
house in La Trobe-street, on the south side, between
Elizabeth and Swanston streets. It had been built
as a private house, and stood back from the street.
Archibald Menzies added a bar on the corner of the
right-of-way, and there opened in 1854 or ’55 -not
’52, as stated frequently. One Archibald Menzies-
the same, no doubt —had kept The Hope Tavern, on
the corner of York and King streets, Sydney, not
far from the southern gate of the old Barrack
Square. Later, when the soldiers were shifted to
Paddington, he kept The Star, in George-strcet;
David Jones’s premises now cover the site. That
was in 1850. In 1867 Archibald Menzies purchased
the corner of William and Bourke streets, Melbourne.
On it stood a fine villa, which had been occupied
one time by Dr. Campbell and the elder Justice
Williams. It was said when Menzies was building
the new house that he was “going out into the bush” ;
but the Spencer-street Railway Station was develop-
ing, and Menzies’ keen Scotch intellect told him
that the city would move his way.
* * * *
“The Pariah” : Mention in the cables that
a section of the Chinese are clamoring for
a boycott of Japanese goods calls to mind
that the weapon has been used twice by
China in the past nine years. The U.S.A.
were the first object, and while no definite
estimate of the damage to American trade
can be made, Australia’s export of flour to
China jumped from 600 to 10,000 tons in a
year, and several mills on the China coast,
which had been struggling for an existence,
suddenly became affluent and declared divi-
dends. ' Incidentally the secretary of one of
them managed to vamoose with £15,000.
In 1907-8 the big boycott of Japan started.
In May of the latter year I saw in Canton
hundreds of tons of Jap. goods hustled into
suitable places and burned with Chinese
pomp and extravagant transports rage
and contempt. According to Japan’s “Year
Book” the whole show lost Nippon £6,500,000
that year.
THE SONG OF THE “PATRIOTIC” SHOPKEEPER.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
With posters done in blue and red
My shop is plastered o’er ;
Cute schemes for gain are in my head
As here, beside my door,
I talk with likely customers about “this awful war.”
“Our Patriotic Sale’s Begun,”
“Amazing Sacrifice” —
So do my flaunting posters run—
“ Goods at Three-quarter Price.”
“Buy One of Our ‘King Albert’ Hats, New Style
and Very Nice.”
“This awful war”- —I roll my eyes—
“lt ruins honest trade !
All German goods, sir, I despise!
Now these smart coats were made
In England; warranted to wear, and will not shrink
or fade.
“Down with the Kaiser! Sir, it wrings
My upright, British soul
To think of all the fearful things
He’s done to win his goal. ...
Wool ’comforters for soldiers, ma’am ? Yes. For-
ward, Mister Mole!”
y
“Indeed, indeed it’s very hard ;
But we must not repine....
That flannel? Two-and-three a yard.
Yes, ma’am, a special line ;
Cut for our Patriotic Sale”....and dear at onc-and-
nine!
“Ah, these poor Belgians! Sir, it shocks
All human decency !. .. ■
(I sold five gross of ‘Belgian’ socks
Last week at one-and-three !)....
Poor, starving folk! It hurts me, sir, such misery
to see!”
“Come to our Patriotic Sale!”
“Great War Reductions Made!”
Such cunning phrases never fail
To capture goodly trade.
“Inspect our dress-goods! Like our troops they will
not run nor fade.”
“Yes, ma’am, that little ornament
Was made in Germany.
The price is up fifteen per cent.,
Because, of course, you see,
’Twas bought before the war, and now that line
we can’t' indent.”
“Ahem !... .Yes, as you say, sir, true—
A race of thieves and spies.
And, let me say, between us two,
All Germans I despise !. . . .
Ah, that reminds me, have 3 T ou seen this line of
‘Joffre’ ties?”
“Enlist, Young Man, if You are Fit!”
So reads my window sign.
(And, while you’re thinking over it,
Come and inspect our line
Of special scarves for brave recruits. Dirt-cheap at
three-and-nine.)
With posters done in red and blue
Mr shop-front flames and flares;
While busily 1 plan anew
My patriotic snares
For trusting customers who come to buy my shoddy
wares.
’Tis an ill wind. .. .Between ourselves,
These Germans do some good.
They’ve helped to clear from off my shelves
Old stock I never should
Have sold unless.... Ah, well, a man must earn his.
livelihood.
“Come to Our Patriotic Sale!”
“Cheap Goods While Times are Slack.”
With secret joy my ad. I nail
Beside the Union Jack,
And blithely make a hoarding of a stricken People’s
back.
VICTORIA,

A very excellent list of records is available this month
there being variety as well as quality.
The two concert selections are superb. “All Hail, Thou
Dwelling Lowly! ” is sung by Paul Althouse, who is new to
our ranks; while “The Evening Star,” Tannhauser, is
sung by Thomas Chalmers, always a favorite.
Two new artists contribute to the regular list, viz.:
Arthur Blight, “Beauty’s Eyes”; Hendrika Troostwyk,
“Springtime.”
Many old and favorite artists again make their appear-
ance this month, and in all you will be able to make an
extremely pleasing selection. Read the items carefully.

POLITICAL
POINTS
Till the other day practically all the gas
globes and lamp glasses that Australia used
came from Austria and Germany; now
some of them begin to come from Leich-
hardt, just outside Sydney. A few years
ago four or five co-operators set up the
little Federal works, and have pushed ahead
in a modest way. Now, with the lift the
war has given the industry and the promise
of an effective Customs duty, Melbourne
and Sydney people have been encouraged to
put in money and buy more brains and skill,
and there is a reasonable prospect that here
Australia will make for itself a whole lot
of the things hitherto made for us by people
who used the money to buy ammunition to
shoot us with.
“Socialist” seems vexed : —•
I’m rapidly getting to the conclusion that the
biggest humbug on earth is the all-wool Socialist
unionist —the sort that will swear on the smallest
provocation that he hates the Bloated Capitalist worse
than watered beer, and is ready to blither at any
time about State Enterprise and the Good of the
Masses. He invariably votes State Socialism —-or as
much of it as the ridiculous Party system will allow
him. Awhile back the N.S.W. Government proposed
to give him a bit of it and hang out a small,
timorous shingle in the bread-making business, with
the idea of purveying a cheaper loaf than the Pri-
vate Enterprise article. Did the alleged Socialist-
unionist enthuse? No fear! The Bakers’ Operatives’
Union jumped with all its feet on the scheme; it
would “throw men out of employment.” Their
troubles about the “masses” —the good old “masses”
on whose account they have shed so many troughs
of salt tears! A bit farther back, when the total-
isator was a little more threatening than usual, it
was the Clerks’ Union’s turn. The clerks’ worries
about the hospitals. The machine “would throw many
bookmakers' pencillers out of work” —so damn it 1
ANOTHER INFERNAL MACHINE.
The N. S. Wales Labor Confereuce passed a reso-
lution calling for immediate steps to secure the
closing of hotels at 6 p.m.
The Broken Hill Prop, is bringing to
Australia, under contract, expert craftsmen
for its new steel and iron works. Permis-
sion to do this was granted by the Federal
Government because this country, having
been content to buy its iron and steel from
the foreigner, has no skilled workmen on the
premises. _ The wages the imported people
will receive give an idea of the value of
skilled industries. Rollers will get from £33 to
£52 a month and melters £57 55., and other
workers will draw from 16s. to £1 12s. per
day. Three pounds a week is a good average
wage for the Australian workman, whose
political friends have been careful to keep
him mostly a hewer of wood and a drawer
of water.
THE EXPLANATION.
Mr. Fisher will also explain his strategic railway
proposals to the Premiers’ Conference —News item.
AUSTRALIA: “Those little crosses that look
like lots of little voles , what are they for? ”
FISHER: “They are—er—um—they are the
strategic points."
A Melbourne manufacturer, encouraged by
the war, has commenced the manufacture of
“Vienna” chairs on a large scale. Customs
Minister Tudor has inspected the business,
and intends to see that it is properly pro-
tected. “This,” moans the S. M. Herald,
“is apparently his answer to the deputation
which asked for a reduction of the duty on
chairs.” It is a refreshingly good answer,
anyway.
The three trawlers which Holman per-
sisted in importing from England in spite of
the Labor policy and the protests of N.S.W.
artisans who wanted the work for them-
selves, and of good Australians who abhor
the importing habit, have grown into six.
The Government intends to establish six
fish-distributing centres in the State; and
in unfolding this scheme (which is good in
itself) Chief Secretary Black mentioned “the
six trawlers which left Grimsby for Sydney
on February 15.” Holman gave a fairly
definite promise, when attacked about order-
ing three of the vessels abroad, that the
rest of the fleet should be made locally; but
his Government seems to have bought the
extra trawlers from sheer delight of out-
raging his party’s platform in as many
places as possible.
According to the Argus (7/4/T5) :
Mr. Mahon occasionally entertains excellent ideas.
The nationalisation of the drink traffic in Northern
Territory is not one of them ; the proposal to induce
returned soldiers to settle in the Territory after
the war is one. There is no reason, as Australia
will owe much to these soldiers, why Australia
should not pay the debt with grants of land in the
Territory.
The Tory sheet, as the mouthpiece of the
party which pretends to believe that only
the cheap nigger is able to stand the
allegedly terrible climate of the Territory,
thus cheerfully gives away the party game.
It can conceive no more fitting reward for
those who have served the Empire than a
grant of land in a part of the continent
where its political allies have for years been
shouting that white men cannot possibly
live !
“Scallywag” from the inside :
The service is dead tired of reading references to
the magnificent administrative abilities of the
lately-retired Carmichael, N. S. Wales Minister for
Education. T could fill 20 pages with instances of
rotten administration. Here are enough to go into
the available 20 lines.
This year he awarded scholarships entitling hoys
and girls to admission to High Schools, and found
out—later —that he had given 800 more than he
could provide books for. Of course the books will
come from England, arriving here when the hum-,
bugged students have done the best part of their
year’s work.
He obliged teachers to live in insanitary, cramped
dwellings; and if they couldn’t live in them, he
deducted the rent from their screw. One old teacher
(30 years in the service) has 15s. per week deducted
from his salary as rent for a house which he is not
living in, cannot live in, and has utterly failed to
find a tenant for at 10s. per week.
He paid foreign failures £250 to £3OO p.a. as
High School teachers, while those within the service
got from £lBO to £2OO p.a.
Under him the Department kept down expenses by
declaring a man inefficient just when he was due for
promotion. No teacher was given any reason why
he was “inefficient”; and 80 per cent, of the
“inefficients” were men and women who had earned
promotion by examination.
But this was his stroke of genius. He constituted,
within his own Department, an “Information
Bureau,” whose duty it was to provide newspaper
reporters with accounts of the marvellous work and
prospects of Carmichael and his Department. This
boosting bureau has been a huge success. In fact,
its chief clerk accompanied the boss on his travels,
and sat beside him in the aeroplane in which he in-
spected the schools of Europe.
There seems to be no doubt that Henry
M'illis. the Napoleonic person who held the
N. S. Wales Speakership, for a space, amid
a cataract of books, ink bottles, curses and
howls; who stilled the shrill scream of
Colonel Onslow and quelled the native
violence of W. 11. Wood and Danny
Levy; who taught the Assembly more
about the fine points of Parlia-
mentary procedure than it had ever dreamed
of; and who was finally defeated in his con-
stituency—there seems to he no doubt that
this latter-day Outcast of Poker’s Flat, Mac-
quarie-street, Middle Harbor, the Hunter and
elsewhere is to be made an M.L.C. Holman
promised him the distinction for services
rendered, and, whatever his other faults may
be, Holman stands by his friends. The re-
surgement of Willis will be a pity. He had
just begun to develop into a Myth. In a
place where no one screams or gets noisily
drunk or blasphemes or casts the furniture
about, he will be merely an anomaly. The
bird that has been wont to ride the storm is
of no use to anyone in a fowl-house. To do
its qualities justice it needs, at the least,
scope and a tempest. If Willis is not to
become an anti-climax the Government
should send with him to the House of Re-
pose, say, Fitzpatrick, or Cohen, in addition
to two or three of the Assembly’s leading
drunks. With no one to rebuke, Willis will be
dull and ordinary and banal —everything
that his grateful fellow countrymen have
learned not to expect of him.
THE AMENDED TOAST.
“Gev'men, his Mask Crash' Maj'sty er King
an the cause tem-tunprmce! [?]!"
The Council of Caulfield (Vic.) has get
into holts with Lands Minister Lawson, and
there may, if the latter sticks to his'guns,
be a brisk engagement presently. The
Council has some sort of control over the
local park, and decided that a slice of it,
which a football club mostly monopolises,
would be the better of a fence, to “keep chil-
dren from wandering into danger among the
players.” The fence it put up was solid
enough to stop a wild bull, was Bft. high and
topped with barbed wire. It also boasted a
gate, and the carpenter who erected it thor-
oughly realised the situation by inserting a
small pigeon-hole for the receipt of custom.
Lawson went and saw the thing and ordered
the Council to remove it. The Council de-
clined to do so; whereupon Lawson swears
lie’ll have the fence down. It is to be hoped he
will stick to his swear, because nothing would
give the local populace more joy than to see
a dozen stout councillors defending their
barbed-wire entanglement, while the youth
of the place bombarded them with tomatoes
and other ammunition. It is astonishing
that no sooner do citizens acquire a slice of
land for recreation than somebody straight-
way sets out to steal some of it. Why not
give the public’s land a rest and try to steal
some from private owners?
THE WISDOM OF THE OLD CROW.
The N. S. Wales Cabinet yesterday decided to set
apart 5,000,000 acres in various parts of the State for
forestry reserves. Certain areas will be planted if neces-
sary. The whole scheme, however, is still in
embryo.— News item.
THE YOUNG B IRD: “Oh, mother! Are they
really going to plant us a lovely new
forest? ”
THE OLD B IRD: “ Don't believe it, my dear,
till you see ’em with the tools.”
Having a Protective tariff, with a super-
tariff to apply to hoggish nations, Canada
doesn’t rely solely on primary industries, and
can give the Empire a valuable hand now in
arming itself. Several of the Dominion s
engineering firms are making shrapnel shells
and other unpleasant ironmongery; and
Toronto yards are working double shifts on
shipbuilding contracts. Australia, under a
revenue tariff, remains a hewer of wood and
drawer of water, and this year there is pre-
cious little water to draw, while the wood-
hewing business is overdone. Australian
State Ministries, it is true, are working
double time ; but that is at the job ot trying
to bite Colien for more loan.
THE PRIZE.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
“Now who will bring me Paris?’’ said the Kaiser to
his host
In the harvest days of August when the troop trains
rattled by;
And the Captain of the Legions muttered: “Paris,
please, or, toast,
And produce the morsel swiftly! We have other
fish to fry.”
So the legions went to fetch it, and the battle
trumpets blew,
While the Kaiser watched their progress down the
loud and dusty tracks,
Till a large and angry person came distinctly into
view,
And he saw that it was Paris—hefty Paris with an
axe.
“Now who will bring me London ?” said the Kaiser
to his men
In the wild November morning as the shells were
heard to sing;
And the Captain of the Legions with a useful foun-
tain-pen
Wrote the order: “Bring us London, for the Kaiser
wants the thing.
“You will find it sitting smoking just across the
narrow sea
With a football-match in progress and a beef-pump
going strong,
And the excellent umbrellas of the Peace Societee
Are the recognised defences that have kept it safe so
long.”
So the legions went to fetch it, and the Kaiser
watched them go
I p the long canals of Flanders till they neared the
narrow sea,
And the bogged and noisy cannon met each other in
a row,
While the gentle folk of Calais seemed expecting
them to tea.
Then the Prussian Guard, whose glory was as shining
as the sun,
Made a rush across the trenches that appeared to
do the trick,
Till a little piece of London caught its valor on the
run,
And the part that wasn’t corpses hadn’t breath
enough to kick.
“Ach, won’t some one bring me something?” said
the Kaiser in dismay—
Almost any kind of trifle would be coin orting just
now.
Kitchener has armies making that may find me any
day,
And the bear of all the Russias is behaving like a
cow.
“But won’t someone bring me something e'er the
final trumpet blows,
E’er the battle turns in earnest and the smoke has
crossed the Rhine —
Just a souvenir to cherish of the way we smote our
foes,
When I really was ambitious and creation looked
like mine.”
Then beneath the briny water crept a noiseless
submarine,
Hiding deep amid the seaweed, choked and blind,
but full of hope,
And on rare occasions lifting, when it thought it
was not seen,
From the surface of the ocean its affrighted periscope.
But it brought the Kaiser gladness at the end of
many days,
And a proud man was its captain when ho bore in
triumph back
Out of range of death and slaughter, ’mid the loud,
imperial praise,
The torpedoed wreck and remnant of a Grimsby
fishing smack !
N.S.W.

SPORTING
NOTIONS
John Wren sent a message to the Sydney
Tattersall Club crowd who were drinking- wine at
iris expense on account of his having done the, Don-
caster Handicap trick with gee-gee Carlin. Wren
hadn’t the face to turn up and say it himself, but
he commissioned his “representative” to say, in
response to the usual “Jolly Good Fellow” speech,
that he was not in favor of the totalisator. The
Bulletin makes the wowsers, the rooks and the
lkeys a present of Jack Wren’s solemn declaration.
The noble army of sharps and saints can have John
for their star recruit if they want him. lie is the
most humorous thing in converts that ever breathed.
As the proprietor of an illicit tote at Collingwood
(Melb.) Wren made tons of money—out of the local
working man for the most part. With the lawless
shop came a plague of the very sort of all-the-week
gambling which a lawful tote would abolish. And
when at last his system of barbed-wire defences,
etc., was no longer able to keep the Collingwood
scandal going, Wren put his money into pony race-
courses. To-day he draws a large income from
unregistered race meetings, where the tote will cer-
tainly not be licensed if turf reform comes to pass.
The machine was never intended to benefit individual
proprietors of racecourses who pouch the profits of
their circus shows, and are understood to have a
commanding interest in the principal “book” made
on their premises. Therefore converted John is an
ardent opponent of the tote. So ardent that he
couldn’t keep o.uiet about it. He had to advertise
the way his influence is working.
Sydney Tattersall’s Club meeting at
Randwick on Saturday provided yet another explana-
tion of the reason why bookmakers don’t use trams.
With but one successful favorite (and that a six to
four chance in a field of 21) backers must have
dropped enough during the afternoon to pay for the
upkeep for about 10 years of as many motor-cars as
there are bookmakers in the Ma State. Colugo, the
lonely animal which was picked to win the Youthful
Stakes by so many that the next favorite stood
at 10’s, is a superb chestnut colt that looks capable
of humping any burden. He is by The Welkin out
of'bn imported mare, and was bred by Ernest Clarke,
the naval brother of Rupert.
A disgraceful feature was the performance of Moon
on Flute I’layer in the Hurdle Race. The public
backed Flute Player down to seven to two. The
unhappy brute never had a shadow of a chance —in
fact, it dropped into second-last place at an early
stage and stayed there to the finish-—but Moon con-
sidered apparently that he was called on to make
a demonstration. He accordingly rode a series Of
gallery finishes long after it should have been obvious
to him that he had no more hope of getting a place
than he had of lumping the grandstand. Flute
Player came in streaming with blood. The stewards
turned the rider down for a month. What they
should have done was to forbid him ever to ’ wear
spurs again on courses controlled by them. Dpnimv
spurs are good enough for the rider who doesn’t
understand the need of sparing a beaten horse. The
trainer, by the way, was also fined £5 'crjr. id. or
something for his association with this event.
The Malster mare, Lady Denman, did the
appropriate thing by winning the Denman- Stakes.
She went out nominally at eights, but actually at
any figure in reason, so sure was Moses, on her
rather barren record of late, of her uselessness.
The Cup was won by Tartanus, from Narivo,
by a short head. It was the most interest-
ing race of a dull day. Narivo, the recent winner
of the A.J.C. Citv Handicap, was turned out in great
form by Paddy Nolan, and might have just got home
had Maher noticed the challenge of Tartanus a
fraction of a second earlieT-7" Kingsburgh, the Mel-
bourne Cup winner, finished third, beaten to the
world.
Of £36,895 distributed at the A.J.C. four
days’ meeting, N.S.W. owners pocketed £27,408,
Victorians £6038, Maorilanders £2749, and
Westralians £7OO. With nine races worth over
£lOOO each, there were some solid winnings. 11.
Berry (£.4201). A. G. White (£3322), and G.
Barnett ( £3042) were at the head of the list. It
was the greatest splash in A.J.C. history ; but, owing
to bad weather and postponements, the club will be
lucky if it scrapes out of the venture with much
profit.
* * * *
Grafton, the stud horse wl>:ch turned up
his hoofs last week, left a big legacy in Australian
blood stock. A figure-fiend tabulates that in 15 years
Grafton’s descendants won 1587 races, worth
£204,349 in cash. He headed the list of winning
stallions in 1904, 1907, 1908 and 1909.
* * * *
Mountain Knight, the Australian cham-
pion tliree-year-old, for whom £2lO was paid as a
yearling, has won £11,196 prize-money in two years.
* * * . *
“Moana”: The weakest spot about the
tote is its habit of being so beastly frank. After
every day’s racing in Maoriland the papers state
that “the sum of £98,735 passed through the total-
isator at yesterday,” or words to that effect.
But with Isaac McShortodds the whole punting
business is hidden away in a capacious bag. So
when the fight for the tote is won the opposition to
it won’t cease. It will go on for years. Even where
the machine is firmly established parsons are con-
tinually rising up to denounce it as “the device of
the Devil.” This because, when parsondom sees that
£98,735 was put through the machine the day
before, it promptly divides the sum into thruppences
and works out how many collection-plates would be
required to accommodate this vast amount and how
many churchwardens would be wanted to carry the
spoil round to the vestry. And most of this is
because the tote stands out in the light and shows
exactly what it is doing.
* * ' * *
The racehorses in England belonging to
the Austrian sportsman Prince Ivinsky were sold
recently under license from the Home Secretary,
but the “enemy profits” fund isn’t much the richer.
If the animals had the plague they couldn’t have
brought much less. One, which had been entered for
numerous races, was* knocked down at 11 guineas!
N.S.W. took on a big contract when it
put four tennis teams on the courts to meet Victoria,
South Aus., Queensland and Tasmania, proceeds to
go to the Belgian Fund. But unfortunately for Bel-
gium, Isaac Moses was also howling for relief at
Randwick ; and the Nose had it. Consequently the
amount of hard seat at Double Bay that wasn’t cov-
ered with spectator was painfully large; and the
“gate” was correspondingly small. Those who
did abandon Moses to his cruel fate had the plea-
sure of seeing- N.S.W. sweep the board. Victoria’s un-
representative team was badly beaten —the only man
who made a decent showing on paper being Camp-
bell, who beat A. U. Jones. This is the 55t;h time
the two States have met, and the win puts Ma on
top, 28 to 27. Likewise were Queensland, S.A. and
Tasmania shockingly handled by their hosts. The
Victorian ladies got much nearer victory, but gave
way to Ma’s representatives—6 sets 15 rubbers 142
games, to 6 sets 15 rubbers 139 games. The cham-
pionship tournament, which runs the whole of this
week with the finals on Saturday, threatens to add a
more respectable cheque to the Belgian Fund, especi-
ally as there is a fine assortment of inter-State
players to make things interesting, and also as the.
before-mentioned Isaac Moses will not be in need of
further relief for a week or two.
Western Suburbs showed its consistent
opinion of North Sydney in the cricket field on Satur-
day, when, for the second time this season, Austin
Diamond’s XI defeated the men from over the
channel, and thereby won the first-grade champion-
ship. It was a straight-out win (nine wickets), which
landed the three points needed by the Westerners to
bring home the cured pork. The five leading teams
ran out this way: Western Suburbs, 68 points ; North
Sydney, 62 points; Sydney, 61 points; Glebe and
Gordon, 54 points each. University, which won the
competition last year, was sixth on the list.
In Sydney’s cricket contest for the first-grade
pennant, just concluded, W. Bardsley (Glebe) headed
the batting averages with 66.88 runs; aggregate, 602
runs ; highest score. 146. The bowling averages were
topped by J. D. Scott (Petersham) with 45 wickets
for 387 runs —8.60.
* * * *
R- V. Minnett, one of a trio of N.S.W.
cricketing brothers, landed at Sydney station on
Saturday after having escaped uninjured from the
train smash on the Melbourne line, and hurried on to
St. Luke’s Park to play for North Sydney in the
final pennant match. The railway collision was a sort
of practice for the Great Collision in Europe. R. V.
leaves Sydney by the Morea on Saturday, and will
join the Army Medical Corps.
* * * *
V tutorial! Cricket Association, having
turned down the eight-ball-to-the-over “reform,”
which N.S.W. was anxious to see adopted, inter-State
cricket next season will be conducted as hitherto.
Via, however, will give the innovation a trial in grade
and junior inter-State and inter-Association cricket;
but; its value there as a means of making cricket
attractive to the public isn’t apparent. Incidentally,
I assy s is in favor of the novelty.

J. F. McManamey, newly-elected presi-
dent of N.S.W. Rugby Union, is an old hand h the
n ?, aCk “ 1882 he I-Hiv-eO in the first
intei-State football match against Queensland. In
later years and to the present generation, he has
been piobably the soundest referee in the game He
has been a member of the Union for 35 years, and
has held every office. The late J. J. Calvert filled
the chair for over 40 years, and thereby left a
substantial record for any successor to rub out.
“Touch”: The N.S.W. Rugby League
seems to have chilled feet about its programme-
despite-the-war policy. The commencement of the
season has been put back a week, from April 24 to
May i, on account of the march through Sydney
streets of the 5000 troops forming Ma’s quota for
the next Expeditionary Force. From the'way the
League first, talked, it seemed as if it thought that
its hoofball was really superior in interest to the
war, but apparently it isn’t game to test its hold
on the “gate” against Australian soldiers on the
march.
* * * *
When Granville (N.S.W.) Soccer foot-
ballers reckoned up their performances last season
they found that a State record of wins had been piled
together. These were the Gardiner Cup and N.S.W.
championship, Rawson Cup and honors caps, and the
six-a-side competition. When they talk about
Granville as a soccer recruiting ground they spell it
Grandville.
* * * *
Did anyone say there was no money in
football ? The Panama Pacific Fair people asked
that the annual match between Stanford and Cali-
fornia be played at the Fair. Reply: “Yes, if you
pay .£12,000.” That is the usual amount netted
at the gate.
* * * *
A. Rollinson won the half-mile profes-
sional swimming championship of N. S. Wales on
Saturday in 12min. 43sec. He was dilatory. Amongst
the pros., Billington, of Londoti, has been more
expeditious by lmin. 6sec., and Barney Kiernan has
been livelier still—llmin. 11 3-ssec. And he was a
mere amateur.
* * * *
Amendments to the record books were re-
cently made at Chicago during the Amateur Athletic
Unions Swimming championship meeting. Michael
McDermott cut the 100yds. and 200yds. breast-stroke
times to lmin. 13 2-ssec. and 2min. 38 2-ssec. re-
spectively. Perry McGillivray, in the 250yds. open
swim, pulled the time down to 2min. 50 Lssec. and
travelled 300yds. in 3min. 26 l-ssec. The USA
record of limin. 29 l-ssec. for the 880yds., credited
to G. M. Daniels, was sponged out by Harry Ilebner
who put up llmin. 14 l-ssec. in its place. In cover-
ing the distance, Ilebner broke no less than 10 U.S.
records, commencing at 330vds.
Bilf S.st. 10]])., Bash Bst. 10|1b.,” bawled
1 ie master of ceremonies, indicating two youths in
the ring at Sydney Stadium, and crawled away
through the ropes. They looked very lonely, these
boys, m the fierce light which beats on the meeting
place of so many pugs. “Shake hands,’’ said the
referee. They shook. “Box on!’’ Biff slid quickly
into position. Inside lOsec. he wished he was ill
rlandeis, for Bash had cut loose with the vim of
a healthy tornado. To the unhappy Biff the atmo-
sphere seemed full of gloves hitting him from everv
angle with rude violence. One to the jaw, another to
the place where liis dinner was—a flush on the nose.
Bill pawed feebly in reply, weathered storm number
one, and sank into his corner with a shattered mouth
and trembling with a sense of outrage. Bash glared
from over the way. “Why the Devil don’t you push
your left out?” hoarsely, demanded the man with
Impressions at the State Amateur
Championships.
the sponge. “O Lord!” panted the harassed Biff.
Then he added: “Put the towel in"- —and in it went.
He turned it up without shame, a. likely-looking
youngster, too, firmly built and lithe. Bash stalked
to the dressing-room with the proud step of a
conqueror.
“Smash—Dash.” The next pair of feathers were
introduced. “Box on,” and for a couple of rounds
they pranced around and tapped one another harm-
lessly. “Go in and hit him, and keep going,” whis-
pers the guide, philosopher and friend of Smash;
and as gong calls he leaps for the fray. Dash’s nose
springs quickly a-leak and a hostile glove plasters
the gore in a sticky mass across his face. Bang
comes the brown weapon again, for Smash is still
going in, and Dash takes it on the neck, staggers in
his tracks, droops just a little —then bang some more.
Dash’s head smacks the board. He is comfortable
now, at any rate, and dreams he can faintly hear
someone counting 10 away on the Western Plains.
“Out” comes in a mighty chorus. Someone helps
him up, gives him a drink, and, grinning" feebly
round, he looks for the apparition of Smash. But
that purposeful youth has skipped smilingly away to
bask' in the sunshine of victory.
And so they come on, one pair after another, to*
compete for the amateur boxing championships pf
N. S. Wales. Some are painfully crude —so crude in
a few eases that there seems to be need. fpr some
restraint in receiving nominations. Others show
form which varies from fair to promising; and in
every ease a frantic anxiety not only to win, but to
get there by the K.O. route: can be noticed—a grave
weakness this with an amateur of only moderate
skill. Time after time there were bouts in which
the obvious winner exhausted and discounted himself
by a feverish hunt after the other fellow’s point.,
The show is vastly amusing at times. There was
more fun at the Stadium on the night a Bulletin
Jnan wandered in than he had seen in Sydney for
many a day. They box in all shapes and styles,
these amateurs, from the long and the short to the
serio-comic. Mosquito and Peanut are summoned.
“Box on!”—and before the exhortation has ceased
the gallant bantams are tied in a knot, their writh-
ing frames the very embodiment of Stoush demented
to the last degree of willingness. For 60 frenzied
seconds the battle rages without let-up. Then a
chance blow brings Mosquito down ; and Peanut, like
Cortes on the lonely peak in Darien, looks pantinglv
down. lie sets Mosquito at his feet —in his ears the
sound of choral music. Happy Peanut! He will
light in the welter-class next. year.
“Hooray!” roared the crowd, and sat up like
kids, at a pantomime. Streak and Freak were ap-
proaching one another with the exaggerated delib-
eration of automatons —Streak, his hair correctly
parted, and most becomingly' attired ; Freak, nearly
Oft. of him, inside a loose and ancient bathing suit,
sadly' chewing gum and waving long, thin arms like,
blackberry brambles. Streak, having given due.’
notice of his intention, passed out a punch much in
the manner of a lackadaisical duchess serving an ice-
cream. Freak deftly flicked it aside, gravely smacked
Streak over the jaw, stepped about 7ft. away in one
stride, and opened his face into a wide, inviting
smile. The audience rocked with glee and howled
encouragement. ■ Streak came to call again with
more suddenness, but in stepping up to knock at
the front door ran into a dark lump of leather
which, being abruptly shot forward, made point of
collision between the eyes. Stung by this mishap
into retaliatory fury, Streak administered Freak an
emphatic smack on the left cheek ; but instead of
finding the right then turned towards him for
attention, himself took a jolt in the midriff. (Loud
applause.) Streak went back to his corner wonder-
ing whether his friend was a take-down. Freak sim-
ply chewed away at his gum, looked affably round,
and had a slice of lemon. As a turn at one of the
theatres this bout would have a place all to itself
on the bill. Freak hopped about like an animated
lathe, with a mingling of mournfulness and amia-
bility in his bearing so queer as to be inimitable.
In the intervals the dejected Streak was urged to
get in to close quarters; and though he struggled
manfully to do this the elusive batliing-suit either
leaped away or disdainfully pushed him off by' the
eye. It was the most popular item of the evening,
and the verdict, “Freak the winner,” was received
with resounding cheers. Freak, still industriously
chewing, vanished gloomily and alone.
* * * *
The smartest exhibition of boxing seen
between lightweights for many a day in Sydney was
given by Jack Clune and Jimmy Hill at the Stadium
on Saturday. Hill brought into the ring, besides a
little extra poundage, all that exceptional skill by
which his long mastery in the feather division had
been sustained. Clune has greatly improved, and
Hill, whcr started out with undisguised confidence and
the customary, jsWitrffc, qdickly found himself with a
much inoTe- difficult proposition on hand that he
bargained for. dune’s left was a disturbing feature
of the proceedings, and Hill ran into it oftener than
was good for him. On the other hand, Hill’s light-
ning-like counter with the right was for a time
mightily disturbing to Clune. But as the fray pro-
gressed Clune beat this form of assault, and at the
eighth had that portion of Hill’s equipment nicely
measured up. Both men at times miss'ed badly, but
in each case the failure was due more to the brilliant
foot and head work of the other fellow than to serious
mistake on the part of the smiter. The hitting was
well and truly delivered, but with a difference. When
Clune' hit he smote with effect, of. which the sudden
backward* jolt of Hill’s head gave frequent evidence.
It was a Hill moving in who took such biff as came
his. way—it was a backward-shifting Clune who
absorbed his share. Clune had taken three rounds in
succession up to the twelfth, and then the end
came. A quick uppercut with the right took Hill
square under the chin and lifted him flat on his
back. At nine he moved, but heard “out” come
before reaching his feet, and the bacon was Chine’s.

The interminable argument between
Herb McCoy' and Frank Thorne was continued at Bris-
bane Stadium on Saturday night. The bone of con-
tention this time was the lightweight championship
of Australia, and' at the end of an hour’s fighting
(20 rounds) the ring-magistrate-found.lor the holder,
McCoy, with costs on the higher scale. The crowd
said it couldn’t have given a better verdict itself.
Probably Clune will get the next chance to snatch
the title from McQoy.
* l * * *
Tommy McCarty, the Irish-Ameriean
who led Colin Bell through a 10-rounds bout on
Saturday at New York, is half an inch taller than the
Australian heavyweight and six years younger—23
against Bell's 29. He has a long list of small
knock-outs to his credit. Bell has not done so well,
but he has done it in much better company.
“Rastus” : The instinct of the race holds
true. The cable announces that Jack Johnson, de-
feated by Jess Willard, has left Cuba via Mar-
tinique for France, where lie will take to raising
chickens.
IT. Martis, of Wanganui (M.L.), is
credited with having raged over the 100yds. last
week, at the Mart on Athletic Club’s sports, in
9 3-ssec. Maybe ; but before swallowing the time
The Bulletin* would like to know how many
watches there were and what experience their
holders had. Every now and then there comes along
breathless information of a runner cutting out the
hundred in 9 3-ssec., but verification appears to
fall down dead by the wayside. Incidentally,
track experts are arguing as to whether, in 9 3-ssee.,
man has reached his limit for the hundred. The
bulk of opinion seems to be that he has. It is true
that A. B. Postle made the journey in Australia at
Christmas time, 1906, in 91sec., but the running
was downhill (3ft. drop). Also, three timers swore
to 9 l-ssec. by B. P. Williams (pro.), at New
London, Connecticut. (U.S.A.), in 1905, and officials
of Tokio University took their affidavit that a
scholar named Minoru Fujii, on a grass track, in
1902, made 9 2-ssec. ; but, rightly or wrongly, doubt
roosts on both performances.

After all the groanings of the print-
ing press and the elaborate organisa-
tion of foreign correspondents, how little
the world knew! When the war started
the British belief in Russia and France was
only equalled by the immense opinion of
England held by Russia and France. Rus-
sia was the steam-roller that would go
slowly forward and crush all opposition;
France was unexpectedly prepared and could
hit hard and soon. Even Belgium was hold-
ing its own, and everybody was amazed
when Liege and Namur crumpled up. The
only country which hadn’t the vestige of a
hope was Servia. Yet Servia, in proportion
to its possibilities, and considering its posi-
tion, has done the best of all, and its people
will have respect as fighting men as long
as there is a pig left in the country to feed.
The N. S. Wales Cullens, represented in
the front seats nowadays by the Chief Jus-
tice and Lieutenant-Governor, though much
given to church-going, never could quite
make up their minds which church was the
right one. The Methodist described the
C.J.’s father as “a portly, well-set-up North
of Ireland man, of fair education and good
presence, and a preacher of parts....He
seceded from Methodism to establish a ‘Free
Gospel Church,’ which, however, did not long
survive.” The Chief lias a brother who was
a Congregational minister, but deserted the
pulpit for the press and Parliament. He
represented St. Leonards for a time, and is
now a journalist in Westralia. The C.J.
nailed himself down more or less by marry-
ing into the Dean Cowper family.

Why an
Eight-Cylinder Cadillac?
Because
the Eight-Cylinder Cadillac does more of
the things which a motorist wants his
car to do.
Because
the Eight-Cylinder Cadillac does things
with greater ease and with the expendi-
ture of less mental effort and less physical
effort on the part of the driver.
Because
the Eight-Cylinder Cadillac does those
things without intruding its mechanical
self upon driver or passengers.
Because
the Eight-Cylinder Cadillac possesses an
abundance of reserve power, instantly
available.
Because
the Eight-Cylinder Cadillac will run more
slowly on direct drive in crowded streets
and congested traffic, and will do it with-
out expert manipulation.
And it will “pick up” from slow run-
ning without hesitation or tremor.
Because
the Eight-Cylinder Cadillac surmounts
hills better and with less apparent effort.
Because
the Eight-Cylinder Cadillac negotiates bad
roads better, more easily, with less atten-
tion from the operator and with greater
comfort to the passengers.
Because
with the Eight-Cylinder Cadillac the
motorist experiences less fatigue on long
journeys.
Because
with the Eight-Cylinder Cadillac, every
enjoyment of motoring is intensified.
Because
the Eight-Cylinder Y-Type principle, with
the absence of vibration and steady appli-
cation of power, coupled with Cadillac
ideals and Cadillac methods of execution,
lends itself to long life and enduring
service.
Because
as we said at the outset, the Eight-
Cylinder Cadillac does more of the things
which a motorist wants his car to do;
it does them better, it does them more
easily, and it does them in a manner
which permits one to enjoy to the utmost
the things which contribute to motoring's
pleasures.
Finally
your first ride will compel you to dismiss
from your mind completely, the question:
“Why an Eight-Cylinder Cadillac” f

That doesn’t mean that nothing at all was
written off last year. Ordinary wear and
tear is provided for annually ; all the plant
and other property is valued every year, and
tlie items written down accordingly. The
wear and tear is pretty serious. Last year,
for instance, though over £2OOO was spent
on repairs, the outfit with which the co.
started on January 1 was reckoned to be
worth £8857 more than it was on December
31. Taking all things into a'ccount, the net
profit for the 12 months was astonishingly
good. The co. pokes up and down the
rivers, carting wool, etc., and hawking mer-
chandise ; and the rivers were open only for
a few weeks in the whole period. As for
trade off the river, the back country has
been feeling too sick to spend an avoidable
ha’penny; and, unless this co. had extra-
ordinary luck, the high price of fodder must
have cut into its profits. But it has gone
through all this and still shows net earnings
of more than 10 p.c. on capital; so the pro-
spects in the good years that are coming
for the back country are very cheerful in-
deed.
Dissection of the balance-sheet gives this
result

Like every concern which has much truck
with the sheep or wheat cocky, P., W. & Co.
have , to give a good deal of credit ; and as
their own capital is only £50,000, and as
their plant and stable and office property,
etc., run into almost that amount, P., W. &
Co. have to go into debt to somebody else in
order that the sheep and wheat cocky may
go into debt to them. The figures are, how-
ever, not at all appalling, and might reason-
ably have been much worse than they are
after two such years as the inlands have
had.

The directors don’t mention the war. Will
self-respecting shareholders put up with it?
Is everybody else to pose as a sufferer and a
martyr and not they? However, let them
console themselves with their old familiar
£8 17s. 9d. p.c., though things have to be cut
very fine to pay it. As against that skimp-
ing there was a special, non-recurring outlay
of £6OO last half-year—a vote to the widow
of the late secretary. A few years ago,
when Gillies started on his scheme to har-
ness up the power of the Great Lake and
provide Hobart with energy for big metal-
lurgical works, this co. rubbed its eyes out of
a long sleep. For years it had been pre-
tending to be an electric-light as well as a
gas co.; but it was only pretence. Gillies’s
co. got into difficulties afterwards, and
handed over to this co. its brisrht hopes of
lighting Hobart. Subsequently the Govern-
ment took over some other parts of the
scheme. Plainly, with this dangerous com-
petitor about, it was no time for this co. to
go on sleeping. Hence in the past four
years over £20,000 has been spent on capital
account in the gas department, and over
£40,000 on the electric light side. About
£40.000 of the money has been raised on
debentures and overdraft; only £9OOO has
come from shareholders. Up till then there
were practically no liabilities. Some day
Tasmania may wake, up and Hobart may
become the great seaport city Nature cut it
out for. Meantime, while it is dodging
along in its slow old way. this co.’s dividends
are likely to stay up in their present neigh-
borhood, though, with Launceston’s example
at the other end of the island, it seems
equally certain that Hobart will never put
up with high enough lighting and power
charges to increase the present rate of divi-
dend by much. Shareholders will have quite
as much luck as most people get if they hold
what they have.
* * * *
“Muttaburra”: lie the importation of
Californian oil-drilling rigs and crews.
“Queenslander” is quite right, and there is
no confusion of oil and water drillers about
it. Anyway, boring for either oil or water
means only the working of a glorified crow-
bar. the machinery being more or less
powerful, according to depth. A well-known
Rockhampton foundry holds patents for
several inventions in boring, and there are
at least half a dozen firms in Australia that
could supply all the rigs required at once.
As for crews, oil-boring countries have been
looking for them here before to-day.

A Noted
Hair Specialist.
There is a clever young English scientist, Margaret
Windeyer, practising her profession of hair and skin
specialist in Sydney, who is going to cause a stir
in this sunny land of ours by the extraordinary things
that she is doing- for the hair. She practises what is
known as the Thorl system of hair culture, under
which falling hair, even if it is coming out in hand-
fuls, as it often does, is stopped within a week or so,
and made firm in the scalp. The dandruff germ is
entirely cleared away so that it cannot return.
Thin, weak, short, broken, decayed hair is made to
spring into life and grow thick, long, strong and
beautiful. It is worthy of note, too, that this re-
markable system of hair culture has the power of
growing thick, healthy, firm hair upon scalps that
have been bald and bare of hair for many years.
The most amazing feature about it is that it entirely
cures grey hair, that is, it brings the natural
original color back to the greyest, whitest hair,
irrespective of whether the hair has been grey since
one was in one’s teens, or whether it is absolutely
all grey, or half or three-parts grey, or just going
gre.v. To have the lost color brought back to one’s
grey hair so that never again in the coming years
of one’s life can a single thread go grey, to be cured
of our baldness, falling hair, dandruff, etc., to have
our hair given such a start that it’ll just go on
growing thicker and longer year after year without
a bit of bother, worry or expense—that seems to us
to be the kind of relief that is worth seeking and
obtaining with all speed. Nearly the whole of the
first floor of the Bank of N.S.W., 228 Pitt Street
(opposite Palace Theatre), has been taken for this
young Englishwoman’s laboratories, consulting r,ooms,
etc., and it is pleasing to know that consultations
with her are free. There are private separate and
exclusive waiting-rooms for men folk and for women.
Country dwellers who send a few combings to Miss
Windeyer to be examined under the microscope will
be written to, free of charge, explaining what is
wrong with their hair and the cost of complete per-
manent cure. Her address: Margaret Windeyer, Hair
and Skin Specialist, Bank of N.S.W. (first floor), 228
Pitt 'Street, Sydney-.*

TO ARTISTIC (AND INARTISTIC)
CORRESPONDENTS.
Norris: At first glance it looks like a mad cow
trying to get out of a cave. When it turns out to be
merely the Kaiser, it loses interest. . . .William F.:

“Kuscobin”: I lere is a sample of
that Gerliog thoroughness which it is
blind folly to dismiss with a cheap
and small-minded scoff. As soon as
they had made Belgium their own
particular sty, they brought up meteorolo-
gists, and supplied them with field observa-
tories. With very little in the way of
appliances trained folk have long been able
to forecast a next-morning from the day-
before. By means of stationary kites a fog
can just as easily be forecasted —and that’s
worth knowing in these days of mechanical
flight. Notice the weather at the time of
German air-craft raids —you’ll find most of
them have occurred during fogs. The next
thing we’ll be offering is a meteorological
corps. Clement Wragge would go, perhaps,
if you allowed him a thermometer and a
press agent or two. In fact, at a pinch, he
might not mind going without the ther-
mometer. And if he’s too busy now with
his pineapples, there are 10,000 Australian
Imshmen who could take his place if you’d
let them carry along their ants and dies and
other forecasting implements.
"Gordon G.” on the gentle art of jury-
squaring. and how it is not done: —
A shady client goes to a shady solicitor with his
troubles. The shady solicitor tells him it’s a rotten
case, and, as such, one for desperate measures. Jury-
squaring is mentioned, and the game commences. The
shady client hands over to the shady solicitor (say)
L 200, to be judiciously'distributed in a good cause.
Whereupon the shady solicitor considers the position
fully and sums up thus: “Risks are dangerous and
ought to be avoided. By distributing this money I
shall be running a risk of being found out and a
risk of suffering dire consequences. Also, I shall be
running a. further risk of not being paid (or, at
least, of not being paid adequately) for my trouble
in taking risk No. 1. This client of mine is a very
wicked man, and should be punished for attempting
to commit bribery. I think I will keep this money
—I need it badly. Three things may happen to him
on his trial — (1) he may be acquitted, (2) the jury
may disagree, (3) lie may be convicted. In cases
(1) and (2) I can assure him that his good fortune
was the result of my assiduous labors, and quite pos-
sibly get some extra money from him. In case (3)
1 can assure him that I obeyed my instructions, but
my jurors turned dog. Should he disbelieve me,
it really won’t matter. The evidence of such a
scoundrel would not be believed. I have simply to
meet him with a plain, emphatic denial, and inci-
dentally throw in a few of the unpleasant things I
know about him. Yes. I think I will stick to this
money.” And that is how, in about nine cases out
of 10, when the shady client thinks he is paying to
square the jury, no rigging is really done.
ROLLING ON THE STRAIGHTS.
The active steward balanced
With a pile of shining plates
When the ship did start a rolling,
A rolling in the straights ;
A shark appeared above the water
When a woman on the Deck
Wrung her hands with horror
There was going to be a wreck.
A stoutish man did growleth
About the tossing of the sea
Said the steward have a soda
And don’t throw up to me;
Heedless of sick passengers
He mingled with his mates
As the gallant ship was rolling,
Rolling on the straights.

aching feet, general feeling of weakness and
irritability, and a drowsy, heavy feeling all
day long.
These symptoms are intended by Nature to
warn you that serious complaints such as
rheumatism, backache, gout, sciatica, lumbago,
gravel, stone and cystitis (inflammation of

IN
BANANA LAND
Dan Connelly, an ex-Mayor of Warwick,
is running against State Treasurer Tarnes
as an Independent. If Labor (which has
no earthly chance in Warwick) would sup-
port Connelly, the Barnes family would
have one seat less in the Queensland
Assembly. But the Queensland Labor party
hasn’t yet quite given up its preference for
helping an out-and-out enemy rather than a
three-quarters friend.
If Denham keeps his grip on the pony
after the speed trials next month; it means a
mandate for quinquennial Parliaments. But
the signs are that a lot of Tory will be
thrown into the digester on polling day.
Denham himself, though he is expected to
get back, is up against the ugliest customer
he has yet had to face in Oxley.
Bananaland has now 17 itinerant teachers
tracking the youngsters to the remotest out-
back. Their districts are so large that
Johnny and Mary are, as a rule, interrupted
only about twice a year. Really, it’s mostly
a sort of examination the teacher holds ; he
leaves a supply of literature for the lonely
kids to drowse over, and calls in six months
later to discover how much of it they have
absorbed.
According to a deputation which waited
on Treasurer Barnes at Brisbane the other
day, the State Government’s promise to
Fisher in 1913, that it would introduce legis-
lation to keep Asiatic aliens out of the
sugar industry, has proved about as reliable
as a wire-netting apron would be to ward
off mosquitoes. It was because of the under-
standing that Queensland should keep its
sugar industry free of brown, yellow, black
and purple brethren that the other States
agreed to stand the £6 duty on sugar. With
John White _ as Minister for Agriculture,
however, various Bananaland- mills are slyly
making big holes iri the fence their State is
pledged to keep in repair. The Sugar Culti-
vation Act allows the Minister to issue’ 4
exemption certificates to colored aliens, and
John has been so industrious that one
mill has 60 colored employees. They out-
number the whites by three to one. Out of
30 whites and about 40 aliens at another
mill, 18 plain whites and no colored sorts got
the sack in a bunch ori a recent pay-day.
There is more than a suspicion that the
affection of some mills for colored labor
is partly due to Nippon’s trick of giving
the overseer a cut from his pay. in return
for the privilege of being permitted to keep
his job.
Brisbane owns a Control of Prices Board
which draws a salary and does a few less
important things. It began by tackling
meat. At the weekly stock sales prices as
usual jumped about, varying between 325.
and 755. per hundred. The Board met
weekly and made the retail prices chase up
and down likewise. This got everybody,
including itself, so mixed that it cancelled
its meat awards and looked round for some-
thing else to do. It tried to persuade, the
bakers to stop deliveries, and sell at the
bakehouse only and at a cheaper rate.. When
the Board’s missus told it to call for the
family loaf on the way home, it saw ants
in that proposal, too, and told the bakers
to fetch the bread and charge any old price.
Looking round for something easier to regu-
late, file Board found that bran and pollard
had risen 90 per cent., butter was up to
1425. per cwt. and other commodities were
thriving likewise. So it congratulated itself,
locked the office door, put the key under the
mat, arid left for a.spell at the seaside. The
key is- still under the mat.
AN OFFER .
PASSING KHAKI: “What's the trouble, kiddy?"
K IDDY: “Eve chucked me girl 'cause she wants me to be a blooming officer. Sail in now
if yer fancies her."
Governor Goold-Adams —he comes of
“quite a- good family,” too. has been in the
Army, and, being 58 next June, is old
enough to know better—introduced the
Sosh tiger in the most brazen manner
to a mixed audience of respectable
people .at the annual meeting of Bris-
bane Hospital the other day. He
could -not understand, he said, why it
was that when a majority of the hospital
committee consisted of Government nomi-
nees the . State did not take the full
responsibility. The public went on con-
tributing towards the upkeep of the insti-
tution, and was apparently satisfied ; but it
was the most peculiar thing in regard to a
hospital that he had ever heard of. And
then,. while the audience’s hair was still
standing up on end, his Ex. observed that
he had noticed that only three cases of sun-
stroke and five of malaria were reported
during the year—-a remarkable thing for a
sub-tropical country, and proving to him that
the State was a white man’s land. Some-
body ought to be written to or something
done about a Governor who goes on tearing
the Fatman’s standard to tatters like this.
Unrepentant.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
They stood me up at the Judgment Seat,
The saints looked down at my stained feet.
They read the Roll
To my shaking Soul.
And told me, “Now you have reached the
Goal.
The times you slid
And the things you diet
Are now revealed with the sins you hid.
“You need not wince if the angels smile
To hear your story of human guile ;
For down below,
In the days ago,
They sinned themselves and they’B’ tell you
so; /
But, just in time, V
On the verge sublime,
Repentance came for the cure of Crime.
“The blots are black on your earthly page;
You erred like all of a puny age ;
You won’t deny
You were used to lie,
And shut your purse as the poor went by;
But, tell us this,
Did you find a hliss
In weeping tears o’er a life remiss?
“We’re not too stiff on the man who stole
And skinned the widow of house arid coal;
We’re not too hard
On the boozing bard.
Or the sensuous brute for the lives he
marred ;
We’ve pardon, too,
For the mu rd’ring crew
And the mad Malay with his opium brew,
“In case we find that they sobbed in time,
And grieved their plunge in the deeps o’
crime.
But you —you say
Never wept away
The deeds you did in the light o’ day.
Avaunt! Get rid
O’ the lout,” they bid ;
And I was aware of a lifting lid,
Of a gruesome smoke and a gleaming grid.
QUEENSLAND.
PHILANDER F.
QUEENSLAND GOSSIP.
Brisbane, April 17, 1915.
My Dear Thargomindah,—
Rejoice with us! We guessed right!
Our new Ex. and his missus are both good
“sports.” Ilis Ex. was asked, in a purely
formal way, to fire the first shot at the
South Brisbane Rifle Club’s new miniature
range, which is to be opened on Monday
night. He beamingly consented, but sug-
gested that he and Lady Goold-Adams
should fire a sighter and seven counting
shots against any member of the club and
his wife or sister. The Mayor and Mayor-
ess of South Brisbane (Alderman and Mrs.
Davey) promptly took up the challenge. I
don’t think that Mrs. Davey has ever
handled anything more murderous than a
sewing needle, but she is no quitter when
the stranger within our gates sounds a
clarion defiance from the bloated battle-
ments or something like that.
Their Exes, are keeping themselves fit by
appearing at public meetings and helping
to saw off motions, amendments and other
flotsam. On one such occasion ways and
means were discussed to provide recreation
rooms for soldiers. The Adamses promptly
threw in for the use of “the boys” their best
pianola. Another patriot passed along a
billiard table; another a piano. The allure-
ments of the bars where the glasses are
sluiced in a pig-trough and dried with what
looks like an old floor-cloth (I got these
details from a duke’s wayward son) won’t
be in it with those of Tommy Bananaland’s
recreation rooms. The difficulty is to obtain
suitable premises, but that will be overcome.
A. Id. Whittingham, the president of the
movement, got the dubious eye from the
women people when he moved that the
rooms shall be open to those who have
departed from the chalk-line as well as
those who have not. That is, that there
should be accommodation for the warrior
who has had perhaps too much liquor so
that he won’t be tempted to get more. I
voted for that, Thargomindah—l remem-
bered R. Kipling’s remarks as I firmly
shouted “Aye” :
There comes a night when the best get tight,
And women don’t understand.
Members of the Johnsonian Club met the
Governor the other day on their premises
amid a round of hand-shake and soda.
Mrs. Spencer Browne, who shortly will
depart this State, was the guest of honor
at an afternoon tea on Tuesday afternoon,
under the hostessing of Miss May Fitz-
gerald. On Thursday afternoon the
departing lady was At Home, and her
friends came from miles around to speak
the necessary “Safe Returns” to her and her
equally popular husband.
Mrs. Symes and Miss Mactaggart are
working like Trojans (Trojans were a sort
of ichthyosaurus, I think) for a concert and
dramatic entertainment which they are
arranging to hold on May 4 for that money-
glutton these days, the Red Cross. Our
Excellencies have promised to he there, and
the Mayor and Mayoress and the Comman-
dant and Mrs. Lee.
Several more weddings can no longer be
concealed. On Wednesday Hilda Love and
Ewen Spence were committed for life at
St. John’s Cathedral, some 150 of the
lady’s friends being present on the solemn
occasion. The bride was a dainty vision
in satin and lace, and the bridesmaids, Jean
Hardie and Hilda Spowers, were effectively
frocked in pale blue and lavender crepe de
chine, with flounces of shadow lace. They
wore blue velvet hats, with . water-lilies for
trimming, and carried bouquets of the same
flower. Lance Morrisset was best man, and
Ralph Gore was groomsman.
The same fell day saw a similar risk
taken by Eileen Hooper, eldest daughter of
Mr. and Mrs. W. J. Hooper, and Kenneth
Cameron, one of the Beauchamp Cameron
clan. Isa Hooper was bridesmaid, and a
Cameron saw his brother through the pro-
ceedings. The perpetrators were last seen
going in the direction of Sydney.
An engagement that has caused a flutter
in debutante circles is that of Thelma
Taylor, the brown-eyed daughter of the
Taylors, of Russell Park station. Surat, to
Alleyn Edols, eldest son of the Burrawang
Edols family (N.S.W.).
Mrs. Halford lent her home at Clayfield
to an energetic committee, which on
Wednesday afternoon draped it in flags and
sold tickets for a. bridge and tennis after-
noon. They refrained from offering prizes,
so every cent raked in was a cent gained
for the Clayfield branch of the Red Cross.
Premier Denham opened the new rooms
oi the Young Women’s branch of the
Q.W.E.L. on Tuesday night, and handed
over the key, swathed in blue ribbon, to
Mrs. Hulsen, the first president of the
xircT' was further blessed by Mrs.
W. S._ Anderson unveiling her portrait for
the edification of all good Liberal ladies and
the ornamentation of the new premises.
Whether it is cautious of the Liberal party
encourage an extra branch of the
Q.W.E.L. tor start business is another
matter. It is entirely due to the muddling
or the parent body of political females that
the Merthyr electorate finds itself in its
present queer position. Two Liberal candi-
dates are standing, and one, Lackey,; is
appealing to the law to tie his Liberal
brother’s hands and gag and leg-rope him.
Ihe other candidate is Bayley, a capable and
energetic young man who has been more
than hindered by his alleged helpers.
The busy Mrs. Forrest, Queensland’s most
prolific verse-builder, has been having a long
bout with the fiend of ill-health, and for
most of this year has been in the Victoria
fnvate Hospital in South Brisbane. Now
a ble to sit up and take notice. In
fact, the other day she made a rhyme; and
then she knew for certain she was getting
to be herself again.
Mrs. Milman, widow of the late Govern-
ment Resident at Thursday Island, and her
daughter, are staying in Berkshire while
the lights o’ London are shrouded. The
Milman family .was so popular in the tropic
isle that a wail of regret from its piebald
community followed the departure of the
feminine part of the menage after the
death of Papa Milman.
Yours affectionately,
AUSTRALIA JANE.
His new Excellency lias lost no time in
letting Queensland know that he has ideas
about agriculture and stock. He has made
the subject a hobby in the English-country-
squire sort of way, and at -Toowoomba
Show fired off one of his notions about the
sensible encouragement of breeding to suit
the surroundings. He would no longer, he
said, . allow stock to be judged by
“foreigners” whose chief qualification was
often the distance from which they were
brought. He would educate a class of
judges with knowledge of what the country
could and ought to produce for its parti-
cular purposes, and so set up a standard
which farmers could lay hold of as some-
thing tangible. National secretary Bain has
long been hammering away at this idea
with his Brisbane Council, and this Too-
woomba speech Caused Bain and the Coun-
cil to give each other the eye of' suspicion.
But the Gov. had really hit the idea off
his own bat, and now that it has had such
exalted commendation something may come
of it.


The Lahmann Health Home
MIRAMAR NORTH : WELLINGTON : NEW ZEALAND
€| The Lahmann Health Home s conducted on the lines of the original Lahmann
Institution in Europe, which accommodates some 35,000 patients per annum, and
where the Nature-healing treatment originated by Dr. Heinrich Lahmann has met
with such remarkable success. The Miramar Home is the only establishment of
its kind in Australasia, or, indeed, in the British Empire.
€]J The splendid successes achieved by the application of Dr. Lahmann’s methods
at the Miramar Home indicate that this system offers relief to many sufferers
from Chronic Diseases. The Institution is owned and conducted by a Medical
Director who is a qualified English Doctor.
The Medical Director is assisted by an M.D. of Zurich University, who has made
a special study of Lahmann methods under Dr. Lahmann himself, as well as of
Electricity, Hydropathy, Massage and Physical Culture in Sweden and Finland.
The Lahmann Health Home is an institution for the scientific treatment of Chronic
Diseases by the Lahmann “Nature-healing” methods.
The Lahmann System
€| The need for such a home as the Lahmann lies in the
many diseases which arise chiefly from the artificial conditions
of modern life, and which do not usually yield to a course
of medicine or an operation. Such cases call for a complete
change of living conditions; for the scientific application of
the healing forces of Nature. The means used are air, water,
sunshine, heat, cold, physical exercises, massage (both manual
and mechanical), electricity, electric light, electric current,
and the high-frequency current, besides carefully supervised
excercise and regulated diet. Consistent treatment upon
these lines has proved impossible in a patient’s own home,
hence the necessity for an establishment where every appli-
ance necessary is available under one roof, whilst the patient
is continuously under the doctor’s observation.
Comfort, Equipment, Climate
€][ Everything that can add to the comfort of the patients
and the efficiency of the treatment is included in the equip-
ment of the Home. Treatment-rooms for electric and hydro
treatment are fitted with the most up-to-date apparatus
available. *1 The social life of the Home receives careful
attention; the grounds include Tennis and Croquet Lawns,
and in the house is a Billiard Room with a good table.
Golf and Fishing are also available.
€J The climate of this part of New Zealand is particularly
bracing, and as the Home is situated amid the pleasant sur-
roundings of hill and harbour and sea, “the call of the out-
doors” finds a ready response from many guests, with
corresponding benefit to heatlh.
tj The Home is not a
Mental Hospital; and for
obvious reasons no infec-
tious cases (including
phthisis) can be received.
The diseases suitable for
treatment in the Home in-
clude most cases of chronic
suffering and ill-health, and
all the long list of nerve
troubles, from Epilepsy and
Chorea down to Insomnia.
Rheumatism, Uric Acid,
Indigestion and Dyspepsia
troubles, including Consti-
pation, have yielded great
successes to the Lahmann
Nature Treatment.
CJ Pleasantly situated at the head of the Miramar Valley,
just at the terminus of the tram-line, the Home stands with
its back to the hills, whilst from the front balcony one looks
over the suburb and golf-links of Miramar out to the sea
beyond the sand dunes. The house is thoroughly equipped
with all the necessities for massage, electric, balneological
and orthopoedic treatment. Bedrooms are heatable either
by open fires or electric radiators.
CJ Recently the Lahmann Home has taken over the entire
Electrical Establishment in Wellington of Mr. Axel Holtz.
Here is obtainable treatment by Electric bath, full or partial;
the Four-cell bath; and the High-frequency Current. All
these are available under qualified medical direction,
without extra charge to patients residing in the Home.
€J The weaknesses of con-
valescence following severe
illness or operation yield
speedily to the Lahmann
methods. Urinary Troubles,
Bright’s Disease and Dia-
betes; Constitutional Blood-
poisoning, Obesity, Chronic
Bronchitis and Asthma; in
the relief of all these the
Home has many successes
to its credit.
q Many cases of Chronic
Female Complaints, difficult
and abnormal Pregnancies
have also been treated by
the Lahmann methods with
remarkable success.
The Management invites enquiries from all whose sufferings are of the nature
indicated, and will be happy to advise or to correspond with the patient's medical
adviser as to whether any case is likely to be susceptible to the Lahmann Treat-
ment. The Lahmann Health Home Illustrated Prospectus will be posted gratis
on request. It contains full information as to terms (these are quite moderate).
address add enquiries to business manager.
THE LAHMANN HEALTH HOME

THE BOOKMAKERS’ TRINITY.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
I am disgusted beyond expression at the idea that
this great movement should be brought down to the
level of being told that the crowning end and flower
of it is to introduce the totalisator.— N.S.W. Premier
Holman, at the P.L.L. Conference.
“I quite agree with Holman,” said McMoses. (“I
agree
With Labor or with anything that holds the fort
for me !)
It fairly stabs my heart, it does, to see the Labor
Cause
Besmirched by men who only want the multitude’s
applause;
By men who size and sum me up, and my possessions
note—
Apostates of the Labor Faith, the tyrants of the
tote!
“A high and holy thing it is, this proletarian creed'
(As anything at all would be that helped me in my
need!).
Was ever cause so wonderful, so gracious, so sublime,
And so' cutely calculated to assist me all the time ?
A noble cause, a lofty cause, and we must keep it
clean
By fighting for the Right and Me—in place of the
machine!
‘‘ldeals are splendid things to have; and were they
lightly tost
Away to please the multitude—well, Labor would be
Lost!
And wouldn’t that be terrible? I shudder at the
thought
Of seeing all my Christian dreams and strivings
come to nought!
It must not be, it cannot be; we’ll let no demons
gloat
Above my crucified remains beside the sinful tote!
“Ask him, my friend the parson, who has fought the
holy fight
Along with me and Holman—just you ask him if
I’m right.
We have fought the fight together, hand in hand,
against the Shame,
(What matter if the Devil winked the day the
parson came!)
Let those who will their tauntings hurl, it’s plain
I’m sanctified—•
For Labor's and the Church’s saints are fighting by
my side.
“Oh! pass the book of daily pray’r—endorsement
that is God’s—
What time I ‘deaden’ punters’ choice and cramp
the winner’s odds;
And shriek the Labor sentiments, the sort that
Holman plans
For me to fleece the simpletons who back the ‘also
rans.’
Three cheers for him who rescues souls; three cheers
for Holman’s vote—•
For both have worked for God and Me against the
sinful tote!”
N.S.W.
R. J. C.
