HAS MONEY TO LEND
Novel Method for
Waving the Hair.
TOILET TALKS.
MANY a charming face is spoiled by
straight, lank, and excessively greasy
hair. Naturally curly hair gives a most
delightful effect even to a plain face, a fact
which has unfortunately; been known for
many years. This knowledge alone has re-
sulted in the ruination of thousands of heads
of beautiful hair by slow torture from that
terrible instrument, the curling iron. Im-
agine, if you can, how the. living hair squirms
and twists under such treatment. Yet this
is the very result you aim at. Well, I have
no doubt that each one individually is of the
opinion that the results justify
the meas, but let me tell you
that the reckoning will have to
be paid, and in a manner which
will be far from pleasant. The
twists and curls created- by the
hot iron are the hairs’ dying con-
tortions, and it is only a question
of time when you will have no
hair left to torture. If it is
absolutely necessary to have
wavy hair, then there is a far
more simple and harmless pro-
cess which any woman may
adopt without fear as to the
results. Get from your chemist
two ounces of silmerine, and
pour about two tablespoonfuls into a saucer.
With a clean toothbrush apply this to the
hair upon retiring. You will be quite
amazed at the result, and one application
will last for many days'. Damp weather
need have no terrors for you if you take
these simple precautions, and straight, wispy
tails will be converted into tight little curls,
giving a charming and pfettv effect, even to
i plain face.
Note. —Silmerine has not been used to any
extent for this purpose, but most chemists
keep a small supply in stock.
NOW about shampoos. Is it reasonable
to expect that one particular make will
suit everybody? Certainly not. Some
â
folks have dry and brittle hair, others have
tresses which are excessively greasy. Each
requires a different grade of shampoo, if one
is to obtain the best results. The writer’s
notice was recently brought to a substance
called stallax, which can be obtained from
most chemists. It consists of small gran-
ules. which, when mixed with a cup of hot
water, make the most charming mixture
imaginable for shampooing the hair. Ac-
cording to the quantity used, so the strength
is graduated to suit all conditions. There
is one drawback, however, and that is stallax
can only be obtained in ilb. tins,
which cost about half-a-crown,
but as this quantity is sufficient
to make 25 to 30 shampoos, it
is really very economical in the
end. It leaves the hair a little
too wavy and fluffy for some
tastes, but this can always'bhe
avoided by rubbing a little olive
oil on the scalp previous to its
application.
A FEW remarks on face
creams may not be out o F
place, and at the risk of being
put down as a little old-fash-
ioned, my advice on this subject
is to leave well alone. Do not
dog the pores with greasy concoctions
which not only prevent the skin from
performing its proper functions, but
usually result in encouraging a growth
or hair on the face, and you know
very well what that means —disfigure-
ment for the rest of your natural days.
If it is necessary to use an emollient at
all, get some mercolised wax from your
chemist and apply in the usual way.
This remarkable substance seems to absorb
the worn and weatherbeaten outer cuticle,
and in a few days’ time the new, healthy
skin underneath peeps forth in all its
brilliance, and is, of course, quite free from
imperfection or blemish. P

Big Ben is always on the job
BIG BEN is on the
job every minute of
the day; he rings on
time —he runs on time —
he stays on time.
He has a great big dial
you can easily read in the
dim morning light a
cheerful deep-toned voice
that wakes you on your
sleepiest mornings —large
strong winding keys that
are a pleasure to wind —
and selective alarm calls
that ring just when you
want and either way you
want, steadily for 5 min-
utes, intermittently for 10.
Big Ben is made in La Salle ,
Illinois, U.S.A., by Westclox.
His price is 12/6, postage 1/-
extra. If you cannot find him
in your town, send a money
order to the makers’ Australian
office, Westclox, cr. King and
Kent Sts., Sydney. They will
have one mailed to you at once
by the nearest jeweller.
The Inky Way.
“Gnat Spry”: Redmond, of the Sydney
Sun, who was killed in the Manly motor
smash, was a sterling news-editor, primarily
because of his genius for organisation. It
was a treat to watch him at work when
any big job was on. The bigger the strain
the steadier he was. Remembering what
he could do as a descriptive writer —his
political work in Melbourne and his Argus
story of the Burns-Jolmson fight in Sydney,
for instance —I always regretted that lie
was a mere news-editor, whose business it
was to see that other men wrote, while he
did no writing himself; but these regrets
vanished when I saw him on busy days, and
perceived the facility with which he
arranged the staff to give the best that was
in them. He was a good pressman, manly
and sincere, with a frank, pleasant style,
and when he went West journalism in
Australia was a man short.
# # * *
C. E. W. Bean’s complaint against the
conduct of Australian troopers in Cairo
raised a whole gale when the report got
back to the camp by the Pyramids. The
author hurried to despatch a message that
his “copy” had been misunderstood, or muti-
lated, or something—that he had made it
clear in his story that he was referring not
to the men in general, hut to a section of
wasters who had since been weeded out.
But Bean, like many another good press-
man, found just how difficult it was to catch
up with and slay the thing that raised the
trouble. In addition to the difficulty he
found it very unpleasant. It was rough on
the correspondent, because he is a great
admirer of Billjim Australia, and a consci-
entious trier to get at the right and wrong
of things he expands upon.
* * vs- *
Another inky wayfarer moves off to the
interminable Front from Maoriland. That
is the editor of Kai Tiaki (The Guard-
ian), the journal of the M.L. nursing pro-
fession. When she isn’t editing Miss H.
Maclean is Assistant-Inspector of Hospi-
tals. She is now commandant of the con-
tingent of M.L. nurses enlisted for the
scarified fields of France.
*- X K- ■*
“W.T.B.’s” few remarks from London :
In The Bulletin (7/1/T5) a pardon, name of
Matthews, defines a wowser. Now this very subject
was under discussion at the Press Club here a few
nights ago. You see, London has a real, genuire,
well-equipped Press Club at last. Originally we
had a place in Wine Office Court, which, owing to
its general appearance, was known as “The Pot-
House.” Just as the lease was flickering out we had
a nice little fire, but some idiot put it out. How-
ever, we had another a few days later —a real one —-
and then we had to shift. Our r.ew club is at St.
Bride’s House, Fleet-street; and Kennedy Jones, who
was responsible for re-organising Waring and Oil-
lows, Ltd., and who runs a few racehorses and other
necessary luxuries, attended to the furnishing. We
haven’t paid the bill yet, because it isn’t safe to
give people too many shocks at once. I think if
Willie Holman would come along and raise a 41
per cent, debenture to cover the cost he would be
elected an honorary member for life, with his por-
trait in oils hung in the club’s art gallery amongist
the caricatures, by Arthur Moreland, of the mem-
bers who have in the past done something they
ought to be ashamed of. What we were trying to
get at the other night, as I was saying, was a true
definition of “wowser,” the word not being in general
use in Loan-land —owing, of course, to the war.
The accepted was: A wowser is an individual who,
if by any means he could be suddenly changed from
mortal form into one of those watches which certain
ultra-smart girls wear on their ankles, would con-
tinue to keep his hands in front of his face.
* * * *
“Copy Boy” : Defence Minister Pearce has
taken the only course open to a good union-
ist by issuing instructions that parsons with
the expeditionary forces are not to increase
their bloated salaries by sending “copy” to
the press. More than one daily that did
not rush Official Correspondent Bean’s work
(all of which can be obtained at a very
moderate price—somewhere about £6O a
year) has shown a willingness to accept “ex-
clusive” drivel at dog-poor rates from gentle-
men who button their collars at the back;
and the fact that they have been scotched
calls for a round of drinks.
* * * *
Malcolm Ross, appointed by the Massey
Government to the futile post of Maoriland’s
official “war correspondent,” is a more-or-less
free-lance journalist who has done a lot of
work from Wellington, particularly for
South Island Tory newspapers. For many
years he was M.L. correspondent of London-
Times, and more than once fell foul of
Richard Seddon in that connection, “King
Dick” accusing him of bias. He recently
published an extensive work on mountain-
eering in M.L. The runner-up for the job
was E. V. Hall, of Wellington Post, who
not long back showed Sydney Telegraph
some of his fine ability, but could not be
induced to remain.
* * * *
“T. 0.1.”: Apropos paper-making in Aus-
tralia, which is again suggested, I have a
copy of the “Annual Report of Public
Schools in N. S. Wales, 1867,” inscribed:
“This book is printed on paper made in
the Colony. Henry Parkes.” The paper
is now dark, but of fairly good quality.
MONOMANIA.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
I have no passion fierce for gold;
I’m prodigal with drink, 1 fear;
Nor land nor houses do I hold ;
But I’ve a miser’s passion here
Hid in my heart nought can divert.
With one thing I am mean }s dirt.
I hoard it up in little piles ;
I steal it, too, whene’er I can ;
I wheedle it with subtle wiles
From Inky Jim, the printer’s man,
And in my loom ’gainst future need
I’m storing it with shameless greed.
Bold burglars may invade at night.
Moths may corrupt—l do not care.
But Hell afflict with lasting blight
The wretch who wanders to my lair
And steals, or wastes with whim or caper,
My own, my cherished copy paper!
VICTORIA.

A Natural Remedy
Time was when disease was thought to be due to the
direct influence of evil spirits, and exorcism and magic
were invoked to cast it out.
Science has taught us wisdom. The evil spirits exist
still. We call them “Disease Germs,” and they also
must be cast out. Once lodged in the stomach or
intestines, fever with its hallucinations, or biliousness with
its aches and pains, is the result.

The Lily and the Landlady.
A TRAGIC ROMANCE OF OYSTERS,
AFFINITY AND TWELVE POUNDS
FOUR AND SIX.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
AN angry person steep with hair
Chafed his large boots upon my
mat;
My landlady, in stark despair,
Threw Chidley’s “Answer” at the cat ;
In various corners of the town
Men sought my bright career to fix.
For I was lord of half a crown,
And owing twelve pounds four-and-six.
The solid facts of life were stern :
My means of livelihood were thin ;
Cold cash is ever hard to earn
By those who neither toil nor spin,
And seeing that all things looked blue
I wandered far and far away,
Observing how the lilies grew,
And how the sunlight warmed the day.
At certain seasons it is good
To note how little ants may strive
Collecting their supplies of food
To keep their busy jaws alive ;
But, on the whole, I much prefer
To sit with lilies in the sun,
The while that great Artificer
Makes bright the robes His rays have
spun.
One mile beyond where Mosman ends
The Middle Harbor softly winds
By little bays and coves, and bends
Where oysters rest with quiet minds,
Knowing that their envenomed shells,
Replete with germs of typhoid drear,
No midnight Dago plucks and sells
Unless the coast is very clear.
They sit and slumber in the wet
Because the odds are on their side,
Or, waking without murmur, set
Their valves to work to eat the tide.
I envied those untroubled lives
Which knew not gladness nor despair,
Nor any trick that Fate contrives
To catch the heedless unaware.
Lilies and oysters have their place,
Teaching the simple life to men ;
But what a marvel of a face
The clear blue summer showed me then!
Her lunch was spread upon a stone,
She ate the remnants of a peach,
A happy damsel all alone
On three brown yards of shelly beach.
I introduced myself with speed.
In case the vision should depart
And leave me in my utter need
With large commotion of the heart.
She smiled. The world was very good.
She gave me welcome, kind and shy.
All things were suited to my mood.
And golden winged the hours went by.
At twelve o’clock I saw her first.
I offered her my love at three.
At just a quarter-past I nurst
tier numerous charms upon my knee.
She was to marry me next day.
The thing was easy to arrange.
Though I had many pounds to pay
And only two and six in change.
At five o'clock we said farewell.
At six I reached my cheerless room ;
Around my entering steps there fell
A silence as of coming doom.
Men hold landladies have their use.
T did not feel the need of mine.
I knew my homing would produce
A flood of rhetoric fair and fine.
I knew that T should hear again
Remarks about the price of beef,
And that the everlasting strain
Of household bills was past belief.
I knew (hose pleading tones and grave
With which I should be asked to find
The necessary pash to save
That simple homestead poor but kind.
She came amid the scented dusk
That crept about the creaking stair;
Sweets as of cabbage, lamb and musk
Lay heavy on the moveless air.
She spoke, and as she spoke she wept.
My soul was filled with vague alarms,
Remorse through all my being crept,
I drew her weeping to my arms.
The gas leaned up upon the stair.
The housemaid uttered one shrill cry
To see her mistress, stout and fair.
Dropping slow tears upon my tie.
Then our engagement was declared —
It seemed the onlv thing to do.
And I whom fate till then had spared
Was pleasantly betrothed to two.
I turned the matter in my mind
And viewed this new entanglement.
I wondered whether I shoidd find
The marriage status worse than rent.
Th“ sweet girl sitting far from town
Was bright as drinks that barmaids mix,
But I was lord of half a crown
And owing twelve pounds four and six.
LiJios and oysters have their nlaee.
They whisper of the simple life;
A maiden with a perfect face
May prove a most expensive wife.
Dark is the visage of the dun.
But fortune keeps me still afloat
In that establishment we run
Five minutes from the Mosman boat.
N.S.W.
PAT O’MAOI.
Shortening Sail.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
ALL day long the wind has been
steadily increasing, whilst the glass
has been just as steadily falling,
until at sunset it is standing at 28.89 inches.
This perhaps does not convey very much
to the average landsman, but when making
down for the Horn, in 56deg. South lati-
tude. with winter coming on, it is apt to
make the seaman ponder over a misspent
life. The sun, after showing at fitful inter-
vals throughout the day, has gone down
behind a bank of ragged copper-colored
clouds; wild, wicked looking clouds, with
great rents and gashes torn in them, as
though some gigantic hand is trying to rip
them to pieces. Overhead, the lower strata
of clouds are travelling before the storm
with ever increasing violence, whilst the
wind, shrieking through the rigging, takes
on a newer and shriller note as it grows
stronger.
The Old Man, who has the reputation of
being a “beggar to carry on,” is on the poop,
watching keenly all there is to be seen
before darkness falls. He does not want to
shorten down if he can help it, as this is
a fine, fair wind, and we are throwing four-
teen good miles behind us every hour. At
the same time he quite recognises the risk
he is taking, in view of the very low glass,
lie knows that by the morning it will be
utterly out of the question to heave-to, as
with the sea that will be running then no
ship could live when once in the trough of
it. And to continue running will be just
about as dangerous, as there is always the
danger of “broaching to” ( i.e ., coming up
broadside on to the sea. the most dangerous
position there is in heavy weather), and
that would mean the end of all things. We
still have our upper topsails set, although
they should have been in. or, at any rate,
reefed, long ago. The skipper takes a final
look round, and decides to keep going.
Night is coming on. The sun. now almost
on the horizon, peers out sullenly for a
moment through a rent in the storm-driven
clouds, and then is seen no more. the old
shell-backs, men who have been rounding
Cape Horn (or, as they would term it, Cape
Stiff) for thirty years or more, shake their
heads solemnly as the sun disappears. One
of them, voicing the thoughts of the rest,
remarks in a croaking voice: “Wonder
where we’ll be when that there sun turns-to
’n’ gets up agin.”
As darkness sets in the scene grows yet
wilder. Monstrous seas rear their foam-
crested heads behind us as though striving
to overwhelm us as they break. But always,
just in the nick of time, when it seems as if
another second will see us engulfed, we rise
to it, and. as though disappointed at not
crushing us, the wave roars away along the
ship’s side, towering high above the fore-
yard as it spends itself ahead of us. Very
careful steering is wanted just now, for a
moment’s inattention on the part of the
helmsman would probably mean the loss of
the ship.
“Ting-ting. . . .Ting-ting. . . .Ting-ting. . . .
Ting-ting!” Eight bells strikes, and the
men struggle aft to muster on the poop,
fighting their way through the swirling mass
of water that was once the main deck. The
Second Mate calls over the names of the
watch coming on deck: “Hansen, Schultz,
Williams, Smith, Farrell, Lindstrum, Drew.
McLean, Harris, Dobson.” He glances to-
wards the Mate (he is always the Mate on
a sailing-ship—only on steamboats does he
assume the more highly gilded title of Chief
Officer). “All here, sir.” he says.
The Mate stumps over to where the men
are herded together on the lee side of the
poop, and gazes fiercely at them. “Relieve
the wheel ’n’ lookout.” be roars. “See you
keep yourselves handy, ’n’ don’t be stowing
yourselves away in the Col-damned fo’c’s’le.
Watch below, be ready for a call, ’n’ don’t
be all the night turning out if you’re wanted,
unless you want my boot around you.”
The wateli below, snatching a chance
when tlie main deck is for a moment clear
ol water, dashes along to the fo’c’s’le, to turn
in with luck, till midnight, whilst a figure
swathed from head to foot in yellow oil-
skins goes over to relieve the wheel. Tie
steps up behind the man now steering, and
reaching over his shoulder takes the spokes
from him. for when running before a high
sen like this, the wheel must not be out of
the helmsman’s bands for a second.
“East a ’arf North, ’n’ mind yer weather
’cllum.”
“East a ’arf North it is.” and the new
man settles himself (Town to two hours of
ceaseless activity. ’A high canvas screen
runs round the poop rail, so that the helms-
man cannot look back and see the walls of
water rushing down towards -the ship. I
know of no sight more terrifying than this,
and when steering before a heavy sea there
is often a tendency on the part of the man
at the wheel to look back when lie hears a
big comber roaring along behind him. It is
beyond all doubt that many a good ship has
been lost through this. The helmsman has
looked round, lost his nerve, let go the wheel
in a panic, the ship lias broached to. and —
a small paragraph in an obscure part of the
daily papers, and “Missing” posted against
her name at Lloyd’s.
The Mate, who has the watch on deck
till midnight, takes up his position so that
he can keep an eye on the steering.
“Find her heavy?” he asks.
“She is a bit, sir,” answers the man.
“Carries a lot o’ weather ’ellum.”
The Mate blows his whistle and the
apprentice doing “binnacle duty” on the
lee side of the poop comes in answer.
“Boy to the lee wheel.” orders the Mate.
No words are ever wasted on a sailing ship;
and in a few moments one of the boys takes
his place on the lee grating, to give the
steersman a hand. Invaluable training this
for that boy if he keeps his eyes open.
The rest of the watch on deck, having
been told to keep handy, are standing by
under the break of the poop. Their oilskin
trousers are marled down with rope yarns
to keep the water out of their boots. Their
short oilskin coats are also tied round with
ropeyarns at the waist and wrists. These
ai - e known as “soul and body lashings,” and
to a certain extent they keep one dry, but
God help the man who goes overboard with
them on.
On a night like this, however, that would
be an advantage, rather than otherwise, as
if anyone went over the side to-night there
would be no earthly chance of assisting him.
No skipper would risk the almost certain
loss of a number of his men, and possibly
all hands, in order to rescue one man ; and
in any case, even were it practicable to
launch a boat, it would certainly not live
for five minutes. Therefore, if anyone does
go over the side, no attempt will be made
to rescue him. and the sooner his “soul and
body lashings” pull him down, the sooner
his sufferings will be over. Also it will
prevent the albatrosses from getting him
while he is yet alive.

By now it is blowing with almost hurri-
cane force, and even above the roar of the
wind the loud cracking of the tops 1 sheets
and halyards can be plainly heard. The
backstays are as taut as harpstrings, and
the tall steel masts are springing forward
like whips under the strain of the upper-
topsails. Only the fact that those same
tops’ls are made of new storm canvas has
prevented them from being blown out of the
bolt-ropes long ago. The Mate gazes
anxiously at the Old Man, who is still on
the poop. He knows that those topsails
ought to have been in before dark, but that
strange, cast-iron thing, sailing ship eti-
quette, forbids him suggesting such a thing
to the skipper. The skipper himself knows
they should be in, but he doesn’t want to
lose a ha’porth of this fair wind if he can
help it. The ship is now smoking along at
close on fifteen knots. Very little can be
seen of her, as her main deck, from the
break of the poop to the fo’c’s’le head, is full
of water from rail to rail. Huge seas loom
suddenly out of the darkness, and fall on
board with a thunderous crash, breaking
into a mass of white foam, against which,
for a moment, the masts, rigging, and other
parts of the ship show out jet black. It is
taking one’s life in one’s hands to go into
that inferno of water for one minute, yet if
we have to shorten down, all hands will be
working there half the night.
At six bells the skipper goes down to
have another look at the glass, and finds
that it has fallen another tenth. He comes
hurriedly on deck again, and remarks to
the Mate: “Think we’d better get them
there upper torps’ls off her, Mister. Call
all ’ands to shorten down.”
In less than the hundredth part of a
second the Mate is leaning over the break
of the poop, bawling at the top of his voice,
‘‘A ll—han(ls—sliorten — sail."
* ¥r * *
Inside the forecastle all is calm and
peaceful. At least, from a sailor’s point of
view it is. A mere shore person might
notice, and be annoyed by, the incessant
creaking and groaning of the ship as she
rolls, the splashing to and fro of a foot or
so of water that has found its way in
through the door, the rattling of forks,
knives, pannikins, hook-pots, tin plates, etc.,
in the lockers, the banging of something that
has fetched adrift in the fore-peak imme-
diately underneath, and the hundred and
one other noises. But the inmates notice
none of these things. They are sleeping
quite peacefully, with their blankets pulled
well over their faces. Some of them still
have chews of tobacco in their mouths, and
all of them have their pipes lying handy, to
be lit the moment they awake.
Hanging from the deck beams are two
slush lamps, which throw a very dim light
on the scene. The atmosphere is solid.
There are nine men sleeping here, and each
one has had a smoke before going to sleep.
The slush lamps are giving off the heavy
smoke and smell peculiar to colza oil, and to
this is added the reek of wet clothes, oil-
skins, tallow-smeared leather sea boots,
oakum, rope, oil, and other odors too numer-
ous to mention and too complicated to
analyse. Of ventilation there is none. There
is no skylight, and, owing to the weather,
no ports can be opened. The door has been
closed all day for the same reason, only
being opened for a few seconds when any-
one is entering or leaving.
Breaking in on this comes a booted
and oilskinned seaman, yelling raucously,
“Nah, then, show a leg, show a
leg, show a leg. All hands shorten sail.
Come on, what abaht it? Rise and shine,
rise and shine. Nah then, show a leg, show
a leg.” He lights, and takes a hasty draw at,
a very foul pipe, looks round finally to see
that everyone is awake, cautiously opens the
door, so as not to admit a sea, and dashes out
on deck again. Inside, the watch below,
cursing and grumbling in true sailor fashion
(for no sailor would find life bearable if he
could not grumble), are already nearly
dressed. In the fo’c’s’le of a wind-jammer they
don’t trouble about wearing silk pyjamas.
When the sailor turns in, he takes off and
hangs up his oilskins, places his sea-boots
carefully on top of his chest, in order that
they will remain dry should the fo’c’s’le be
flooded out, removes his outer garments, and
places them at the foot of his bunk, and, his
simple toilet being completed, he turns in.
Uncomfortable and dreary as the fo’c’s’le is,
it seems the acme of comfort when com-
pared with the deck. For a few moments
they stand huddled in a little group outside
the fo’c’s’le door, until their eyes grow accus-
tomed to the darkness, and then make their
way to the quarterdeck, where they are
joined by the bos’n, carpenter and sailmaker
(known as “idlers” on account of keeping no
watch, and consequently having "all night
in”), who have also been turned out to
assist. With the three mates and six
apprentices there are 26 men on deck, none
too many to handle the canvas of a big, four-
masted barque in a Cape Horn gale. The
Mate, who is in supreme charge of this job,
quickly apportions his forces.
“Starboard watch to mizzen tops’l! Port
watch to main tops’l! Look lively now, you
limping hoodlums, lay hold of those down-
hauls. Lower away tops’! halyards, Mister.
Haul on those downhauls now. Some of
ye tail on to the spilling lines. Put some
weight into it, you. slob-eyed Dutchmen.
Pull, Gol-damn ye, pull."
Slowly the heavy topsail yards come down.
So strong is the upward pressure of wind in
the great sails that they have to be dragged
down by main force.
A white mass of water rises up from over-
side. Out of his alert weather-eye the
Mate sees it coming. “ Hang on, everyone,"
he ye 115.... Crash! comes the sea on to the
deck, pouring over the men, tearing them
away from their holds, and sweeping them
into the scuppers, a confused mass of men and
ropes. Breathless, battered and half
drowned, they drpg themselves up to the
downhauls and spilling lines again, and re-
sume their pulling, up to their waists in icy-
cold water. Time after time they are
swept away into the scuppers, but at length
they get both topsail yards down on the
caps of the nnlsts, and the spilling lines
hauled as tight as possible. “Lay aloft and
furl ’em.” is the next order, and everyone
but the Mate makes his way aloft to the
swaying topsail yards.
I suppose that everyone at some time or
another has cliriibed a ladder, the ladder
being quite steady, and probably held in
place by someone. Consider, then, what it
would mean to climb that same ladder on a
dark, dirty night, whilst the person holding
it below swayed it' violently to and fro over
an arc of say, 60, degrees. If you can
imagine that you Will arrive at a very faint
conception of what going aloft in a gale of
wind means. Up,, up we go, with the reel-
ing mastheads swaying drunkenly above us.
Up to the futtoek rigging, where, while still
climbing upwards, we also have to climb
outwards, and where for a few perilous
seconds we are hanging back downwards,
still up, over the top, and into the topmast
rigging, until at length we come to the
yard, and the flapping, banging, canvas devil
we have come to subdue.
There is a sea proverb which says, “One
hand for yourself, and one for the
owners.” But this in most cases is
impracticable, as one usually requires
both hands to get a sail (especially
one of the large ones) in. So most ships
have fitted on the yards, ropes known as
“lifelines,” which pass along outside the
back of the men on the yard, and this
allows them to use both hands to the sail.
The men spread themselves along the yard,
and. when everyone is ready they reach up
with both hands and dig their fingers into
the vast wall of canvas, which is swaying
and oscillating like a captive balloon. For
some time no impression whatever is made
on it; then, little by little, inch by inch, we
gather it in. Then BANG!....a sudden
hard puff of wind has torn from our hold
everything we had gained. Never mind!
Try again.... Dig your fingers in, sons.
Never mind if your finger-nails are torn off.
This sail has got to come in some-
how ... .Now, altogether. Notv we’ve got
her... .Another hard puff....lt is slipping
out of our fingers again.... Hell! Hang
ou to it, boys.... Here she comes....
Inch by inch we gain it once more.
Now we are able to reach over the
top of it. A man sits down on the jumping
footrope, and throws a gasket up over the
fore part of the sail. After several attempts
it is caught, and then passed again....
Good. Slowly, but surely, the great thresh-
ing sail, struggling against captivity like
some huge wild animal, is conquered,
stowed, and securely lashed along the yard.
It has taken over an hour to do, but it had
to be done, so it was done. Whilst we
have been furling the main-tops’l, the Sec-
ond Mate’s watch has furled the mizzen,
and there now remains the fore-tops’l to
furl, and, the biggest job of all, the foresail
to reef.
The fore-tops’l does not take so long, as
we are able to get all hands on to it; then
once again down on deck to haul the fore-
sail up, preparatory to reefing it. A tick-
lish job this, in a heavy gale. The Mate
climbs up on to the deck house, where he
can watch both sides, and the Second and
Third Mates are ready to slack off the
sheets. The Mate’s practised eye takes all
in. . . .Everything ready ?... .“Slack away
handsomely on the sheets,” he roars ; “haul
away on your clew-garnets.” This is a big
sail, and takes some hauling up ; but gradu-
ally the clews creep up to clew-garnet
blocks, and bit by bit the buntlines and
leechlines draw the huge sail up to the yard.
Then the reef-tackles are manned, and
hauled out (seafaring men will remember
that it was the reef tackle that placed the
historic Dutchman in a very awkward posi-
tion). Once more all hands lay aloft, and
out on to the foreyard, to tackle the biggest
job of the night.'
Two hours later they struggle down to
the deck again, tired, half frozen with the
bitter cold and stinging, driving spray, many
of them with bleeding finger-tips, but filled
with satisfaction that the worst of the work
is over. The foresail is reset, though not
without some difficulty, and the Mate goes
to report progress to the Old Man. On the
faces of the men there now comes a very
peculiar expression. They all look aft as
though they were waiting for something.
Presently it comes. From the poop there
comes in the Mate’s stentorian voice the
welcome words, “Grog Oh.” Very eagerly
this time the dog-tired men make their way
on to the poop, and find the steward shelter-
ing in the cabin companion-way, with a
copper kettle filled with rum in one hand
and a pannikin in the other. As each man
comes forward he has a liberal tot of the
fiery spirit whacked out to him, which
courses like fire through his half-frozen
body, and puts new heart into him. The
skipper looks at the Mate. “That’ll be all
just now, Mister,” he says. “That’ll do the
watch,” shouts the Mate in his turn, and
the watch below make their way forward
to the fo’c’s’le, to put on dry clothes, if they
are lucky enough to have any, and to gener-
ally make the best of the short while there
is left, for it is now nearly four o’clock.
With the tops’ls off her, the ship is now
running much easier, and although still
shipping a lot of water, it is not nearly so
bad as before. Also we now have an oil-
bag hung from each cathead, which largely
minimises the power of the seas. Five
o’clock comes, that hour always beloved of
sailors the world over, as by old. old cus-
tom, that is the hour when piping hot coffee
is served to the watch on deck. And how
welcome it is on a morning like this, after
a night at sail drill, only those who have
been through it know. Six o’clock comes
round, and with it the first welcome beams
of daylight. A frightful sea is running.
Heavy black clouds race madly across the
slowly reddening sky. whilst gigantic
combers, with mile-long breaking crests,
roar away to the eastward on their eternal
journey round the world. But what do we
care now? We are making a fine, fair wind
of it, the ship is running well, and with any
luck we shall be round the Horn to-morrow.
Presently full daylight comes in, and five or
six miles to the northward of us we see the
grim, desolate Diego Ramirez Rocks, throw-
ing the spray a hundred feet into the air,
as they defy even the mighty Cape Horn
rollers to shift them from the foundations
the Lord made for them. Another eighty
miles, and we shall be abreast of Cape Stiff,
and then it won’t be long before we have
pur jibboom pointing north, with the girls
of East India Dock Road hauling in the
slack of the tow-line.
A. T. NORTON.
